“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal
to the price stated, books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address:—
Wickedness of Woman Books.
“ A SUPERFLUOUS WOMAN.” Cloth, 316 pp,; 3s. 9d.
This story has charm and power, though too obvious a purpose. The theme
is the struggle between the primal, physical, feminine side and the secondary, lady-
like, conventional side of a London society belle. Conventions hold her till she
is 20 or so, when she revolts, buries herself with Nature among Scotch peasants,
and falls in love with a simple, grand, serious boor. Sexual attraction impels her
to offer herself to him, but his Covenanting principles are so many wet blankets
on the flame of passion, and he makes the grand refusal. Disgusted with herself,
the lady returns to London conventional side a-top; marries a lordly roud; bears
two epileptic infants, who slaughter one another when old enough ; tries vainly to
reform her roue; and dies miserably. The peasant, meanwhile, has kept single
and simple, and grows grey in getting up early in the morning to gaze across
the misty heather and ponder what a Covenanting ass he was.
“ OUR MANIFOLD NATURE,” by Sarah Grand. Cloth, 271 pp.; 3s. 9d-
“THE HEAVENLY TWINS,” by Sarah Grand. Cloth, 679 pp.; 7s. 6d.
“ IDEALA,” by Sarah Grand. Cloth, 269 pp. ; 3s, 9d,
Sarah Grand is the accepted leader of the Revolting Woman, in her
crusade to win a New Jerusalem of Clean Men. “We have lost faith in
our men,” she says in “ Ideala.” “ Why should we keep ourselves pure
if impurity is to be our bedfellow?” And every one of her books has a
horrid example in the shape of a decayed rake who mates with a virtuous
maiden (with episcopal sanction and approval in “The Heavenly Twins”)
and fathers a precocious baby like the one in the Bab Ballads, who “ died an
enfeebled old dotard at five.” Now, against this crusade it is impossible for
unprejudiced persons to cast any stone of censure. Men like Clean Women ; why
shouldn’t women like Clean Men ? It is of no use for Angel Clare, after encoun-
tering a strange woman in London, to come back and tell Tess Durbeyfield, joyful
in her confidence that “ ’tis just the same,” that sexes alter cases. For they don’t,
at any rate from the standpoint of morality and justice. Let us, therefore, be
magnanimous, wish, the crusade luck, and concede that Sarah Grand has a right to
her Clean Man whenever she can catch him,
“ THE HEAVENLY TWINS ” is material for two novels awkwardly thrown
into One. It is clever and attractive, but Evadne is tiresome and should have been
omitted altogether. The “ Twins” themselves are a really sparkling pair, and would
almost justify superlatives if they were not weighted down by the necessity of
providing suitable morals for Unclean Men. It is true that, for a Clean Woman,
the author writes her copy-book headings with a queer fist. ■ But probably nine girls
out of ten would jump at the chance of repeating Angelica’s adventures with the
Tenor.
“ IDEALA ” moralises rather monotonously on the wrongs of woman, and, on
the whole?-the shock to Mrs. Grundy is not sufficient to counterbalance the dulness
from the average reader’s point of view; though social critics may find plenty material
for comment.
“ OUR MANIFOLD NATURE ” is a collection of short, readable stories of no
particular value, with the Clean Man again advertised for. The preface is the most
striking thing in the book.
“THE YELLOW ASTER,” by lota. Cloth, 317 pp.; 6s. 6d.
This is a smart and amusing book, which sets and solves a special sex-problem
on its own account, as already explained in The Bulletin columns.
“DODO,” by E. P. Benson. Cloth, 441 pp.; 6s. 6d.
“THE RUBICON,” by E. F. Benson. Paper, 394 pp.; 2s. 9d.
Mr. Benson is said to be nephew to the Archbishop of Canterbury, and pre-
sumably he represents the power of the Church as called to the help of the crusaders.
He cannot, however, help being biassed by his manhood, and has devoted the gi’eater
part of his space to limning the Unclean Woman, which is not fair. On the
whole, “ Dodo ” is about twice as good as “ The Rubicon,” and “ The Rubicon ”
is good for very little. “ Dodo,” whether she be Mrs. Asquith or not, is an excellent
lady not to meddle with. She talks smartly until she marries a lord whom she does
not care for, and when he dies in disgust and she has a chance to marry the man
she likes she takes a Russian prince instead. The lady of “ The Rubicon ”is merely
a “Dodo” who talks less smartly. All the same, “Dodo” is in her thirteenth
edition, and her sentimental flippancy throws considerable psychological light upon
the dark places of her devious sex.

u MEMOIRS OF SHERLOCK HOLMES,” by A. Conan Doyle. Paper, 296 pp .
2s. 9d.
“ADVENTURES OF SHERLOCK HOLMES,” by A. Conan Doyle. Paper
343 pp. ; 2s. 9d.
First-class detective stories, lacking Poe’s subtle literary flavour, lacking also
something of Gaboriau’s minute patience, but strong and fascinating.
“ NATIONAL LIFE AND CHARACTER,” by Charles H, Pearson. Cloth
381 pp. ; ss. 6d.
A cheaper reprint of a remarkably able book by a former Minister of Education
in Victoria, whose death was recently chronicled. It is a scientific forecast of the
future of human societies, based on ample knowledge and adorned by wide culture
and well deserves the attention of thinkers.
“THE WOMAN IN WHITE,” by Wilkie Collins. Paper, 240 pp.: 9d
■ Cloth, Is.
A cheap edition of the author’s masterpiece, well printed.
“IT IS NEVER TOO LATE TO MEND,” by Charles Reade. Paper, 9d.
Cloth, Is.
“THE CLOISTER AND THE HEARTH,” by Charles Reads. Paper, 9d.
Cloth, Is. '
Two of the best works of one of the foremost novelists of the age. The first has
special interest for Australian readers.
“ TESS of the D’URBERVILLES,” by Thomas Hardy. Paper, 520 pp.; 2s ; 9d.
This is a cheap re-issue of a book adjudged three years ago to be a masterpiece of
English fiction —a judgment which the lapse of time has strengthened and confirmed.
The Permanent Force.
These are books deserving, in Bacon’s phrase, “ to be, read and digested
books which every thoughtful man should own and study.
“ SOCIALISM,” by Graham. Cloth ; ss. 6d.
The best book extant on the subject.
“ THE STUDY OF SOCIOLOGY”,’ by Herbert Spencer. Cloth, ss. Gd.
The essence of the mind of the greatest living philosopher.
“ MAN AND WOMAN,” by Havelock Ellis. Cloth; 6s. 6d.
An up-to-date statement of sex-relations, brimful of information and interest.
“THE STORY OF AN AFRICAN FARM,” by Olive Schreiner. Cloth; 4g
A tale unique in power and fascination^
“ THE CRIMINAL,” by Havelock Ellis. Cloth, 3s. 9d.
The best popular monograph on the subject.
“POEMS, by WALT WHITMAN.” Cloth, 318 pp.; Is. 3d.
An admirable selection from the works of the Poet of Democracy, with a
valuable introduction by Ernest Rhys. “ Children of Adam ” is omitted" in defer-
ence to English prejudices, but all other sections are well represented. Just fits the
pocket.
With the Tail of an Eye.
It is said that the old magazines—Macmillan’s, Temple Bar, and so on— are
steadily going down hill. They cannot compete with the brilliantly-illustrated
Pall Mall and its kindred, which now vie artistically with the best American
work. -
Everybody who makes a hit in literature now sets to work at once to write
another book and catch the dollars flying. Beatrice Harraden, who wrote the strong
and suggestive “ Ships that Pass in the Night,” Mrs. Cafiyn, and half-a-score more,
are busy on second books, meant to catch the tide of popularity at the flood. Happy
those who, like the author of “Heavenly Twins,” can sell forgotten failures on-the
strength of new successes.
Stevenson has one new story finished—“ St. Ives,” and one nearly finished,
“The Lord Justice Clerk,” For the English serial rights of the latter he is said to
ask £2OOO. What isn't in a name?
George Meredith’s daughter was married, the other day, to an English squire.
Her father’s new story, “ Lord Ormont and Aminta,” which should reach Australia
next month, was paid for at the rate of £lO per 1000 words by the Pall Mall
Magazine. There is no such master of supple, sinuous English as Meredith, but
his books do not appeal to the multitude. Unhappy multitude !
No less than 60,000 copies of Zola’s “Lourdes ” were issued as a “first instal-
ment ”to the booksellers. When last heard of, Zola was on his way to see the
Pope, whom he hopes to exploit in his next novel, “ Rome.” A third, “ Paris,” will
complete the trilogy.
A bill is before the Canadian Parliament empowering anyone to reprint and
sell an English book if not printed in Canada within one month after publication.
Ten per cent, royalty is reserved to the author or owner. The idea is to help the
poor Canadian printer, for Canada, like Australia, is deluged with English books
which give not a cent’s worth of employment in the country.
NOTE,—As most of the hooks mentioned are in strong* demand, some may
he temporarily out of stock. In such case, orders will he filed, and the books
forwarded immediately on arrival of next shipment.

“THE BULLETIN."
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“Tlx© Bixlletln,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and di s
tinclive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility. *
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
arge fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow itS' lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered bjy the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
SPECIAL NOTICE.
Subscription (payable strictly in advance). Six
Sh illings and Sixpence per Quarter, or One Pound
per Annum.
Business communications to be addressed to

all remittances are payable. Editorial communi
ations to be addressed to J. F. Archibald.
Some fashionable London astrologers pre-
dict that the Yorklet, “if he gets safely through
middle age, will probably live to be old, but even
then he must look forward to physical inactivity
through corpulence.”
Sir George White, Madras commander-in-
chief, has ukased that, in future, R.C. soldiers are
not to be allowed to salute the “elevation of the
host.” And yet the army, at Alexandria, has
been in the habit of saluting the Holy Mahom-
medan Carpet during the annual procession.
Max O’Rell seems to have done at least
one good thing in his new book. He has pilloried
G. Republican Dibbs as the man who said one
year (at the Federal Convention) that the people
of Australia had such an instinct for freedom as
would force them, with very little provocation, to
declare themselves independent, and who the very
next year went to England and took a knight-
hood.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “ Cannalin ” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies.
Some 300,000 copies of a London illustrated
weekly were seized about six weeks ago in London
on the ground that two “spicy” illustrations
were placed in juxtaposition with an immoral
object. The seizure, however, took place five days
after the Australian shipment of the papers was
made. A very smart and pious trader “got the
office” by cable and bought up one province’s entire
shipment to arrive. With the object of suppres-
sing the circulation of such pernicious stuff? No!
He wants fid a copy for the Id publication and has
sold out, after most carefully explaining to his
most consistent customers what a disgusting thing
this particular publication was.

The (J(digraph Writing Machine,
fIL
ißf ~>=
■ •;
THE CaLIGRAPH embodies all the highest
developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that ths platen of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their type
out of perpendicular. Then remember that the
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every-
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
The Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

(FOR THE BULLETIN)
The ecstasy of anguish passion thrilled,
Before, behind, not now—for thee I hold.
Around me ripples all the silk-shot gold
Of restless hair. Thy pulsing lips are filled
With beating kisses, burning to be killed.
Thy supple arms of fire about me fold;
Upturn thy liquid eyes not overbold,
Rich as the sheen of sun-kissed waters stilled.
What if the future with our doom be rife 1
The brimful present leaves no space to sigh,
With consequence a dim, remote reply.
Thy smile is shadow after sun of strife,
And though, ’fore men, I may not name thee wife,
I, in thy arms, the aftermath defy.
Sydney.
W.J.

The five ages of man are : 1. The ancient
stone age. 2. The newer stone age. 3. The age
of bronze. 4. The age of iron. 6. The brass or
Talm-age.
A N.S.W. country editor candidly con-
fesses to a correspondent who pointed out various
errors in syntax and orthography in a previous
issue : “ J.S. : Thank you ; you are correct. We
consulted a local grammarian and be confirmed
your criticism. The grammarian has been re-
cruited and will remain a permanent member of
the staff. We’ll want a new editor soon; so if
you can you had better come along yourself.”
D. A. Yindin, stamp-merchant, late of
Sydney, now of London, has joined Phillip
Mennell in the British-Australasian, and will
in future attend to the business management of
the paper, which no one interested in the doings
of the Australasian world should be without.
The British-Australasian’s list of Anglo-Aus-
tralians living in or visiting Europe, published
every week, is a unique institution.
“ I won’t buy reapers and binders for you
—I shan’t get billets for your children —I know
nothing about buying seeds or perambulators.
The Government has no money to waste on roads
and bridges or schools. I’m not a local man but a
busy one, and I want to be your representative
and not your Melbourne agent.” Thus quoth
G. D. Meudell on first meeting the electors of
Barwon at Queenscliff. This food must have been
too strong for the barracouta people, because Meu-
dell retired from the contest next day.
In a certain Australian Works Department
A was in charge of a district as Road Superinten-
dent. B was sent up as Bridge Inspector. As
there were no bridges in the district to inspect, B
was kept pottering about doing odd jobs so long as
anything could be found for him to do. Then A
sent him to the capital with a letter stating the
facts in the most considerate terms. Soon after A
was removed to another district, then removed
again, and finally retrenched. As he bore an
unblemished character, and had influential friends,
his case was brought before the Minister. The
latter’s reply was ; “To save all further trouble, I
may as well tell you that I will never give that
man employment of any kind, or allow anyone
else to do so if I can prevent it.” A’s only fault
appears to have been that he was not clever enough
to divine the wishes of the head of the department,
and become accessory to a waste of public money
on an unnecessary official protege.

According to cable, ex-Premier Stam-
bouloff, of Bulgaria, has been stoned by the
populace of Sofia, and narrowly escaped with his
life, owing to a suspicion that he had used ‘‘in-
sulting language ” towards Prince Ferdinand.
Stambouloff was the Bismarck of Bulgaria—the
man who covered the country with roads and
railways, who stood off the Russians, Turks and
Servians, who broke up Panitza’s conspiracy by
going alone and unarmed at night to the house of
the ringleader and hauling him away to prison
with his own hand, and who has all along held
the little wild-cat kingdom together against
enormous odds. And Ferdinand is a butterfly
potentate with 150 pairs of gaudy pants and a
taste for botany, and with more embroidered
shirts than any other monarch in Europe.
Monarchy is a great institution.

A DISPUTED CLAIM.
PARKES: “ I say, you’ve been and jumped my claim!”
REID: “ Well, where’s your pegs ? And. besides, you haven’t fulfilled the labor-conditions.”
PARKES : “Well, you just give me a hold of that there haxe, and I’ll jolly soon cut some pegs. And, as to labor-conditions, you ought to know me well enough to
know that I never fulfil any conditions of any sort. But this here smy claim, and I mean to nave it!"

By sending direct to Thb Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
•which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director

Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
•Correspondence ” column, all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 22, 1894.
The Queensland Coercion Bill.
Up to date the shearers’ insurrection in the
Western districts of Queensland has defied
all the efforts made by the Brisbane Govern-
ment towards its suppression. These efforts,
it is true, have been so abjectly feeble that
it seems more than doubtful if the Govern-
ment ever had any real desire for its prompt
extinction. It is just within the bounds of
probability that it was considered better, in
the interests of the aggregate Fat Man of
Queensland, to let the disorder run its course
for a while, so that it might serve as an
advertisement of the lawlessness of Unionism
and the incapacity of Labour in general for
self-government, and help to divert public
sympathy from the waxing Democratic cause
at next general election. And, having allowed
the trouble thus to develops in the first
place, it naturally followed that it was advis-
able, in the second place, to accentuate the
magnitude of the crisis by taking extra-
ordinary measures of repression - such mea-
sures as would serve to impress the com-
munity with the idea that the utmost
resources of the existing law had been tried
and found insufficient. Therefore, the pro-
ceedings of the Government, in the first
p ace, were limited to the despatch of 250
police to keep order throughout a district
large enough to make a respectable empire,
as empires go in Europe. The handful
of men despatched to the West were a mere
fly-speck on the great wastes and solitudes,
and they failed to produce any serious effect,
not so much from want of numbers as from
the absence of all means of rapid concen-
tration. If the authorities did not de-
liberately aim at this result they, at all
events, took the best and most effective way
to arrive at it.
As a natural consequence of this policy,
the insurrection grew apace, and when the
panic had spread over a vast extent of
country the Nelson Government rose to the
occasion it had itself created, and introduced
a sensational measure entitled he Peace
Preservation Bill. The leading features of
this proposed law are that, in certain
“ disturbed districts ” the carrying of arms
shall be made illegal, and that any persons
“suspected” of violence, or conspiracy, or
of any felonious intentions towards Pro-
perty, may be put in gaol on a magistrate’s
order, for three mouths, without evidence,
without trial, and without even any specific
charge being laid against them. The bill
contains a number of other features, but
these are its most notable characteristics, and
are sufficient in themselves to cover every
emergency. Between them they provide
that the person who is found, armed may be
sentenced to a long terra in gaol for carrying
unlawful weapons, the carrying of weapons
being a crime in itself and also a sufficient
evidence of the intent to commit other
crimes ; while the person who is found un-
armed may be put in gaol for three months
on suspicion, without even being informed
of the nature of the suspicion, or the grounds
for it, or by whom it is entertained. The
Peace Preservation Act, in short, is a copy
of the various Coercion Acts which have been
applied, at various times in the last thirty
years, to Ireland, with certain obnoxious
details of comparatively small importance
thrown in,
# # •
Just here it is advisable to explain that
The Bulletin does not object, in the
slightest degree, to the Queensland coercion
law, so far as the interests of the Unionist
shearers are concerned. To all intents
and purposes the law already posses-
ses all the powers which it professes
to acquire, consequently it makes their
case neither better nor worse. There-
fore, even if this paper sympathised with
the worst excesses of the insurrectionists it
would still take but little interest, so far as
they are directly involved, in the new
departure of the Nelson Government. But
the object of the new law is not to put down
the uprising in the West ; its own utter
futility is sufficient evidence of that. Its
obvious purpose is to impress upon the com-
munity that the working classes of Queens-
land are a desperate race who can only be
prevented from wrecking the constitution by
a process akin to martial law ; and to scare
the urban population, which has so far failed
to appreciate the alleged magnitude of the
crisis, with the idea that an overwhelming
danger has been with difficulty averted
through the promptness and vigour of the
Boodlewraith Cabinet. If the existing law—
which is quite as effective arid practically as
stringent as the new one—had been enforced,
the desired sensation would have been lost.
But the furious debates on the foolish and
impotent Peace Preservation Bill, the
suspension by the Speaker of half the
Labor members, and the dramatic retire-
ment of the Opposition from the House of
Assembly, have all helped to create a vague and
hazy and utterly unreasoning idea that the
Cabinet has risen in some unexplained man-
ner to some kind of an occasion which is not
very accurately defined. On the strength of
the alleged danger which is supposed to have
been thus averted, the present Ministry may
secure another term of office at next elec-
tion, and come back to do more land and
railway jobs, and lend more public funds to
theparty’s meat-freezing factories, and to make
further advances of State loan moneys to the
Q.N. Bank. The Coercion Act, regarded as
a means ot suppressing the aggressive
unionist, is a dismal superfluity, but, as an ad-
vertisement for the party of Lawnorder,
it may possibly turn out to be a great
success.
• * •
A very brief glance at the situation in
western Queensland is sufficient evidence of
these assertions. Up to the present date,
every person arrested and tried for taking
part in outrages in the disturbed districts
has been convicted. There has not been the
slightest difficulty about getting the unionist
shearer into gaol when captured. The great
trouble has been the practical impossibility
of capturing him —a difficulty which arises
wholly through the vast extent of the
territory over which the trouble is spread
and the miserable weakness of the Govern-
ment forces. The Coercion Bill will make
the Western wilderness no less vast and
inaccessible, nor will it make the Govern-
ment forces any stronger. When some out-
rage is committed 100 miles from the nearest
police camp, the chances of overtaking the
offenders will not be improved in the least,
and when ten policemen endeavour to stand
off 100 angry bushmen in the dark the
Coercion Act, or any number of Coercion
Acts, will make the odds not one whit less
overwhelming. Provided the Government
forces are sufficient to enforce the law, there
is already abundant law to enforce. When a
gang of shearers attack some pastoralist’s
property, it is quite competent to arrest the
entire crowd, and get a conviction against
them—which can be done with the greatest
ease, and send them to gaol for three years
or more, for riot, incendiarism, assault with
intent to commit grievous bodily harm,
wounding with intent, unlawful assemblage,
conspiracy, breach of the peace, and various
other offences. If this prospect does not
serve to restrain them in any way, the
futility of attempting to overawe the rioters
by the threat of three months’ imprisonment
without trial is too obvious to require
description. As for the clause in the Coer-
cion Act which provides for disarming the
disaffected parties, they are already dis-
armed, without any extraordinary legal pro-
visions, whenever the police succeed in
arresting them. When the police are unable
to arrest them then they are not disarmed,
and so long as the offender has to be caught
before the law can be applied no amount of
law will alter the situation to any great
extent.
• • «
This is the position as regards the unionist
who is captured while actually committing a
deed of violence. As to his position at other
times the Vagrancy Act gives almost as
extensive powers as are provided under
the new law. According to its provisions,
every unionist in any of the camps in
Western Queensland can be arrested and
held for months as a suspected person, as a
person of evil repute, as an idle and dis-
orderly individual, as a person frequenting a
public place under reasonable suspicion of
an intent to commit crime, as a person who
refuses to “move on” when ordered to
do so by the police, as a person having no
lawful visible means of support —hardly one
unemployed shearer in ten cannot be brought
in under this latter definition alone, and as
various other things. The Western district
is full of magistrates and J.’sP. who are
ready to rush every unionist shearer in the
country through the form of a trial, and
sentence him to six months’ imprisonment
on almost any one of these charges —always
provided the police can capture the objec-
tionable person and bring him before them.
But so long as they are unable to capture
him, the fact that the magistrate is authorised
to send him to gaol for three months without
trial is of no earthly account, so far as the
intended prisoner is concerned. And if the
magistrate were authorised to send him to
prison without trial for three centuries, it
would be equally ineffective under the same
circumstances.
* * #
The provision by which almost any person
in a “disturbed district” may be arrested
on the sole responsibility of an ignorant or
officious policeman, and imprisoned for 13
weeks without even a formal accusation,
and without evidence, is a gratuitous and
insolent outrage on the spirit of liberty, and,
in itself, fully justified the fierce protests of
the Queensland Opposition. Under this
utterly needless enactment, any individual
who is personally obnoxious to the Ministry,
or to their influential supporters, or to a
magistrate or J.P. or policeman, can easily,
by direct or indirect means, be shuffled into
a gaol, and left there for three months with-
out prospect of redress. Also, there is no
assurance that, when he comes out of prison,
the process may not be repeated. But the
ostensible purpose of the Peace Preservation
Bill is to suppress the riotous shearers
of the West, and there is such super-
abundant provision for getting them into
gaol under the ordinary law that, so far as
they are concerned, this extraordinary
measure can only be set down as an ingenious
attempt to create a panic which may react
against the Democratic cause. And if it is
not intended for that purpose, it only serves
to stamp Premier Nelson as the most com-
prehensive and far-reaching imbecile among
all the Governments of Australia.
“Ghosts:” and Some Reflections.
Dear Bulletin, —The splqudid poem “Ghosts,”
reprinted by you in the issue before last, was
wrongly attributed to “A. R. Rogers in the
Contemporary,” instead of to A. R. Ropes in
the National Observer. What a magnificent
rose-cut diamond of poetry it is ! One of the
jewels which should sparkle, as Tennyson
says, “ on the stretched forefinger of all time.”
I cannot now- call to mind any modern poem of
equal length, or rather equal brevity, wherein
a rounded and complete story is told with such
simple words of thrilling force. Something
like it, in softer sound and calmer perfec-
tion, is Tennyson’s own exquisite “Cross-
ing the Bar.” It is even more brief,
for although the number of lines is
the same they are shorter lines, and in count of
words the late King of English poetry got
ahead by 12, “ Crossing-the Bar ” comprising
102 words and “ Ghpsts ” 114.
» • *
But I think there would be little difference
of opinion among those who really do know
poetry when they meet it, regarding the respec-
tive merits of the two poems. I mean the men
and women who cannot be misled into reading
much of Sir Edwin Arnold or any of Lewis
Morris ; the men and women who feel an elec-
tric thrill start from the nape of the neck and
finish in the left heel when they come upon
such lines as these of Kipling’s :
“The sins that ye do by two and two ye must pay for one
by one,
And the Qod that ye took from a printed book be with
you, Tomlinson I”
or those of Swinburne’s—
“ At the Door of Life, by the Gate of Breath,
There are worse things waiting for men than Death.”
who can recognise instantly that William
Watson’s “Domine Quo Yadis ?” is of a
separate species from anything parented By
mere talent. They will have no hesitation in
placing “ Ghosts ” higher, as a single perform-
ance of clear, absolute genius than “ Crossing
the Bar.” Technically, it is almost flawless.
Mr. Ropes (what a name for a strong smiter of
harp-strings !) rhymes “ her ” to “ were,” while
there are no faulty rhymes in Tennyson’s
much-admired lines, but in uniformity of
metrical construction he scores heavily against
the dead laureate. His words are almost
equally melodious and simple, while the central
thought they serve is in this presentation new
and intensely tragic. “Ghosts ” is “ like a man’s
laughter heard in hell far down.” It is a terrible
authentic cry of the damned. The woman lost
in this syndicated world of God and the devil
who drowns memory in drink, and the broken
man who hides from all that used to be, shriek
out so when the ghosts look in and make them
think. This is a dreadful lightning-flash of
poetry; “Crossing the Bar” but a strong sunset
gleam: an idealisation of thought common to all
Sunday schools. Nearly every right-thinking
person expects to go to Heaven when he dies”
as well as most murderers. ' Very few would
express it as “hoping to meet their Pilot face
to face when they have crossed the bar ”
though; none who had maritime experience
enough to know that just then the pilot himself
“ turns again home,” which is the opposite
direction. But poets don’t rjuow everything
and, on the whole, Tennyson’s is a noble poem*
Still, the obscure Ropes’ is greater.
* * •
On the subject generally. The poetry of the
future has been by several writers of
late. In Cosmos, published the other day, Mr.
Arthur Carton closes with Mr. William
Sharp about this and, it seems to me, draws
blood. Sharp thinks the world is hungering
for a return to the old ballad form, and will
not much longer tolerate sonnets. G Alton
doesn’t, but considers that the sonnet has had
its day. Now, I think the sonnet and the
ballad must go—also Galton. Anyone who
starts in to write the records of our past should
dip his pen in rum and post himself in the
criminal slang of several generations, instead of
using the ink - horn of the past to indite
Addisonian phrases. This, by the way,
although it is important. The reading
world of the future will have no time for
expansive yarns of the ballad sort. “Chevy
Chase,” and all narrations of nothing in par-
ticular, illuminated by lengthy descriptions of
the scenery, will be of no more use to the
coming man than the “Faery Queene ” or
“ Paradise Lost.” Neither will he care for
sonnets, in which so much of the best poetic
thought has been spoiled by a tyranny of con-
struction almost as fatal to it as the mechanical
needs of the acrostic.
* • •
The poetry of the future will be in any form
that comes handy, so long as it is short,
and means something which is apparent and
striking. “Do it with your big toe, if you
like, but do it,” was the advice of Turner to a
pupil who wanted advice about methods in
painting. Kipling comes nearest, of the new
poets, to doing “just as he darn likes” about
form and style, but he does what he starts out
to do, nearly every time. Most of them are
commendably sparing of words, though none
have told a life’s story such as is conveyed in
“Ghosts” in words so few and so potent.
That js, I think, a model for universal imita-
tion. Here is the current language of to-
day put to its best use ; here is the very
bovril of expressive speech. There is no
groping in the dust-bin of the past for guidance
—no resorting to traditions and trickeries. The
poets of the future will have to write so. In
the past some of them have scrawled their copy-
on the backs of letters. But for readers who
will scan by electric-light all they will require
of poetry printed by flying linotype machines
the coming poets had better cultivate the habit
of writing on the business side of cablegram-
forms, so as to keep well in mind the value of
words.
WALTER CLAY.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Repudiation.
Under the Maoriland State Land Bank scheme,
in its latest form, the province proposes to
borrow £1,500,000 per annum at from 3J to 4
per cent., and lend it out at 5 per cent, on
landed property. The amount lent is never to
exceed two-thirds of the value in case of free-
hold and one-half the value in case of leasehold
property —which is certainly a sufficiently nar-
row margin ; but as the borrower pays one per
cent, per annum off the principal the margin
will be constantly increasing, and under these
conditions the chance of loss will be infini-
tesimal. By a process of compound interest
this repayment of the principal at the rate of
£1 in every ,£lOO will, in 36£ years, wipe out
the entire debt, so that the borrower will pay 6
per cent, for 36J years and then be free of both
principal and interest, instead of, as at present,
paying seven, eight, or nine per cent, in interest
alone, without shifting the original burden one
iota. The process of borrowing and lending is
to go on so long as there is one individual
left in the province who wants to raise
money, and who has sufficient security to offer,
lu other words, it is to go on until the private
money-lender is practically extinct, and the
State is the only mortgagee in Maoriland.
And when this consummation is arrived at,
as the Tory press is now busy pointing out,
almost everybody in the province will be mort-
gaged to the State, and being himself a part
of the State he will be practically mortgaged
to himself. Therefore, when the aggregate
borrower, in his private capacity, falls
arrears with the interest, he can vote himself
an extension of time in his political capacity ;
and when he finds the interest oppressive he
can vote that it be reduced ; and when he
doesn’t pay up at all he can vote that his
property shall not be seized in satisfaction ot
the mortgage ; and, finally., he can vote that it
is unnecessary to pay either principal or in-
terest; and then the country must either “go
broke ” informally or else formally repudiate i s
liabilities. All of which things, no doubt, may
come to pass—in fact, there is no visible reason
why they should not come to pass ; but when
they are advanced as proofs that tire Lan
Bank project is iniquitous and foredoomed o
failure, and as reasons why the private capi-
talist should get his seven, or eight, or nine
per cent, as heretofore, the logic is not veiy
apparent. The one thing that is beyond a
question, in any case, is that—under ndu
suffrage —if the majority of the natio
wants to repudiate the public debt it wi
do it, whether the debt was incurred 0
the purposes of the Land Bank, or for any ot ie
purposes. Also, if the majority wants


repudiate its private debts, it will do that also.
In fact, if a solid majority, in a country that is
run on the universal suffrage principle, wants
to do any iniquity whatever, it will do it, or
attempt it at all events. Some day or other,
all the Australasian provinces may repudiate
their outside liabilities, and confiscate all the
British capital in the country ; there is no
evidence, and never can be any evidence, that
they won’t. But the establishment of the State
Land Bank won’t bring the catastrophe—if
there is to be a catastrophe—any nearer.
Judging by present appearances, it is quite
within the bounds of possibility that the
ultimate destiny of Australia is to go broke, as
half the nations of Europe and America have
done at one time or another, and pay its awful
creditor, J. Bull, a dividend of one Fink in
the £• But j ust nov,r the present and the
immediate future are sufficiently serious sub-
jects, and there is no time to consider what lies
beyond that.
The British Sabbath.
It has been definitely resolved, by the aggregate
foolishness of Parliament, that the New South
Wales “Sabbath” is to (legally) continue in
all the beauty of its howling desolation for a
little while longer. Under the old Sabbatarian
statutes which the country inherited from
Britain, it is unlawful to do almost any kind of
work on the alleged day of rest. There is a
penalty for selling tram-tickets, or ginger-ale,
or biscuits on that day, or for driving a vehicle,
or for supplying tea and coffee to the thirsty
traveller. It is even open to question if the
bishop’s magnificent flunkey isn’t liable to be
seized on his own apostolic coach-box for
driving his master to church on the Sunday,
and the tramway-conductor, and the man who
runs a ferry-boat, and the person who sells a
Sunday paper, and the compositor who sets
up the Monday morning’s issue of the
moral Herald, and Editor Curnow, of
that paper and his staff, and the whole gifted
management of the Daily Telegraph, are all
liable to he arrested and imprisoned under the
grand old Sabbath-observance law. If the said
law were honestly enforced all round, existence
in New South Wales would become utterly in-
tolerable ; it is only rendered tolerable because
a corrupt judicial system winks at all the in-
fluential law-breakers, and only captures some
miserable Chinaman now and then for watering
his cabbages on the Day of Gloom. Still, it
would appear, strangely enough, that public
opinion is in favor of the present anomalous
system. The other day Willis, M.L.A.,
introduced a Bill to provide that the various
things which are now allowed to be done unlaw-
fully on the Sunday should henceforth be done
lawfully instead, and, to the surprise of every
logical individual in the community, it was
rejected by 69 votes against 20. Its chief
opponents were Reid and Copeland, who de-
nounced the measure as an offence against
Heaven, and Parkes, who pointed out that it
was an offence against John Bull, and a sinful
outrage upon the principles of the “ British
Sabbath.” Plainly, the only way to get
rid of the Sabbatarian law is to enforce it all
round. If the venerable statute was set in
motion against everybody who now treats it
with contempt it would not exist a month ; the
only reason for its continuance is that every
“respectable” or influential citizen is per-
mitted to break it with impunity, and, being
so permitted himself, he naturally doesn’t care
a straw what happens to some stray Chinaman
or Seventh Day Adventist or other common
malefactor.
The Registration, Clause.
Amid the mazes of the new Land Bill scheme
evolved by New South Wales Lands Minister
Carruthers, there is one provision which is an
undoubted step in the right direction. The
clause in question sets forth that every selector
■or conditional purchaser shall, by registering
Ibis homestead with the Registrar-General, be
protected against his creditors to the extent of
£2OO. In other words, if a creditor sells him
up, he must pay £2OO into the Supreme Court,
so that the debtor will at least have the
means of establishing a new home for him-
self and his family, and will not be al-
together an outcast. Several years ago, W.
H. Traill introduced a measure which arrived
at the same result by a less circuitous
route, but he received scant consideration from
the Foreign-trade press and party, and his
measure came to nothing. But though the idea,
founded on the U.S. homestead law, is borrowed
from a political opponent, it is none the less
■commendable, and the only regretable feature
is that the Reid Government has not the full
courage of its opinions, and only carries its
great reform half-way. Having once acknow-
ledged that it is undesirable to allow any
creditor to seize the home of his debtor and
eject him on the world—undesirable for many
reasons, but mainly because the process burdens
the community with paupers and outcasts—the
Land Bill should protect the residence of every
agriculturist, and a certain area of land, suffi-
cient for the support of himself and his family,
from seizure on account of any debts contracted
after the measure becomes law, without regis-
tration or any other formality whatever. If it
is for the general good of the nation —as is un-
doubtedly the case—that such protection should
be afforded, it should be afforded to all.
Half the agricultural population—the more
stupid half, and consequently the half which
most needs protection—will take years to
learn the cumbrous formalities necessary
for its own protection; and, in any case,
the registration process will only have the
merit of affording work for attorneys and land-
agents, in drawing up foolish documents, and
Ming them, and charging exorbitant fees for so
domg. The law recognises that it is desirable
to protect every citizen from fraud or brutal
assault, and, therefore, if he has been defrauded,
°r has had his eye blackened by an inebriated
stranger in the street, it protects him, whether
he is registered or not. And, in similar fashion,
it should protect the selector from being made
homeless whether he is registered or not. More-
over, instead of compelling the ejecting creditor
0 pay a certain amount as compensation for the
*ost home, it should make the home itself in-
violate, and thus avoid the whole elaborate
proceeding of making deposits with the
Supreme Court and other expensive legal pro-
cesses. Also, the same measure of protection
should be extended to every home, whether in
town or country, and should cover, not only the
house itself, but the furniture and clothing con-
tained therein. And it should do all this by
the same automatic process which prevents the
debtor from being seized in person and sold in
satisfaction of his unpaid hills. The registra-
tion process is merely another evidence that a
Legislature consisting largely of lawyers
always builds new statutes with a view to
bringing more and still more grist to its own
legal mill.
A Subsidised Arcadia.
The wheel of the “ simple, beautiful, Arcadian
system ” of Pitt Town (N.S.W.) Labor Settle-
ment is round again to the old notch, and the
Sointer stands once more at the signal “ Give!”
■ut there is a good deal of reason why Parlia-
ment should disregard the appeal. This Settle-
ment was started on the understanding that
Government should provide ,£25 for each
married man with a family ; £2O for a married
man without a family ; and £l5 for an unmar-
ried person. Nearly 100 men, towing nearly
100 wives and nearly 300 children, turned up
promptly to settle Arcadia on these terms, and
have been cheerfully ruralising on eight hours
a day and Government tucker ever since, with
marked results on the provincial birth-rate.
Many of the original Arcadians have left the
job, but there are always hundreds ready
to supply their places, seeing that a
good hut and good rations in return for
labor while you are looking round for
something better are things by no means to
be despised in these dull times. After about
six months of country residence the Settlement
had exhausted its subsidy, and appealed for
more on the ground that no one would wish to
spoil such a beautiful ship for the sake of a
miserable penn’orth of tar. At that time the
cash return for an expenditure of nearly £2400
was just £l4, but, of course, the Settlement
was not in working order. Twelve months
have now passed, £4600 has been expended,
and the cash return is estimated at £5O, which,
however, cannot be guaranteed as correct, since
it is not considered judicious to furnish the
public with a balance-sheet. The ship is still
spoiling, and the cry is now for more tar. So
the subsidy is to be trebled, which means
an outlay of £75 for every married man
with a family, and at the Settlement
nearly everybody is a married man with a
family. With this new grant, the Settlement
will have drawn nearly £7OOO of cold cash from
a benevolent Government, and the day when it
will be self-supporting is still far, far in the
distance. Yet in three years’ time the settlers
will have to commence to repay liabilities at the
rate of about £560 a year. Repudiation is
certain. Then the Government may foreclose
if it likes on the 100 huts and the fencing and
the dams and the crops, which are all excellent
things for the purposes of Arcadia, and would
not fetch at auction 25 per cent, of the money
invested. Meanwhile, the settlers outside the
Arcadian boundary—men who have strained and
sweated to make a living with no backing but
their own energy and industry—are wondering
where is the justice of a scheme which taxes
them to feed the people on the other side of the
fence; who, thanks to Government pap, can
afford to work eight hours a day to their ten,
and have a Saturday half-holiday into the
bargain. The Pitt Town Settlement and its
romantic ideals will flourish just as long as the
Government subsidy continues. When that
stops, down it goes like a house of cards.
Night and Day.
Editor Bulletin, —J. Medway Day in your
last issue makes two unjustified statements in
his letter on “Bimetallism.” He says Aus-
tralia is “ within a conceivable distance of the
time when payment of interest on and prin-
cipal of ” her debt to Britain “ will be a physical
impossibility,” except upon the assumption
“that the mass of workers will be content to
toil longer hours and live upon a bare sub-
sistence, leaving the bulk of their earnings to be
mopped up by the usurer.” Now, if the debt be
not further increased, a continuance of the pre-
sent rate of growth of wealth and population
for 50 years will make the existing burden im-
perceptible, or release us from it altogether. It
is quite possible to argue that the money bor-
rowed by Australia has on the whole been well
expended ; and a comparison of accrued wealth
with accrued debt does not countenance Mr.
Day’s assumption so far as present liability is
concerned. The point lies here. Money borrowed
from foreign countries to develop natural
resources is a lever without which often we
could not raise the wealth we seek for. It
pays us to pay for the use of the lever. But,
as the burden of interest increases the fulcrum
gradually shifts up, until by-and-by we shall
be trying to work with the short end of the
lever. The introduction of borrowed capital is
attended with benefit and followed by danger.
So far, I incline to think that in the case of
Australia the benefit has outweighed the risk.
In the future, the risk and loss will outweigh
the benefit. Mr. Day also says that “in paying
interest, we are paying something for which
we get absolutely nothing in exchange.” 1 fear
the sophistry of theorists like Flurscheim
has blinded Mr. Day’s common sense. Among
both individuals and nations, the exaction and
payment of interest are practically universal.
Does Mr. Day think that the average individual
or the average nation is such a fool as to give
something for nothing? They pay interest
because it is generally recognised as not just
that a man should reap where he has not sowed
or gather where he has not strawed ; and
because they cannot get the benefit of other
people’s accumulated fruit of energy or self-
denial without paying interest. If I do with-
out ale and tobacco till I save £5, and lend that
£5 to Mr. Day for a year to spend on ale
and tobacco, I shall not be satisfied with
receiving £5 back at the end of the year.
I must have something for the risk
incurred. For in that year I may die,
or Mr. Day may become unable to repay
me, and then I lose all. Or if I lend a cow to
Mr. Day for a year it will not do to return me
only a similar cow at the year’s end. For in
the interval Mr. Day has profited by the milk
and may also have acquired a calf, which he
keeps—why ? I am a year older; I have had
no milk for a year; that year’s milk cannot
possibly be made up to me; and Mr. Day
profits directly by the difference between the
cost of keeping the cow and the value of milk
and calf. Why should he profit by my loss ?
Yours, Not Much.
&n Insult to the Bench.
The Speight v. Syme libel-case, in the fifth
month of its second hearing, has been made
newly and strangely interesting by Judge
Williams, who enjoys the unenviable privilege
of putting the merits of that cumbersome law-
suit into the proverbial nut-shell. Before start-
ing to sum-up the reasons why Richard
Speight should, or should not, obtain a verdict
against David Syme the learned Judge gave
vent to his feelings upon the subject of plaintiff’s
counsel. Mr. Duffy, the barrister in question,
had been guilty, so the Judge affirmed, of cast-
ing moral mud on the highest branch of his
own profession. He had said to the 12 men in
the box : “It would never do, gentlemen, to
have a case of this kind tried before a J udge
without a jury. It would be cruel to have this
case—a case between a private individual like
Mr. Speight and a powerful newspaper like the
Age —tried by a Judge alone. The Judge might
have been mixed up in politics. He might be
seeking for further honor or promotion.”
Judge Williams protested against the sug-
gestion that a man of his official position
could be tempted to betray his trust. His pro-
test was natural and very excusable under the
circumstances. He took the opportunity to let
the world know that his personal faith in his
own integrity was strong, which faith, as far
as The Bulletin knows, is well founded. There
are, nevertheless, Judges unfitted for tasks
which a Justice Williams is able to perform
with vast credit to himself and to the Law.
The fact that one swallow doesn’t make a
summer is not more incontrovertible than the
evidence that one Williams is merely an indi-
vidual person. Why speak of the highest
branch of the profession as though its height
elevated the character of every man who sits
upon it ? But the irritated gentleman did not
stop here. After blowing a blast from the
judicial trumpet he went on to “ prove ” that
Speight’s counsel was suppressing truth and
suggesting falsehood in reference to this matter
of having the case tried by a jury. Judge
Williams took upon himself to declare that
Speight had endeavoured to get his case
considered by a Judge alone, inasmuch as
he had at the outset filed this notice in
the Prothonotary’s office : “ Plaintiff desires
to have the issues herein tried without
a jury.” Judge Williams further represented
that Syme had defeated the intentions of
Speight by immediately filing an application
“ to have the issues herein tried by a jury of
12.” It is true that defendant, Syme, asked
for the jury, but Justice Williams had no
right to assume that Speight was really
desirous of obtaining any different form of trial.
Mr. Speight’s advisers merely seized their
opportunity to thrust the expense of a special
jury upon defendant, Syme. Assuming that
Syme, the proprietor of the Age, must wish to
put his case before a tribunal that would pro-
bably include several men who read his paper
and were influenced by it, the plaintiff’s wide-
awake lawyers took advantage of an unfair
legal rule. They filed their application, not
with the idea of avoiding a jury, but in order
to avoid the jury cost that now falls solely
upon the other side. By their wily pro-
fessional proceeding they obtained a legal
laugh as well as a jury of 12 at Syme’s
expense! “ If it had not been for the action
of the defendant this case would have been
tried before a Judge alone,” cried Justice
Williams. But the indignant luminary didn’t
Eoint out the important fact that plaintiff
peight’s application was neither more nor
less than a legal trick. Each party wanted a
trial before 12 juryman, and one party by com-
pelling the other to ask for it compelled him
to meet all the expense appertaining thereto.
Thus it will be seen that Speight’s counsel was
not necessarily guilty of suppressing truth, or
suggesting falsehood, when he said, “ It would
be cruel to have this case tried before a J udge
alone.” The fact of Speight applying to have
the case so tried does not prove that he
intended his application to succeed in other
than a financial way. As for the great ques-
tion, “ Ought a Judge to sit without a jury in
cases of the Speight v. Syme character ?” it can
be best answered by the lawyers and laymen
who, after listening to Justice Williams for
the past five months, can now draw conclusions
from his unbiassed summing-up.

Of the 16 candidates returned without opposi-
tion to the new Vic. Assembly four were
members of the Munro-Shiels Government.
Also, four of them— Peacock, H. R. Williams,
Isaacs, and H. Foster —have spoken strongly,
or mildly, in favor of a tax on unimproved
land values. Ten are against Patterson, and
six approve of that exploded person. But it
is a fact that an unopposed return is, generally
speaking, not so much an indication of the
feeling of the electors as a proof that the con-
stituency covers a lot of ground and is a good
deal at the mercy of the well-to-do man who
has sat for it before. Three of the sparsely-
populated divisions of Gippsland, one of which
is “farmed” by the delectable F. C. Mason,
Chairman of Committees, were won again with-
out a contest. Ten of the other walks-over
occurred in rugged districts where possession
means nine points of the seat. Also Frank
Madden, the despised of all thinking people,
had a walk-over for the large ramshackle
Eastern suburbs electorate, merely be-
cause there was no liberal-minded candidate
prepared to risk the expense of the canvas
necessary to give him a chance of winning the
seat. Frank Madden’s case is thoroughly dis-
heartening for those who believe that a reduc-
tion ia the number of M.’sP. would tend to
cleaner legislation in Victoria. The fewer the
constituencies the greater the number of eyes
fixed upon each individual member, it is true.
But the larger the electorate the easier for Fat
Man, or Fat Man’s nominee, to get in, and,
having got in, to stop in.
Daily paper item :
Messrs. John Morgan and Company, of Tenterfield,
N.S.W., lately issued coupons and off red a prize to the
person who made the nearest guess as to how long a
monster candle would burn. The police authorities have
now stopped the affair, considering it illegal.
N.S. Wales has now become so pious and so
police-ridden that a citizen cannot even oiler a
prize for a harmless amusement without some
lumbering, unlettered policeman ordering
him, on his own sole authority, to stop
and “get out av that.” But in Sydney,
under the same code of laws, the tote-man
not only runs his illegal gamble openly,
and lights up his premises at night so that his
name and the full designation of his game may
appear conspicuously on the plate-glass window,
but he puts up a big lamp—also with his name
on it—on the edge of the pavement to draw
further attention, and advertises his address
and all the particulars of his trade in the
papers. And the police-force, which drops so
violently on the poor little candle-competition,
rarely sees him. The Bulletin hereby recom-
mends the Tenterfield man to get a new candle,
and start the show afresh, and tell the local
myrmidon, when next he interferes, to go to
bed. The candle-competition ought to be a
tolerably legal affair. It is a game mostly of
skill, not of chance. All the conditions being
known, the time for which a candle will burn
can be estimated to a minute or so.
The woman-missionary to China is thus referred
to by Mr. St. George R. Littledale, who
contributes to the Geographical Society’s
Journal for June a paper on “A Journey
across Central Asia
In this town (Kwei-hwa-Cheng, on the Hoang-ho, a few
score miles west of Pekin) there were a colony of Swedish
girls living alone. They had been sent out to China
through the instrumentality of an American. These poor
ladies had been exposed to many insults on their way up
the country, and to daily ribald remarks in the streets at
Kwei-hwa-cheng, and one shudders to think what may be
their fate. It is difficult to speak temperately of a society
or individual that, in the name of religion, lightly takes
the responsibility of sending these women out wholesale
to a country like China, and when there, leaves them
practically without supervision or protection, and with
most inadequate means. It will scarcely be credited that,
though they usually go about in pairs, a_ girl hardly
speaking a word of the language will sometimes be sent
alone with a Chinaman, several days’ journey in a cart,
to visit some town where there is no European. Al-
together, I don’t think Mrs. Littukdalk or 1 have felt more
sad than we did on the day we lett those enthusiastic,
open-hearted Swedes.
Yet here in Australia are subscriptions invited
to perpetuate this revolting practice; and,
worse still, girls have been induced by the
parsons to trust their lives and their virtue to
the lascivious Celestial. They may seek to save
the Chinese soul, but (as shown by American
social experience) the almond-eyed Mongolian
will give more consideration to the bodies than
to the words of his plump teachers.
After all, there is a chance of a tax on un-
improved land-values coming from the nev\
Victorian Assembly. Many candidates who
waited to get their cues from their earlier meet-
ings had discovered a necessity for a land-tax
before the end of last week, and most of these
shifty gentlemen should get seats. Then it will
rest with their respective constituencies to
force them to reiterate their fair promises and
pin their wriggling minds down to the new
national policy. As for Protection in Victoria,
it can be left to take care of itself. The first
serious attempt to slaughter an established
trade with a reduction of duties will result in
such a demonstration as Melbourne hasn’t
known since the Berry days. And seeing that
Melbourne badly needs a burst of political
excitement to stir its blood, a Freetrade attack
upon the tariff would prove a real public bless-
ing. The Bulletin would like to see the
return of every Freetrader who has been
frightened into advocating any kind of new
land-taxation. As anti-Proteetionists they will
not get a show to do any harm, whilst their
alleged desire for a fair tax on the soil-owners
can be forced into practical legislation.
Dear Bulletin, —At his meeting in Essendon
Town Hall, Deakin urged the claims of Federa-
tion to the van of the Liberal cause, and I had
an opportunity of proving your allegation that
Australians are indifferent to union. The
crowd standing in the rear of the hall grew
listless and dull when Federation came on
the carpet. When Deakin got to taxes and
local works the people woke up and cheered.
My idea is to grapple with this indifference by
Ministerial intervention in the matter of taxes
on interprovincial trade. A Customs Union
between S. Australia and Queensland, for
instance, would be easily accomplished, and the
fact of the union would set all Australia think-
ing. “ The door would be opened to the thin
end of the wedge.” The tariffs of the provinces
named are identical in a hundred places owing
to the common popularity of the 25 per cent,
impost. The thing is to commence where
identity exists, and not let inconsistencies of
policy block all the path. The following articles
now bear the same duty in each province:—
Ale, bricks, baskets, biscuits, spirits, boots,
furniture, hats, harness, ironwork (most),
jewellery, &c., &c. What is to prevent the
two provinces from standing in together
on these things against the outside world?
The thing is desirable not only for its own sake,
but for the sake of the Federation it would
help. At the present moment the fiscal ques-
tion, as a whole, is a mountain of difficulty.
Here is an instance of the stupendous variance
that exists —the item is “ Posts and Rails ” :
Vic.—Free.
Fiji and N.S.W.—Is. 6d. per 100 ft. sup.
Queensland —3s. per 100 ft. sup.
S.A. and W.A.—lO per cent. ad. val.
Tasmania- \ over 3 " ls ‘ 6d ' P er 100ffc - BU P-
Tasmania—-j Un(Jer 3 „ 6d per loou> sup-
M.L.—Posts, Bs. per 100. Kails, 4s. per 100.
How can any corporation hope to unify in one
acta tariff possessing tangles like this? The
motto might be given : “ Union where identity
exists, and consideration for the conflicting
issues that remain.”—Fluffy.

Ths Wild-Cat Column.
N.S. Wales is borrowing, in London, £832,000
at 8i per cent, to pay off certain old 6 per cent, deben-
tures falling due shortly. The Bulletin respectfully
draws Premier and Treasurer Reid’s attention to the fact
that he could probably have got the amount in N.S. Wales
at the same rate if he had had courage enough to ask for
it. All the banks have immense cash balances just now.
So have all the big life-insurance offices—three of these
have no less than £750,000 of coin among them. The
reason is that they are afraid to put it in securities which
can’t be quickly realised in the event of a sudden demand,
and simply because almost all the Australian Government
Stocks are in London there are hardly any safe and easily
realisable securities in this country to invest in. If a
regular local market were established so that Government
securities became readily saleable, there are several insti-
tutions which would gladly put a million in 8i per cent,
stocks and thus earn £35,000 a year in interest rather than
keep the amount lying idle and profitless. But from sheer
force of habit Reid looks on J. Bull as the only available
financial resource, and goes to him, as usual. There is no
beastly originality about Q. H. Reid.

N.S.W. Assembly has affirmed the advisability
of establishing a State Bank. The Government, most of
the daily press, and the Upper House are hostile, how-
ever, so the resolution means very little in the way of real
progress.
• • • •
The Maoriland Insurance Bill has been laid
before Parliament, and proves to be a widely different
affair from what was anticipated. Its most conspicuous
feature is that it absolutely prohibits any foreign fire
insurance co. with limited liability doing business in
Maoriland, and uncompromisingly bounces the English
offices which make profits in the province, and ship them
away to piy divs. to English shareholders. This is a
great and praiseworthy idea, and The Bulletin trusts
that Australia will presently do likewise.
* • • •
The amalgamation of the B.N.Z. and the
Colonial Bank of N.Z. has been finally arranged, subject
to the formal consent of the shareholders. The B.N.Z. is
to give £400,000 worth of paid-up shares to the proprietary
of the Colonial and take over all its assets and liabilities.
Also, £300,000 more of the B.N.Z.’s capital is to be written
off to cover losses, leaving only £600,000 paid up, so that
the combined institution will start with a paid capital of
£1,000,000. This makes £900,000 of capital and over
£600,000 of reserve-fund which has been written off in six
ruinous years ; of the £1,636,000 which formed the bank’s
capital and reserves in 1887 there is now but £lOO,OOO
remaining! The first thing which the new institution
proposes to do is to pay off the disastrous per cent,
debentures which have weighed on the B.N.Z. ever since
*B9, Then a steady effort is to be made to sell off the
deid-weight of landed-property and wind up the B.N.Z.
Estates Co., and when that is done the combined institu-
tion will begin to know where it stands. It is already
admitted that the estates, despite all the past writing-off,
won’t bring their book value, so a certain amount of
“ surplus profits ” is to be set aside to meet losses in the
liquidation. Probably the Government may take over the
biggest part of the landed incumbrances at a reasonably
high valuation. But at their best the properties don’t
pay more than 3 per cent, on their book value, so even
the most friendly Government could hardly take them
over, save at a considerable reduction. And if the Govern-
ment doesn’t take them, the combined and reconstructed
institution will have a pretty hard row to hoe.

N.S.W. Mont de Pidt4 shares keep steadily
advancing. This joint-stock loan office does business,
both in its pawnbroking and bill-discounting depart-
ments, at less than half the rates usually charged in the
same line of business, and, but for the slowness of the
public to learn about anything new, it would long since
have either annexed the entire pawnbroking business of
Sydney, or else have forced its rivals to reduce their
unspeakably extortionate charges to its own reasonable
level. The co.’s only office is at 74 Castlereagh-street,
Sydney, though a number of other institutions, mostly
run on the old 80 or 100 per cent, basis, use the title in
various parts of the city.
• • • »
The Australasian Rights Purchase people are
übilant at the result of the Hillgrove water-power
scheme. The shareholders in both companies are almost
identical. The success of the Hillgrove move has already
more than trebled the value of the Colo shares ; and the
promoters intend to fight tooth-and-nail for the spoil. If
they succeed, they will be in a far better position than if
they owned Londonderry and Mount Morgan rolled into
one.
The generation of electricity by water-power
must indeed be a good thing. According to a report
issued by Prof. Threlfall, adviser to the Hillgrove and
Armidale Co., the expense for contracts will come to a
little more than £15,000, whilst the annual net revenue
derived from the works as per signed agreements would
amount to £6815, equal to 45 per cent, on cash invested.
If such colossal results can be shown from a small affair
like the Hillgrove Co., what revenue would be derived
from the Colo, which is so much nearer Sydney, and has a
thousand times as much horse-power and hundreds of times
as much constituency? No wonder the Australasian Rights
Purchase crowd are straining every nerve to get the Bill
through.
If N.S.W. Parliament passes that Bill it will be
a national crime. The concession is actually worth mil-
lions. Why should the State give for nothing so noble a
property to a far-reaching array of ravening grabsters,
who will probably—the odds are 40 billions to I—simply1—simply
turn over their charter in the London market ? Why
shouldn’t the State be its own middleman? The con-
cession is as marketable an article as 24-carat gold at £4
an oz.
••• • •
The Colo grabsters are the moat representative
crowd ever gathered together under the skull and cross-
bones in Australasia. They come from every province—
Tasmania, Maoriland, Victoria, Queensland, N.S. Wales.
If they pouch their glittering prize, just imagine, if
you can, what their position will be 1 Tramways are even
now being run by electricity—and as the Colo Valley
crowd will be the only people who will be able to supply
electricity in Sydney at competitive rates, they will prac-
tically, if not absolutely, own the trams, for which a strong
contingent of their number has been angling for years.
If not allowed to light the city themselves (city-lighting
is, of course, the chief direct aim and object of this
colossal reach-out), they can levy huge toll on any system
of municipal or other lighting. If Sydney wants to
electrocute a criminal, the Colo Valley grab will
be able to quote bed-rock rates. If anyone wants to
drive a printing-press, or a lift, the Colo Valley brigade
will be able to oblige—at a price. If you want to grill
chops, the Colo Valley grab’s electrical stove will be on a
better footing than the gas-monopolist’s stove. The gas
clock has struck 12. The electrical clock’s hands point to
12.1, and a big section of M.’sP. are winding it up with
the Colo Valley grab key.
• * • •
The Daily Post, Sydney’s projected Demo-
cratic newspaper, is a shining example of the virtues of
co-operative management. The authorised capital was
£20,000 in 10s. shares. Power has been taken to double
the capital. The plant of the defunct evening Echo was
bought, and to complete the payment an advance was
obtained, for which security had to be given. Then type
and etceteras were bought on credit, and security again
given. The capital is coming in slowly, and current
expenses make a big hole in it as it arrives. The first copy
of the paper is still far in the distance. No mere capitalist
would blunder so badly.

First the newspapers stated that one D. Tweedie
had purchased the Londonderry mine; after that Col.
North was the buyer. It was expected that “Roths-
child ” would have turned up, but somehow he didn’t.
Before he had a chance one Lord Fingall appeared on the
soene. There can be no doubt about Fingall’s purchase.
He has evidently come along, with a substantial deposit,
perhaps £50,000. The mining gamblers of Australia
should be ashamed of themselves for the miss. Apart
from any consideration as to what the future of the
Londonderry may be, the great quantity of phenomenal
specimens and the three rich faces in the mine, will float
it in London for double the purchase-money. Fingall
will make a pot of cash out of the deal. If not an ex-
perienced man in the business he has picked it up very
smartly. The writer can imagine the sensation his
£30,000 worth of specimens will create in London, and
with his 'mind's eye he can see them trying to lift “ Big
Ben." It is time the Australian well-to-do mining-men
started to kick themselves. And Wilson was there too I
• • # •
What has become of the Wealth of Nations? or,
rather, what has become of the wealth of the Wealth of
Nations? The Coolgardie newspaper correspondents
should toe the mark. They made the public believe that it
was infinitely bigger than the Londonderry; they made
Alex. Forrest declare that it was worth a million of
money ; that the reef was yellow with gold, and the
moment the least attempt was made to minimise their
statements they came forward with the assertion that the
Wealth of Nations was as rich as ever. During the last
fortnight they have lapsed into silence, and silence is
always suspicious at Coolgardie.

Cabled that only slightly above a quarter of the

gardie, were subscribed. This is not so puzzling as the

• * * •
If well mined and managed, the Mt. Drysdale
mine, N.S.W., might make a name for itself, but it needs
more work and less newspaper yab-yab. The latest is an
account of a visit of some shareholders and directors and
a statement of the manager that h« could raise 100 tons
which would go lOOozs. to the ton, and another that
shares are not obtainable at any price. Now, 100 tons at
lOOozs. is 10,000ozs. or £40,000 worth, and the better
plan would be to raise it first and gas about it afterwards.
That is what a coolheaded man would do if he were
working gold, no matter how rich, in country rock—with
no defined lode or walls. Gold-bearing country like this
is most hideously treacherous, and the shareholders
should be told that the two biggest fools in gold-minihg
are the man who buys shares at any price and the man
who won’t sell at any price.

come along with a big permanent N.S.W. lOdwt. reef and

The return of Mills’ Day Dawn United for
August, 5297 tons for 66400 z., remarks Alan Bright’s
circular, is the greatest ever obtained in the period and
the greatest in Australia from a depth of between 1200
and 1600 feet vertical. There are three cleanings-up in
the month, and the normal out-put of the mine may now
be regarded as, say, 4000 tons for 43000 z. The usual
monthly div. of £7500 was paid, but as the gold was
