* * * *
The London underwriters of the Great Boulder.
Co. were very sore at having to take so many shares. Out
of 175,000 the public only subscribed for a little over 5000,
which shows which way the wind blew. Every second
man one meets in the city (remarks The Bulletin’s cor-
respondent) has a Coolgardie mine for sale. This will
retard the long-hoped-for boom, and do a lot of injury to
bona-fide properties which may be put upon the market.
♦ * * *
Rumoured that extraordinarily rich ore has
been struck in the deepest level of the Aladdin mine,
Lucknow, and that it is being bagged and stacked for
shipping to England. The Wentworth mines being
developed with English capital and owned by British
shareholders the managers “don’t feel called upon to
give local publicity to their returns." As a consequence,
Australia is quite in the dark as to what Lucknow is
turning out. Surely the State should take steps to see
that the N.S.W. public are kept regularly informed as to
what their country is stuffed with.
• ♦ • •
The tons stone sent by the Young O’Brien
g.m., Grenfell, N.S.W., to Clyde Works for test, gave
2oz. 3dwts. per ton. Six tons more stone and three tons
blanketings are being sent as a further test. Mine has
now 200 tons at grass. In the new shaft there has been
met an entirely different class of quartz, chiefly pure
white, bearing free pebbly gold. The Young O’Brien
crowd have 7Ja. on line of reef and can take up 10a. more
If necessary. There are 16 shareholders in this mine,
including the Pitch family and George Anderson. Old
man Fitch and Anderson are two of the best and oldest
miners on Grenfell.
• • * *
The Broken Hill Proprietary mine will shortly
have at work a concentrating plant capable of treating
1100 tons of ore weekly.
• • * ♦
The comparatively small bulk of the tin output
of the world, and the universal use of tinned ware, makes
it peculiarly open to the machinations of the market-
operator. It is announced that a corner in tin is con-
templated. Cableman announced a corner in gold a few
months ago, and it didn’t arrive. A tin corner is perhaps
feasible, but the figures quoted in connection with the
present move are not big enough to seem a guarantee of
success. United Kingdom tin imports, Jan. to June, ’94:
—Straits, 12,921 tons; Australian, 2370; other, 1630.
Tin ore produced in the U.K., ’9313,689 tons, valued
at £687,053. Price of tin, July 21Straits, £66 10s.;
Australian, £67.

The very latest lie is that a Westralian pro-
spector has crushed J of a ton of gold for Idwt. of stone.
* • • *
The old Lucky Hit mine, Tuena (N.S.W.), now
called the Amos, has cropped up again with some rich
specimens of gold in caloite, and the public are told by
Sydney newspapers of the finding of gold in calcite at
1300 ft. at Broken Hill, and of the possibility of an iron
lode at Tuena running into calcite. After all this the
writer predicts a prospectus.
• • • *
Bodalla (N.S.W.) reefs have reached the stage
here anticipated months ago—they have been proved too
poor to pay under the most economical working. The
“ music of 1000 stampers ” fortunately never started.
The scheme looked well on paper, but several mining men
have bitten themselves and a few of their friends over the
Bodalla bluffs. The old moral: Never estimate thevalu ;
of a body of stone by crushing a few tons, and then never
put it through any other machine than that intended for
general use. Better lose a dwt. to the ton by battery
than save it by a slow and otherwise costly grinding
process.

84C> Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
September 18, 1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The A. J.O. Spring meeting began last Saturday
(D.erby Day) with a well-filled members’ stand
an'd a goodish-sizcd crowd in the V.R. stall,
which did not include Lady Hopetoun—much to
the disappointment of the hordes of people anxious
to re-assure themselves as to the state of her Vic-
torian Excellency’s piquant profile. Lady Duff
(she tied the Derby ribbon too high up on Bonnie
Scotland after the Derby, and his owner hadn’t
the grace to let it throttle the good steed until he
got him round the corner, but altered it while
within vice-regal eye-shot) wore the cheerfullest
gown in the V.R. camp: bright pink silk with
intervals of black lace and a floral bonnet; but the
other women in her party presented a very lack-
lustre appearance, like people seen through a
brown gossamer veil. Lord Hopetoun, in a black
overcoat with a velvet collar, had broken away for
once from his everlasting grey race-suit, and
looked as pale and worn-out as a popular actor
(which is only another name for a popular gover-
nor when you come to think of it), and Gov.
Duff’s concession to the turflness of the occasion
was a horse-shoe in a black scarf.
What strikes the onlooker in re the Governor’s-
box nowadays is that not a hat goes up in passing
it, as if salutes were prohibited. There were nods
and becks and wreathed smiles in the days of
other dynasties, but people now parade past the
vice-regal roosting-place as if the cane furniture
were unoccupied. And, talking of bowing, it is
evidently the caper for Governors not to uncover
to pen-ladies of their acquaintance. I don’t know
whether the fashion has been set by a secretly-bald
Governor who doesn’t want the press to know too
much ; but I saw an indefatigable newspaper lady
bail up Gov. Duff and pump-handle him by the
limp right hand, but neither in meeting nor part-
ing did His Ex. bare his poll!
Speaking of vice-regal little ways, it strikes me
that our very best local examples let out a reef or
two in their manners when they begin to forget
the wrought-iron etiquette of their native land.
For instance, it sounds most untrammelled to readl
about the Governor of So-and-so’s single daughter
travelling all the way from one capital to another
accompanied by a single aide-de-camp ! The day
of the duenna must be long past when such things
don’t even provoke a ripple on the social surface.
Not that there is much fear of the Governor's single
daughter running away with papas tame “aide.”
Strangely enough, though “suites ” marry and are
given in marriage all over Australia, they never
seem to attract or be attracted by vice-regal
maidens. Probably both see too much of each
other under various trying domestic circumstances.
But to return to my text. There is a superstition
that the Spring Meeting of the A.J.C. reveals to
the public eye what sort of clothing will rage dur-
ing the half-and-half season; but, as I have grown
quite accustomed to pointing out, in the bright
lexicon of Potts Point there is no such word as
Spring. Society flops out of furs into flimsies as
if sudden summer came with the green peas, and
then we have the hideous spectacle of furry wraps
over muslin skirts, or else red noses over tulle
bodices. Neither do the girls who are airily clad
and yet keep ungoosefleshed force the hot season
upon us to any practical extent. We know under
these circumstances that the muslin is only an
airy top-dressing over snug merino undergear.
All sorts of garments flopped and fluttered in
the searching southerly breeze. One stout society
lady (who amused the whole stand by allowing
her admirer to tie her shoe, in the eye of the mul-
titude) finished off her toilette with a pair of mus-
lin sleeves that filled with icy wind like captive
balloons. Midas’s wife, in mauve crinkled cr£pon
barred with ribbon, held airy chat with a Gover-
nor, while Mr. Midas took the children in to
afternoon-tea as meekly as if womanhood suffrage
were already in good working order. By the way,
it is rather hard on a stylish matron who looks
about 20 in a hat to have a file of children, some of
whom are getting to be big girls now, everlastingly
haunting her footsteps on every racecourse. It s
bad enough (from a butterfly point of view) to
have a grown-up daughter to chaperone, bub to
have the whole family spread out like a fan is
enough to make the spnghtliest matron take to
grey curls and bombazine in despair.
Mrs. “ Uncle ’’ Hutton, in pumpkin green, with
a pink vest veiled in black chiffon, and black lace
hat en suite, was seasonably, though not very
becomingly, turned-out. Mayoress Manning wore
g earl-grey silk, the skirt lined with pink. It is a
'renchy fashion of the moment to have your
skirt lined with whatever your contrasting color
may be. I say Frenchy, because a girl who is
anxious to display a “ harmony ” underneath
sometimes forgets that only the ballet is allowed
to go about with a double reef in its petticoats.
Miss M'Donagh was seasonably got up in seal-
brown, with velvet points and bands of Oriental
embroidery. Miss Nellie Foster (England)
sported a tailor-made suit of grey cheviot, with »
collar-and-tie—indeed, a lot of people shot their

tarched habit-front and a neck-tie to an other-
wise flighty turn-out. Linen and lace 1 I verily
helieve Providence makes some people with no
edges to their teeth.
A, Victorienne, in pink floral stuff, with spinach-
en trappings, walked very well, and stood out
■ consequence, for it seems to me as if we were turn-
} n j n to a race of turkey-tramplers. Another
Melbourne girl, in the “devil’s colors,” black
nd red, came escorted, as usual, by Melbourne’s
«eau Brumrael. The members of “Morocco
Bound ” turned up in force, and the tonnage and
tilt of Wilfred Shine’s cigar gave color to the
flottering rumor that he had been enlisted under
jV banner of W; and M. The Criterion was
represented by Mr. Matthews and Percy Brough, in
r | U inp-Jim-Crow frock-coat and a red boutonniere
to take the ginger out of his hair, which had
apparently been cut with a basin; but men were
no loking matter on Derby Day—the departure
of the warships made them almost as scarce as
tnosQUi toes* t
The calico and fancy-dress ball in aid of the
Boys’ Brigade, in Sydney Town Hall, the other
Wednesday, must be regarded as a “drawn
blank.” There were so many screws loose, for
one thing. The people who ought to have gone,
and did, were not enough to compensate for the
people who ought to have gone but didn’t; then
most of the warships had rushed away, thinning off
the male interest, as dramatists say ; and the floor
was like a ploughed field. The music was certainly
u p to English high-baton mark in that waltzes
were whirlwinds and lancers sprinting-matchea;
but Australians were never born to dance at the
rate of a mile a minute, so the band was grumbled
at even when it played the barn-dance. The
supper was really the strong point of the show, for
the hall-decorations weren’t lavishly expressed.
The pillars were bandaged like gouty legs, for
one thing.
For a change his Excellency attended without
Lady Duff (it is generally in reverse order !), who
was chained to her room_ with a severe cold. The
lieutenant-governoress, in black with diamond
necklace, adorned the dais instead, for Mrs.
Edward Hutton, in a melting costume of pale-
creen and heliotrope etherealised with white chiffon
and cream lace, didn’t arrive until late. The
Admiraless, in what are known as “old maid’s
colours” (grey and pink), went about with her
train in her hand as if the committee had forgotten
to broom the flooring-boards, but the plain dressers
got very little attention.
Those “ ladies and gentlemen ” who had been
naively requested per circular to don fancy cloth-
ing “ to amuse the spectators in the gallery ! ” (as
if society went to public balls just for the glorious
privilege of keeping the two-shilling seats on the
qni vive!) took all my available attention. The
best fancy form was displayed by the “Nancy
Lee” set, in which a scurry of damsels from what
they call the “ Upper Ten ” in Morocco got them-
selves up in abbreviated white skirts, pale-blue
collars and jack-tar caps with varied success.
What I mean is that one poker-backed damsel in
spectacles didn’t look quite the sort of girl who
would stand waving her hand upon the Quay,
and another evidently hadn’t the foggiest notion
of how thick her ankles looked with her calico
skirts at half-mast. And, talking of big ankle-
joints reminds me that the prettiest family of
Sydney girls (some have married well) ought
never to go abroad with bare arms. I sat near
them in the theatre a little while ago, and from
admiring their beautiful faces I fell to picking
their bones, so to speak. Their clumsy elbow-
points were as much out of proportion to their
delicate limbs as a bullock’s-joints would be on a
baa-lamb.-
But I digresifSd from the ball just as I was
about to tell you of the plump society card-
sets led by plump ladies as the queens. Evi-
dently these festive sovereigns had been chosen by
weight, like Xmas turksys. But the kings most
deeply interested me. What must be the mental
stature of a youth who would consent to put on a
long glazed pink calico gaberdine topped off cere-
brally with a tin crown and go gambolling round a
public ball-room as the King of Hearts ?
The card-dresses were not particularly ’cute.
They consisted largely of baggy gowns of
calico in various primal colors with the “pips”
of their particular suite dotted about. The
“ clubs ” set in yellow and black, with a
club in their hair, looked smart in a Japanesey
way, but it must have been very dry work for the
“ caramon cards ” of the suites who danced ladies’
lancers—a pack of cards without a joker ! There
were other fancy sets, “ Powder and Patches ” and
“The Seasons,” but the uninitiated onlooker
would never have known them from evening
dresses, “Winter,” in arctic sheeting and powdered
glass, excepted. One or two unknowns cropped
np in outlandish attire. There was a “ bee ” with
tinsel wings who had to stretch her short skirts
over her knees every time she sat down, and I
certainly think one large old lady, in a wig and a
glaring garment, ought to have stopped at home
and frightened her own family.
Apropos of the card-sets, it seemed to me that
there were two queens to each suite 1 Certainly
card queens are two-headed, but the realistic
organisers evidently overlooked the fact that
when one sovereign was on her feet the other one
ought to have been standing on her cranium 1
Last week was “ studio week ” in Sydney, when
all the local artists threw open their workshops
arid entertained invited guests with views of their
lovely frames and little cups of tea. Every studio
was thronged with “complimentary” and com-
plimenting visitors, but bitter experience has
taught their several hosts and hostesses that when
the tea’s in the truth’s out, and the spectacle of
the most ardent admirer (in the studio) of
00-and-So’s chef d'oeuvre openly flouting and
jeering at its pretensions (when the gallery opens)
must make the artistic circles of Sydney hope-
lessly cynical. Yet the fault doesn’t altogether
he with the visitors. It is only a person possessed
of 40 h.p, moral backbone who can tell Miss Crim-
son Lake to her face that the neutral-tinted land-
scape looks like an overworked blotting-pad.
I must begin the list of music-notices with a
note of admiration against
Maude FitzStubbs’s emotional setting of Mu 8
Proctor’s words in her new song, “ A Broken
Promise ” (mezzo-soprano range). I heard Mrs.
F. J. Riley sing it with strong effect, the other
morning, to Miss FitzStubbs’s accompaniment,
and anyone who has enough voice to cough with
cannot fail to make points with such a tuneful
and sympathetic air, set to so graceful and sup-
porting an accompaniment. I have also received
from Messrs. W. H. Paling the “ Annette
Gavotte,” by Signor Lardelli —a sprightly
measure, though somewhat too careful in the
general effect. The “Annette Gavotte” makes
the same impression upon you as a good little girl
with a clean nose. It is just a trifle too demure.
From that prolific publisher, Mr. H. J. Samuells,
comes the “Nitouche Grand Galop de Bravoure,”
by Ldon Caron. It is a rattling good bit which
goes full pace as a galop should, and was played
with great success at the Lyceum during the run
of the musical comedy. And last of all, there is
yet another “Song of Young Australia,” com-
posed (this time) by M. Amati, and dedicated to
the native-born. It is a weak and flickering com-
position. When the real Australian war-cry
comes along it won’t end on a long, low note like
a growl, as if Young Australia were getting ready
to bite a bit off a book-canvasser.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “Henry V.”
Lyceum “Morocco Bound.”
Criterion “ The Private Secretary,”
Theatre Royal “Struck Oil.”
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s Theatre “ Sowing the Wind.”
Theatre Royal “ Unjustly Sentenced.”
The “Private Secretary” made its re-
appearance at Sydney Criterion amid as much
enthusiasm as attended its first visit in the days
of long ago. Judging from the business done up
to date, this insult to the Established Church is
good for a month’s run anyhow, if the manage-
ment can see its way to keep it going so long. A
few changes—more or less important—are notice-
able as compared with the original production. A
door which used to have its hinges on the left side
now has them on the right instead, so that where
the Rev. Spalding formerly introduced himself by
putting his apologetic head round the door
he now puts it round the door-post. When his
Uncle Cattermole drags him round the room in
search of a hiding-place he now rushes over a sofa,
whereas he used to charge up the back of an arm-
chair and bound over the top. There is a
slight falling-away in Uncle Cattermole, where
Harwood’s old billet is occupied by E. W.
Thomas. The new uncle lacks the impressive
dimensions of the old one, and looks needlessly
ferocious in his make-up. On the other hand,
Gibson, tho tailor, has decidedly improved, and
the Squire, who is now done by Fabert, is better
than he ever was before. The improvement in
Gibson (represented by F. W. Leonard) is largely
owing to the fact that the present tailor makes a
picturesque and beautiful “drunk,” and many
experienced inebriates speak of him in the highest
terms. Thornton, as tile crushed and wormy
curate, has not changed by so much as a hair
since his first appearance in Sydney. The ladies
of the co., especially Mrs. Walter Hill and little
Miss Dot Frederics, are sufficiently good, and
there is no serious cause of complaint among any
of the other characters.
* * m *
Just now, in Sydney, the old shows seem
to pay a lot better than the new ones. “ Struck
Oil,” so far, is filling the Royal to the door, and
the audience laughs at the doings of Lizzie Stofel
as joyously as if it had never seen or heard of that
Dutch maiden before. One Hebraic - looking
youth in the circle, on Monday night, laughed so
violently that he was seized with cramp after the
second act, and for five minutes or so his silent
contortions were dreadful. Then he recovered
and proceeded to laugh some more. Maggie
Moore has apparently been in training of late,
and is a good deal more slender and girlish than
she was on some recent appearances. The wel-
come reduction in the dimensions of this favorite
actress has also, apparently, done some good to
her voice ; that organ is always sweet and power-
ful, but it is also fresher and younger than it has
sometimes been. “Struck Oil” is staged more
elaborately than hitherto, a lot of extra machinery,
derricks, and steam-engines being laid on to lend
realism to the oil city in Act 111. Apart from Miss
Maggie Moore herself, the cast is mostly new.
H. R. Roberts wrestles with the character of John
Stofel, and prospers most of the time; now and
then, however, he gets wrecked on some jagged
consonant in the Dutch dialect, and founders
badly. Also, when he returns as a deranged
Dutchman in Act 111, he looks much too broken
and shattered, and when he wakes up and proceeds
to clear out the city of Oilville with a stick, his
sudden activity is incongruous. Mr. J. P.
West, a gentleman whom The Bulletin has
always viewed with feelings of indignation, does
Deacon Skinner, and inherits the curse which
hangs over that black-coated and black-hatted
iniquity; and the rest of the company, Richard
Stewart especially, is satisfactory. The “Chinese
Question,” which comes in at the end, enables this
paper to state, as one having authority, that
Maggie Moore, in loose pants and a pigtail, makes
an entrancing Chinawoman, and that Mr. Roberts,
as a Chinaman, is much better than it expected.
Miss Moore’s farmyard chorus was encored six
times on Saturday, and then that exhausted and
hard-driven lady shut down on her admirers in
disgust.
* * * *
“ Morocco Bound,” at Sydney Lyceum,
undergoes so many changes every night that it has
become several new shows in the course of the past
week. The only consistent features are Wilfred
Shine’s hopeless insolvency and Elton’s desire to
see the harem; everything else is fleeting and
transitory, and subject to ghostlike departures.
The piece promises to get through its four weeks’
season without any actual disaster.
* * * *
“Henry Y.,” at Her Majesty’s, is going
very strong, and there is no prospect of a change
for some considerable time. The astonishing fact
that all the four big Sydney theatres are in full
blast at once ought, according to all modern pre-
cedents, to imply that there will presently be a
bad smash somewhere, but by all appearances
Henry Yee will survive the trouble, whoever goes
under.
* ffi * *
The Bulletin publishes, on page 10, a por-
trait of Miss Isabel Webster, the young and
accomplished soprano now appearing at the
Oxford, Melbourne. From her earliest years
Miss Webster exhibited marked musical talent,
which her conquest (as a pianiste) of the Clarke
Scholarship gave her a chance to develope. With
this view she went to England, and, on the recom-
mendation of the professors at the Royal College
of Music, took up singing as her first study.
After four years in London Miss Webster returned
to Australia, sacrificing several good English and
American offers to appear in light opera. Since
her return in ’9l she has sung at most of the con-
certs given in Melbourne, including two seasons
at the local Exhibition. She also “ supported ”
Madame Sterling during her recent Australian
tour —rather to the disadvantage of the welter-
weight most people thought. “I am now at the
Oxford,” says Miss Webster, “ which some people
think a pity; but I have an idea that variety is
the entertainment of the future.” Which may or
may not be true ; but this is certain : it pays best,
and in these days professionals can’t afford to sing
for the mere good of their health.
** * *
R. S. Smythe believes that Mrs. Besant’s
Sydney season will be a great success. _ He never
had so many applicants for free admission (none
were acceded to), and the number of photographers
eager to get a sitting from “the most eloquent of
living women ”is unprecedented. Three-fourths
of the Melbourne audiences were ladies.
Mrs. Annie Besant was announced to com-
mence her course of lectures at Sydney Opera
House on Tuesday night, and Cyril Tyler, the
“ boy soprano,” was to make his appearance at the
Centenary Hall on Wednesday, Both these events
arrived too late for notice in this issue, and are,
therefore, so far as The Bulletin is concerned,
postponed until next week.
** * *
The Candidate has been the most popu-
lar Melbourne show for the past week. Speaking
generally, The Candidate is a five-act dissem-
bler, who produces himself with the aid of a chair-
man and addresses a hostile meeting from the
platform of a suburban hall. He is also addicted
to appearing for one consecutive night at a pub.,
whilst in rarer instances the degraded mummer
perjures himself upon a lorry. As an entertain-
ment, he would be absurdly dear at a penny, pro-
gramme included, but since he always performs
gratis and can be groaned at with impunity, large
audiences roll up in the hope that some priest or
deacon will give him beans. Another pointy in
The Candidate’s favor is his habit of shouting
drinks for the committee. Altogether, lie is a
blight upon legitimate theatrical enterprise, and
managers will be glad to see him either elected or
dead. Tlmv would prefer him dead.
* # •
Under the political circumstances of the
moment, B. and B. were wise in postponing their
new bill, and although “Lady Windermere’s Fan”
is not a very strong draw at the Princess s, it fills
in the time quite satisfactorily. Oscar Wildes
heartless, soulless play is worth seeing, not for the
sake of its epigrams, which are often meaningless
and sometimes cribbed from deeper cynics, but on
account of its splendidly comic pathos. For any-
body who goes to a theatre in search of unwhole-
some fun there can be nothing more amusing than
Lady Windermere’s sudden awakening to a sense
of maternity. She had forgotten her beloved off-
spring when she took herself off at 2 a.m. to the
giddy bachelor’s apartments, but as soon as her
blackmailing mother says “ God gave you that
child,” Lady Windermere’s face changes for the
better. A strange light comes into her eyes as
she remembers how pretty baby looked in his new
frock, and begins dimly to recognise that Nature
has claims even upon the British aristocracy. One
can understand a proud marquis or a duke being
doosidly affected by this incident. There is much
in Oscar Wilde’s narrative to thrill the peerage
and excite a cheerful contempt in the decent
lower orders. On Saturday the Brough and
Boucieault Co. give a first Australian performance
of “ Sowing the Wind,” by Sydney Grundy, and
this highly-spoken-of play will probably be the
final production of the season.
m* * *
“Quid Ireland,” at Melbourne Royal, is
said to have been acted 500 times by the Taylor-
Carrington crowd in tho back-blocks of this
country. Such specimens of rural drama tend to
keep the inhabitants of large cities from going
forth upon the soil, and must be honestly con-
demned by The Bulletin as bad for the strug-
gling producer. “Quid Ireland” is a collection
of musty scraps from inferior melodramas, ancient
and modern, strung together by Charlie Taylor
aforesaid. That estimable comedian pleads guilty
to the authorship, and seems unaware of the real
character of his offence. His show, instead of
being riotously absurd, is absurdly dull. Also, the
incidental songs are ghastly things set to insuffer-
able tunes in nearly every case. Finally the
sorrow concludes with the rescue of a drugged
man from a railway line. So many drugged men
have been pulled out of the way of a cardboard
train under exactly the same circumstances, and
the train itself is such a disreputable old acquaint-
ance, that the spectacle of Taylor tied to those
eternal rails and struggling to get free when he
hears the eternal puff of the engine is a terrible,
horrible nightmare. Next Saturday (22nd), for
the programme at the Royle is changed regularly
once a week, they play “Unjustly Sentenced. *
** * *
The Urso-Sapio concerts, at Melb. Town
Hall, had the bad luck to fall due about election-
time,’ but the series will not conclude until Patter-
son has been wiped out, or George Turner is
extinguished. Therefore it is possible that these
two capable artists (meaning Urso and Sapio) will
attract at least one big audience before they go
away. A re-opening of the Alexandra is medi-
tated by a hardy author-manager named Inigo
Tyrrell— so rumour whispers.
Percy Grainger’s pianoforte-recital at Melb*
Masonic Hall was quite a brilliant success. The
boy played selections from Beethoven, Bach, Raff,
Schumann and Schubert with charming effect in
every case, and his appearance completed his con-
quest of the audience. It was generally remarked,
and with reason, that if this handsome, thought-
ful-looking little genius had just arrived from
Hingland instead of South Yarra sassiety would
have voted him a miracle. Meanwhile, Percy’s
best musical friends will no doubt manage to get
him a European education, for although he is the
ideal of a show-boy, his prospects are so bright
that it were a pity to make an infant prodigy of
him.
** * *
Talking of prodigies, Madame Belle Cole,
who is said to have “discovered” the little girl
pianist of Cyril Tyler’s Co., will confer a favor on
Australia by not pushing her discoveries any
further. The young lady plugging away at the
box of music is not a pleasing item by any means.
For all the feeling she gets out of the notes in a
general way, she might just as well be treading on
the instrument or smiting it with a hammer.
** * *
The Williamson and Musgrove Comic
Opera Co. is doing a good business at Brisbane
Opera House, but not so good as last year. The
reserved stalls fill up well enough, but on only
one night has the dress circle been filled, and that
was the first night of the season. It would almost
appear that B, and B. had taken away from Bris-
bane the bulk of the available amusement-money.
“Paul Jones” was not a huge success, but “The
Vicar of Bray ” was played for two nights to
houses full only in the cheaper parts. “Ma Mie
Rosette ” caught on so well that it was staged for
five nights instead of four, as originally announced.
One of the drawbacks of the season has been Wal-
lace Brownlow’s sore throat. He was out of the
bill for three nights and had his throat operated
upon, after which there was some improvement.
“Ma Mie Rosette” will be the opening opera of
the Co.’s Sydney season, a fortnight hence. In a
general way, iir is the best of things operatic yet
seen in Brisbane. A great success was made by
Lauri’s wife—Miss Marietta Nash—whose eccen-
tric dancing was a distinct novelty in its way.
“ The Mountebanks ” was to be produced for two
nights beginning Tuesday (18th) and “Ma’mzelle
Nitouche ” on Thursday (20th).
* % ifc *
Gov. Norman is distinctly the most popu-
lar of all Australian viceroys. When he visits the
theatre the band, of course, plays the orthodox
anthem, but the boys in the pit and stalls give him
a big chorus of “For he’s a jolly good fellow ” and
end with rounds of cheers. Brough, Lohr and
Musgrove have each declared that these spon-
taneous receptions are without parallel in their
Australian experience.
At Poverty Point.
People reading the gushing accounts in the Melb. paper*
of Nellie Stewart’s performance in “Ma Mie Rosette,”
wonder why she didn’t make a hit in London. There is
ample room for her at present here (writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent) if she will only come and
show Londoners what she is really made of.
Clement Scott has been approached revisiting Australia
as a lecturer next year. He does not strike one as a likely
success. He has never done anything, from a vulgar
public point of view.
Sylvia Grey on returning from her honeymoon found
that a lot of jewels, given her by her Gaiety admirers,
had been stolen. Sylvia, by the way, pines for the foot-
lights, but Mr. Fenwick will not hear of her return to the
stage.
Haddon Chambers was hopeful that the new play he
has written for London Adelphi, in collaboration with
B. 0. Stephenson (it is cabled to have been a success),
would be a turning-point in his fortunes.
Smythe advertised Talmage as “ the man who speaks
with most miraculous organ.” It has just occurred to
people that this is a most indelicate way to allude to a
gentleman’s nose.
"Lady Windermere’s Fan” is the paradox of the B.
and B. Melb. season. The management were more
nervous over it than anything, yet it redeems the failures
of “The Bauble Shop’’ and “Cabinet Minister.” This is
the more extraordinary, because the piece is of the
society class, which Melbourne will not endure, on the
most experienced managerial dicta.
Music-halls copy each other, like newspapers. The
Melb. Oxford and Alhambra each has its star lady vocalist.
She does not deign to sit in the semi-circle with the other
ladies, but the interlocutor respectfully leads her on from
the side.
The syndicate running Cyril Tyler’s tour are C. J.
Stevens, P. A. Howell, and Tommy Hudson—all Ade-
laideans. They made money out of Antoinette Sterling
and Belle Cole.
Gov. Robinson’s operetta will be publicly heard as a
Melb. Princess matinee, or at most a one-night affair,
during Cup week. The Firm’s own people will undertake
it. Miss Katherine Hardy, whose name has been men-
tioned as one of the principal singers, will not perform:
she is engaged for Maggie Moore’s production of “ Little
Jack Sheppard ” at the Royal.
Young Stayton, who has made such a hit as the frock-
coated idiot in “ Morocco Bound,” was until lately doing
small parts with Bland Holt, but drama was apparently
not his line. He is an unassuming young fellow, of whom
creditable things in comic opera were expected by his
many Melbourne friends.
Lance Lenton goes to Brisbane shortly to look after the
interests of Jones and Yorke, who open a variety show at
the local Gaiety Theatre, Nov. 9th.
A scratch performance of “Jack Sheppard,” the drama,
at Melb. Alexandra, was gravely reported by the local
dailies as a revival of “ Little Jack Sheppard ” the bur-
lesque. The Arous even committed itself to a gush over
Dora Mostyn’s “ qualifications for extravaganza,” the
oritio having, perhaps, struck the show at a moment
when the little woman was putting in some variety
business.
In the course of Minnie Palmer’s “theatrical” divorce
proceedings it transpired that “Yours Merrily ” Rogers
had laid down this doctrine in a letter to his fluffy-haired
angel—" America for money ; England for a home.”
“ All the world’s a stage ’’—with the Devil for prompter.

At Dublin Nation (and Irish Catholic)
office, the old home of Irish “liberty,” there is
now a lock-out of all Union compositors !
FLETCHER’S PILLS
never fail to cure
INDIGESTION,
COSTIVENESS,
SOUR BREATH,
HEARTBURN,
LIVER DISEASE, and
KIDNEY COMPLAINTS.
FLETCHER’S PILLS and CLEMENTS
TONIC are the recognised household remedies of
the Australian colonies, and every designing quack
tries to trade on their reputation and renown.
This is the greatest proof of their merit, and
sufferers want to be particular to get the genuine
articles, as regret and disappointment are sure to
follow the use of the thousands of “All-failing"
remedies so freely advertised. The reputation
and wide-spread use of Clements Tonic and Flet
oher’s Pills are the greatest proofs of their ap-
preciation by the public. If they were not as
represented they would have passed out of memory
long ere this ; but instead their sale is greater and
they are more esteemed day by day and week by
week, and this emphatically proves their undis-
puted supremacy. Listen to no argument from
interested parties; demand the genuine articles
and TAKE NO SUBSTITUTE.
Personal Items.
Julius Vogel, now in England, was
originally a chemist’s assistant in Melbourne.
A nephew of “Thunderbolt,” the bush-
ranger, sits in an Australian Parliament as a
Labor member.
The Austral Salon did not capture Mrs.
Besant and get her to write her name on the roll
of its skinned lions.
A juror in Space v. Time case died the
other day—from old age contracted in the jury-
box. “Sedentary habits” again.
At Rockhampton (Q.), the wife of one of
the discoverers of one of the biggest gold-mines on
earth has been arrested as a vagrant.
After all, old Silas Harding, of Geelong,
didn’t “cut up” so immensely wealthy. Real
estate, £43,000 ; personalty, £242,000.
More Greeks. Pericles now practises physio
in Sydney, and Aristides was lately fined at a
suburban court £5 for sultry language.
Pastor Fitchett calls sin “an impertin-
ence.” Which attempt at original definition lets
down easily the breaking of the ten command-
ments.
A Sydney newspaper prefaces its weather-
reports: “We are indebted to the Government
Astronomer, Mr. H. 0. Russell, 8.A., 0.M.G.,”
&c., &c.
Sydney’s recent visitor, Fraser-Smith,
editor and proprietor of the Hong Kong Tele-
graph, has defended 39 libel-actions. His paper
is popular accordingly.
On dit that Mme. Couvreur (nde Huybers,
of Tasmania, better known as “ Tasma”)has been
appointed Times correspondent in Brussels, in
succession to her late husband.
The visiting-card of one of the new N.S.
Wales M.’sP. reads ; —“Mr. Blank, proprietor of
the Blankstown Inflamkr, and member of the
Legislative Assembly for Blank.”
“ Just what I was like when a baby—pure
pink and white,” was the exclamation of an old
lady on seeing the one and only N.S.W. Labour
Electoral League boss orator, Holman.
A brother of British Chancellor Harcourt,
who, in figure, much resembles the burly Liberal
statesman, lives quietly in Melbourne. He is a
lawyer by profession, but not now practising.
Graham Berry must be the most agitated
candidate over the Vic. elections. His throw-in
for the Speakership entirely depends on a paltry
constituency, with local men hissing out of their
goose-necks.
Helen Hart has settled any stray doubts
as to Vic. Speaker Bent’s moral character. She
went to his election meeting the other night and
publicly shook hands with the gentleman amidst
loud applause.
S.A. Legislative Council numbers six Aus-
tralian natives, including the President, out of 24
members. The Assembly counts 19 natives, in-
cluding Speaker, Premier, Treasurer, and Lands
Minister, out of 54.
The sufficiently-gilded Miss
Chirnside, Victoria’s “ greatest
heiress,’’really did give up £60,000
and a prospective peer in marrying
Mr. Everard Browne. By her
uncle’s will that sum extra was to
accrue to her if she captivated a
title, with a man attached.
Late ex-Mayor Cripps, of
Bathurst, whose suicide is re-
ported, first became prime magis-
trate of the City of the Plains by
a fluke. The local orange and
green sections were fighting the
seat when Cripps slipped in ahead.
His reign was not remarkable for
gluttony —in fact, it is related that
he once entertained 15 thirsty
Bathurst men on one bottle of
fizz, and, so it is asserted, drank
his own health and led the cheers
for the Mayor (Cripps). His
election was the cause of much
bitterness between the local con-
temporaries.
George Collins Levey has
many irons in the fire. In addi
t.ion to being London corres-
pondent to Melb. Age, he is sec.
to the London Commission for
Hobart Exhibition ; edits a city
paper, the Citizen, in which he
dogmatises on municipal law and
reform with all the self assur-
ance of a recognised authority on
the subject; partly edits anillus-
trated weekly, in which he has an
interest; acts as sec. to a local
agitation for a branch railway line
from Uunmow to Essex, where he
lives; and, it is said, is a candi-
date for the historic flitch of
bacon. With all this work he is
prepared to undertake any new
business which will bring grist to
his mill; anything, in fact, from
managing the National Debt to
selling Australian butter.
Treasurer Holder is, perhaps, the ablest
financier who has ever sat on S.A. Treasury
benches. Holder wears a black frock-coat, is six
feet high, as thin as a match, and has a face that
would sadden an undertaker.
“ Commissioner ” Coombs, of the Melb.
S.A., returned from a European pilgrimage, says
Gen. Booth is about to descend upon Australia
again. Ho comes to spread " trewth ” and collect
“ oof,” but he will find oof is off.
The S.M. Herald leader, indicating in-
discretion on the part of T. B. Clegg, clerk of
awards, for his well-meant interposition in the
shearer-pastoralist difficulty, made the one-time
editor of Sydney Echo heavy at heart.
Father Hilary, of the Passionists, nrw
missionising through the back-blocks, gives the
pledge against all alcoholic drinks except Austra-
lian wine, his theory being that Australia is a
wine-country, and that wine-drinkers are rarely
drunkards.
S.A. Legislative Council has affirmed the
desirableness of having Mr. Wragge’s forecasts
officially published by S.A. Government. This
places the local meteorologist, Todd, in an awk-
ward position, as he and his learned brothers claim
that Wraggo has boycotted them, or vice versd.
“Tummy” Wales is getting quite thin,
and has almost recovered his pristine elegance of
figure. This, remarks a correspondent, is the
result of a course of thyroid tabloids—introduced
by one Trevor-Jones, who is coining money by
this treatment for corpulency. MTlwraith please
note.
Our dear old friend, Alfred of Saxe-
Ooburg, erst of Edinburgh, is showing his beloved
subjects how mean he can be. The late Duke
always maintained a State theatre at his own cost.
The present one asked his Parliament to pay the
cost. This refused, Alfred is going to shut the
theatre.
Be fighty if you would advance. Sleath,
convicted of law-breaking, and the Broken Hill
bank-manager who assailed him with a ruler, are
both doing very well. Sleath is M.L. A., and can
talk now in Parliament as he dare not talk abroad,
and the bank-manager is now inspector of
branches.
Premier Seddon, of Maoriland, addressing
a recent meeting of electors, was much riled by
frequent interjections from a person on a front
seat. Quoth Dick, in an undertone, “Just you
keep your cursed tongue quiet!” He did keep his
cursed tongue quiet. He could see a big thump-
ing ahead.
A Victorian veteran named Perry has
invented “a flying-machine for military purposes,”
which threatens to be as big a success as the
Brennan torpedo. The inventor leaves for England
shortly, with good financial backing, and bearing
enthusiastic testimonials from Govt, astronomer
Ellery and other well-known scientists.
The returned Australian Primate, Bishop
Bob-cab Smith, “ was very pleased with Canada
as a whole,” and has approved of the scenery
erected by the Almighty in the Rocky Mountains.
A Prelatical Father doesn’t pan out much more
solid result than the Prodigal Son, and isn’t senti-
mentally a patch on that misguided husker.
Miss Badham urges women to insist on
their political rights. She was invited recently to
contribute weekly to an Australian paper, but
declined, as the editorial policy was hostile to her
ideas on woman progress. As great-great grand-
daughter, in direct line, of poet Campbell, she
considers she has a literary reputation to protect.
The report which lately went the rounds
of the press that N.S.W. Gov. Duff had a nephew
truant-officering in Victoria appears to have been
“sound’’only. Gov. Duff, who is essentially a
family man and loyal to his clan, though he dis-
likes kilts, has tried to discover this Highland
scion, but has failed ignominiously in the
endeavour.
Smythe has Mark Twain on his list of
celebrities to visit Australia in the future. The
American funny man is more likely to find real
humour in Australian life than the French

John Downer, Q. 0., yielding to some in-
explicable Imperialistic impulse, paraded in Wind-
sor uniform for the Adelaide Mayor’s Ball, and so
became the observed of all observers. The com-
ments on his personal appearance were not
complimentary, for his legs are nearly as ugly as
his face. Anyone who knows him will recognise
the severity of this statement.
The young lady who, some two years ago,
went around the drapery houses of Melb. and got
a lot of credit on the strength of her representa-
*4ons that she was a “daughter of the Speaker,”
has much to do with the clerical opposition that
has arisen against him in Brighton (Via). Pos-
sibly had Mr. Bent submitted to the attempted
extortion there would be no trouble now.
According to Parson M’Meekin (Eden-
hope. Via), not only the little town of Harrow
(Vic.), but the big, wide world has lost a triple-
starred genius by the death of David Edgar, J.P.;
for, said the holy man, as he passported the soul
beyond the grave: “If he had launched out into
politics, he would have been a Beaconsfield ; in
law, a Higinbotham; or in literature, a Carlyle.”
Buttering a corpse is a fine art.

Charles Gavan Duffy, now 78, is bringing
out a volume, “My Life in Two Hemispheres,”
Apropos, a story is current that Duffy was, on one
occasion, returned to Vic. Parliament by a
majority of one, the numbers being 998 and 997.
A son of Gavan’s meeting an elector shortly after-
wards thanked him as being “one of my father’s
998 supporters.” “I was two of them,” was the
reply. The free and independent had voted
twice.
“Joe” Marks, reputedly the best all-
round man in Sydney at Supreme Court practice,
had the conduct of the Railway Commissioners’
case in the bigProudfoot litigation, and celebrated
the “amicable settlement” by getting married.
Apropos of Joe’s methods of business. A Q.C.
once said of him that he wouldn’t consent to enter
Heaven unless he had previously sent the
Almighty a registered letter stating that he did so
“without prejudice.”
Otto Berliner, of Melb., died last week.
Many years ago, he was a leading man in the
detectives, representing a kind of new school, in
rivalry to Black and Jack Williams, principals of
the old. He was a good detective, but over-fond
of newspaper glory. He was the first of the
honest private detectives to make a reputation;
never a whisper was heard against his personal
character. Newspaper-glory men don’t become
detective chiefs, like Kennedy and Brown.
To get “ far from the madding crowd,” as
R. L. Stevenson has done in Samoa, has for years
been the dream of that modest litterateur, George
Gordon M'Crae, who selected the Seychelles
Islands as his place of utopian exile. When
M'Crae retired from the Victorian Civil Service,
as young at 60 as the average man of 40, he
betook himself to that paradise of the Indian
Ocean, but the climate proved ruinous to his
health, and he has now returned to Melbourne
invalided and robbed of, the hope cherished
throughout the past decade of his life.
Now that new ideas in finance are urgently
wanted, a graduated tax on names might fill a
rather extensive gap. The only exemptions would
be John Smith and Bill Jones, and ladies named
Sarah. After that the tax would get up by degrees,
until a person with a name like Augustus Fitz-
gibbon would pay about £5 per annum. All
hyphenated names would be taxed ; also all
Christian names after the first one, with the single
exception of Mary Ann, which ought to go free.
The Government which wanted to tax a person
with a name like Mary Ann would be destitute of
the finer feelings of humanity.
Cyril Tyler, the boy-soprano, doesn’t take
at all kindly to a suggested method of preserving
his voice : “What’s the good of making money if
you can’t enjoy yourself?” he asks inconse-
quentially. The Tyler is a nerveful boy. John-
son, M.P., took him to the Adelaide Zoo, and
Minohin, the director, showed the distinguished
visitor round, and with him entered some of the
enclosures. Turning suddenly, he saw young
Tyler just about to stroke the Bengal tiger, which
was rubbing itself enticingly against the bars of
its cage. “ Yah 1 ” was the mutual yell of the two
protectors, startled to see a £20,000 voice in such
awful peril, and the indiscreet singer was hurriedly
snatched out of harm’s way.
“ Charlie ” Millar, the rich Australian
contractor, has his yacht in commission
and Somers-Vine and Agent-General Duncan
Gillies had at latest joined him at Cowes to as.-ist
in entertaining Tummy, who lunched on board
the Australian’s vessel. The Saide has just re-
turned from the Arctic here she has been on an
abortive voyage to discover traces of the missing
Wellman expedition. Millar, by the way, has
been bitten by the mania for a double-barrelled
name, and is known in England (per press-notice
communicated) as Mr. “ Gibson-Millar.” No
doubt by-and-by “Sir” will be added (through
Vine’s engineering). A few thousands to the
languishing funds of the Imperial Institute would
do the trick easily.

So far, office has made no
difference in N.S.W. Post-
master-General Cook’s habit
ofliving. He still wears the
same clothes as those in which
he first represented the miners
who sent him from the pit-
mouth to Parliament, and still
lives in the same £l-a-week
coffee-palace, kept by a colored
gentleman. And Joseph is
wiseinhis generation. Directly
he changes his plebeian garb
for the smug frock-coat and
top-hat the outraged prole-
tariat will jump on him with
all its hobnails. If a democra-
tic politician wants to wear a
top-hat, he must wear it from
the start. Apropos, The Bul-
letin knows one N.S. Wales
M.L.C. who, while holding a
portfolio in a Parkes Ministry,
obstinately stuck to the same
old £l-a-week lodgings, and
thus managed to save enough
out of his Ministerial screw
to give him a modest life-com-
petency.
“Your young Australian sculptor, Mac-
kennal,” says The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “ has a fine studio at St. John’s Wood,
but he has so many commissions to execute that
he already finds it necessary to have the place
enlarged, and the builders are at work. He has
just joined the Savage Club, which, by the way,
is going strong and well, many of the old members
who had deserted it under the Vine rigime having
returned to the fold.”
Dear Bulletin, —You do not do justice to
Mr. Boldrewood in the matter of Mr. Becke. His
acknowledgment runs (I take the Telegraph ver-
sion this time): —
T DESIRE to acknowledge my indebtedness to Mr. lows
x Becke, author of “By Reef and Palm,” for the South
Sea Island portion of “ A Modern Buccaneer,” excepting
the chapter headed “ Poisoned Arrows,” which we founded
on a diary of a whaling cruise by my late father.
T. A. BROWNE.
Albury, 13/8/94.
(“ We founded ” is probably a misprint for “ was
founded as it appears in the Herald). Now,
“the chapter headed ‘Poisoned Arrows’ occupies
from page 87 to page 110 of 1 A Modern Bucca-
neer ’ (erroneously spelt ‘ Bookaneer ’); page 87 to
98 (11 pages) are by Boldrewood ; page 98 to
page 110 (12 pages) are by Becke. This is why
Melb. Leader unconsciously kicks Boldrewood
by republishing Becke’s particular part of this
especial chapter (without a line of Browne’s part)
as “a thrilling episode from ‘A Modern Bucca-
neer.’”—Yours, Sir Fretful.


POLITICAL
POINTS
The man in the blouse sits in the French
Legislature, but nob in the Australian.
The minimum-wage is coming in Victoria.
Williamstown is the latest municipality to favor
the principle.
Patterson says he has sent word to tne
Colonial Office that the next Yio. Governor must
come in on a retrenched screw.
There is a falling-out in the Maoriland
Woman’s Reform Dress League. There are now
two parties, the divided skirt and the knicker
party.
A N. S. Wales M.P., has already pro-
tested against the action of the brass band in
biasing out, “ Oh, boys, we’ll never get drunk any
more,” at the opening of Parliament.
The Argus with its rhymes for the people,
the ‘‘ creed of the country,” and big italics freely
sprinkled over its pages, recalls John Bull and
the other political papers of the Reform time.
According to S.M. Herald’s Jeames in
London, English Corean-war sympathies are, for
trado reasons, with China. Australians, if they
bother about the affair at all, barrack for Japan.
Speaker Bent, the day after the parsonic
denunciation of him at Brighton, was regarded as
absolutely safe. His opponent, Moule, rushed to
the press, with a disclaimer of the clerical tactics.
“Mr. Mountain,” said the chairman,
introducing W. J. Mountain, M.P., a hybrid
politician, to an Albert Park meeting. Coarse
democrat at the back, “ Yah 1 he’s on’y a mole-
hill.”
Millionaire Burdekin’s successful repudia-

cerned in putting down Victoria’s free-pass
swindles. For, “out of courtesy,” .Victoria’s

“I believe in a puritanical Sunday, but it should
be made enjoyable.” In other words, “ I believe
in asafoetida as a perfume, but it must be made to
stink sweetly.”

Argus as to which party is responsible for Bent
as Speaker, reminds one of Max Adeler’s story of

dividing fence.

One Smith, a pious hatter, has been sitting
•or the South Melbourne constituency that used
to love David Gaunson in days of yore. Smith is
(. slow, methodical driveller, democratic in senti-
ment, but fearfully Tory by instinct, and no live
Bitizen can hear him addressing the decorous
toilers who wear his hats without wanting to
whoop for David. A droning saint, supported by
the Nonconformist conscience, is a sad thing to
encounter at an election-meeting, and when his
n ame happens to be Smith your cup of sorrow
runs over.
S. A. Labour-party publishes a weekly paper
in a week or two, and not before it is wanted.
The Voice, the Single-tax paper, started in
Adelaide by Medway Day, died of Single-tax.
Single-tax has slain pretty nearly every paper
that ever touched it.
The declaration of the Reid Government
that it will appoint no outsiders to the N.S.W.
Civil Service hardly squares with the rumour that
a Minister’s sister-m-law, or niece, or something,
has already been appointed to a sub-matronship at
a Government hospital.
The Queenslander says that the Q. Labor
members of Parliament are “essentially lawless,”
and, in illustration remarks that, in a House of 71
members, 17 should promptly submit. What
would the parliamentary history of the world bo
were such a rule of thumb final law ?
One of the recently - elected N.S.W.
“ solidarity ” Ms.P. has promised to give up .£1
per week of his Parliamentary salary, and to
report himself weekly to his Electoral League.
“ Fined .£1 and bound over to appear when called
upon.” It looks like a political ticket-of-leave.
The Labor-candidates at the Vic. general
election are solid in demanding that there shall be
no reduction of Customs duties on any article
which can be manufactured in Victoria. The
alleged “ Freetrade reaction” was born dead;
also, there never was such a thing at any time.
When N.S.W. Assembly balloted for the
(paid) Public Works Committee, last week, there
were about 100 members present. Of these, 79
received votes. Among the 79 there were 31 who
received one vote apiece. Not an unreasonable
inference ; Of 31 M. ’sP. each voted for himself !
Just before the recent N.S.W. elections a
man who had been chosen and wished to act as
poll-clerk at a country booth, waited on a J.P. to
sign his declaration. He couldn’t write but he
made his mark, which the sapient Jay pee duly
witnessed and passed for the clerk who couldn’t
write.

The last has by no means been heard of
the big Proudfoot railway-case, lately settled in
Sydney. Owing, apparently, to a dispute between

graphs of certain most interesting documents in
the handwriting of a defunct politician, and is
going to submit them to the Assembly.
The island of Santa Cruz has been hither-
to debarred the Queensland recruiters on account
of the poor physique of the natives. “Of late,”
says a sugar journal, “ a better class of natives
has been seen, and the little island is now furnish-
ing recruits.” Pshaw! The real reason is that
the increasing scarcity of recruits is forcing the
Government blackbirders to lower the standard.

YARRA BANKER THE SECOND: “We haven’t had the last one yet.”
The Southern Government has artfully
‘thrown a beautiful apple of discord among the
Northern and Central Q. Separatists. It is pro-
posed to connect Hughenden and Winton by rail,
thus robbing the Central line and dear old log-
rolling Rockhampton of the best of their trade
and diverting it northwards.
“A straw shows.” When in N.S.W.
Council last week someone urged that troops be
dispatched to quell the shearers’ insurrection,
Chairman of Committees Jacob ejaculated : “Yes,
with Gatling guns.” Considering that Jacob is
dependent for his billet on the votes of members,
the ejaculation is significant as illustrating the
true feelings of the Council majority.
The Aoe omits from its reports of Geo
Turner’s speeches all reference to the question so
frequently put him—whether he will, if elected,
support any propositions for paying defendant’s
costs, in Speight v. Syme, out of the Treasury.
Turner says frankly that he will do nothing of the
kind. It doesn’t suit the purpose that that should
be known, so the answer is suppressed. As a
matter of fact, it is well-known that feelers were
put out to Patterson & Co. to see whether they
would make a proposal to this effect, treating the
trial as a State affair.
The seats of 14 Victorian M.’sL.C. became
vacant this month by effluxion of time, and it is
significant of the hopeless political stagnation of
the country that twelve of the retiring fossils
came back unopposed.
Wise, M.P., fired the first shot from the
Parkesian corner on the debate on the introduc-
tion of the new Land Bill. It will be interesting
to watch the old veteran play the game of his poli-
tical youth in his extreme age.
The Bill providing for the election of
Ministers by Parliament has been lost in Maori-
land Assembly by 27 votes against 21. With such
a narrow majority, the end of Government by
Contradiction is almost in sight.
The two principal “test” candidates in
Victoria are Tom Bent, at Brighton, and Bryan
O’Loghlen, at Port Fairy. If they get back into
Parliament on the strength of their public records
very little political meaning will attach to the
election of the other 93 members.
SUICIDE OF A DUKE.—AN EXTRAORDINARY MOTIVE.
“LONDON, Sept. 13. —The Duke of San Carlos has committed suicide in
Madrid. The Duke was driven to the deed through distress at his inability
to attain moral perfection.” He should have gone for the usual goal,
“Immoral Perfection,” which has, up to date, apparently been
within the reach of every Duke.
Whenever the Castlemaine electors betrayed
a disposition to howl Patterson down he plain-
tively besought them “to respect the position he
held ” As he had been practically booted out of
the premiership, and only recovered it for a time
by the dissolution dodge, Patterson s wail is about
as cheeky as that of the man who stole a white
wig and when produced in court, wearing the
spoil pleaded with the Bench to respect his grey
hairs’. Perhaps, however, James expected the
crowd to respect his K.C.M.G.-ship. There is
no limit to the assurance of such a man.
H. H. Champion, standing for Albert
Park (Melb.) was the chief success of the elec-
tioneering campaign, as a plain, straightforward
talker to the point. Champion says nothing with-
out giving a reason for his doctrine, and at
answering every kind of question from both sides
of it at once, he is a much smarter man than
Deakin. He makes 10 words go farther than 50
from the ordinary platform spouter. He un-
doubtedly educates his audience to opinions which
they don’t come prepared for, as, for instance,
when he tells them that State banks and all
schemes of socialism involving the handling of
public moneys, must do more harm than good if
they be maladministered. He shows them how
Credit Fonder funds in the hands of Melbourne
Savings Bank Commissioners would probably not
bo more wisely dealt with than the cash of the
Savings Bank itself. He insists upon the urgent
need for public honesty with the logical force of a
Mrs. Besant, but in a more worldly, matter-of-fact
style. Champion’s style lacks the features that
most impress an audience, especially a working-
man audience. He is a slight, young-looking
speaker, with a voice unlike a bull’s—no fire,
gesture, or air of “deep sense of responsibility ”
about him. However, he has been a revelation in
fluent democratic argument to the denizens of
Albert Park, and if they don’t return Champion
in preference to the late member (an ex-night-soil
contractor) his failure will be entirely owing to
the lateness of his arrival on the scene.

For the 95 seats in Vic. Legislative
Assembly 38 candidates are auctioneers, estate
and financial agents, and 24 are either barristers
or solicitors, but in only one electorate (St. Kilda)
is shark eating shark. Lawyers have killed Par-
liamentary Govt, in the U.S., and will some day
reduce this country to the level of Uruguay. And
yet they spend =£9ooo a year on rabbit-extirpation
down South in Davies Territory !
Significant. Graham Berry drops the
“Sir” on his election-posters. When old and
rich and fat and retired, an Australian politician
can perhaps afford to wear a title, just as a man
dead and decomposed can afford to have lies
written on his tombstone. But, otherwise, a title
is the political brand of Cain. It is in polities
w'hat swollen noses and thick ears are in leprosy.
K.0.M.G., literally interpreted, means : I have
been bought. lam a Public Enemy.
When Premier Nelson, of Queensland, in-
troduced his Bill giving magistrates power to
imprison “ suspected persons ” for a lengthy term
without evidence or trial, Glassey demanded more
information before allowing the motion to pass as
formal. Whereupon Nelson expressed his astonish-
ment that any person could be so evil-disposed as
to put obstacles in the way of a measure whose
very title—the Peace Preservation Bill—was suffi-
cient proof of its excellence. Considering the
villainous means by which Nelson proposes to
preserve the peace, that old man’s well-acted
astonishment marks him as a first-class hypocrite.
The Maoriland shearer, who comes over
to Australia so plentifully in strike seasons and
fills the vacant sheds with the best and most
reliable non - union labor ever supplied in this
country, is a dismal commentary on the “federal
spirit.” The Maorilanders fought their big strike
far more ably and perhaps more tenaciously than
the Australians ever did. But while they are
prepared to fight all they know for their own
unionism, a very large number of them care
nothing at all for Australian unionism, and will
help to sit on its head without the slightest com!
punetion. The Australian isn’t a man and a
brother in their eyes—not much.
Eccentric Kaiser Wilhelm has not broken
out in a fresh place—it is the same old place with
a fresh breach in it. This lovely potentate is on
the most affable terms with the Almighty, and
“Me and God” is his modern substitute for
Cardinal Wolsey’s “ Ego et rex raeus ” —with the
difference that God, unlike Henry the Eighth,
can’t hit back. His recent tirade against the
nobility, concluding with the words, “Onward
with God against the parties of revolution, and
dishonour be his who forsakes me!” could astonish
people who know only of the physical ailment*
from which he constantly suffers, and are not
aware that these ailments are fought and over
thrown by the regular use of that world-famed
remedy, Marshall’s Tonic Nervine.
CHANGE OF MINISTRY.
It is hard to get two heads under one hat, or to
get a cabinet to agree—hence, changes of Minis-
tries have become so frequent in our days, that it
is rather a surprise to meet an M.P. who has not
been a minister some time or another. A marvel-
lous exception, however, is the unanimity with
which ministers of all opinions and shades agree,
and say that a better smoke than Frossard’s Cavour
Cigars at 8 for 1/- has never come under their
notice. They are mild, aromatic, and do not try
the nerves.
POLLING DAY IN VICTORIA.
Cast your votes for Binks and Bloggs,
Time has proved ’em dirty dogs :
Trusty members, Bloggs and Binks,
Hardened to financial stinks.
Active servants of the State,
Jobbing early, jobbing late;
Destitute of heart and soul,
They deserve to head the poll.
Cronk in thought and cronk in deed.
They are just the men we need.
None can say why this is so.
But into Parliament —
Ha ! ha ! ha ! liament,
Into Parliament they will go.
Good old hands at rolling logs.
Brother Binks and Brother Bloggs,
While they swim the country sinks —
What’s the odds to Bloggs and Binks I
Barefaced swindles are in vogue,
Melbourne dearly loves a rogue—
They were in the ’Ouse before,
Send ’em back to stink some more.
If the outlook’s really black,
All the mere we want ’em back.
None can say why this is so.
But into Parliament —
Ha ! ha ! ha ! liament,
Into Parliament they will go.

Society
Among the regulations of N.S.W. police-
force is one that is very much abused. It allows
a policeman, who has “run in ” a drunk, or some-
one, during the night, to leave duty two hours
before those of his colleagues who have nob been
so fortunate —the object of the concession, being,
of course, to enable the “trap” to have a few
hours’ sleep before appearing, a little later on, at
the court to prosecute. On a wet, miserable
night the policeman who studies his health and
comfort always takes good care to make a case for
the morning. Then he is snugly abed by four,
instead of by six a m.

Dear Bullets, — That Hibernian lady's
boast to Lord Hopetoun was a little different from
your account. This is how I have it, “Justluk
at my man Mike ! When Oi first knew him he
hadn’t a sate to his trousers, and now he’s gob a
sate in Parlyment.” Then, confidentially: “Do
ye think he have anny chance fur Mimber fur
Eddication, yer Wurship?”—W.S.W.

The latest Vic. election dodge. Tout looks
in at front gate and calls out to youngster playing
in the garden ; then, in a deeply-impressive tone :
“Do you say your prayers every night, my little
man?” Little man: “Yes,” “And, my little
friend, do you say * God bless father and mother ’
in your prayers ? ” Little man does. Then says
the tout: “My dear little friend, here is a penny
for you. Now, to-night you say, ‘ God bless
father, and mother, and God bless good, kind Mr.
Bent and pub him into Parliament.’ Now, don’t
forget to let your father hear you say your
prayers. Good-bye, little man.”
• * •
That Sydney Sunday paper’s discussion on
“ The Man I would Like to Marry ” discloses one
unexpected fact. Hardly 5 per cent, of the
women who wrote describing their ideal husband
wanted to marry a teetotaller. The curse of
owlish sombreness is upon the male teetotaller —
female teetotallers are not sombre as a rule, and
no woman wants to marry a half-dead slab of self-
sufficient misery.

History repeats itself. Gov. Duffs offer
of “colonial” beer to Sydney Lancers, at the
recent opening of N.S.W. Parliament, recalls
Gov. Charles Hotham and his first Melbourne
State ball. From meanness, or want of know-
ledge, the staple drink for the night was
“ Murphy’s best XXX colonial.”
What has become of the Vic.
Journalists’ Association?

In the exciting row among
the Manly ratepayers over the pro-
posed local sewerage-scheme, Dross
backs Dirt. Absentee property-
holders, aided by the more ignorant,
uncivilised and close-fisted local
residents, object to the small extra
expanse which proper drainage
would involve—hence the trouble.
Manly has always given itself airs
as a health-resort, and it is only
as a health-resort that any reason-
able excuse can be offered for the
existence of a place so difficult to
reach. If Manly won’t wash, it
had better die decently. It isn’t
nice to smell anything that’s alive.
# * *
An ingenious rascal, the othei
day, wrote anonymously to a pep-
pery and volatile Northern mer-
chant, advising him that his (the
merchant’s) wife had employed a
“ tall dark man with a brown ulster
to watch him on her account.” Sure
enough the t.d.m. with the ulster
was hanging about outside the mer-
chant’s office next day, and the
merchant assaulted him grievously,
without waiting to ask for any ex-
planation. A solicitor’s interview
with the merchant showed him that
the supposed watcher was there on
other business, and the assault was
squared for a tenner. But, if the
merchant had only known that the
anonymous informer was thebrown-
ulstered man himself, and that the
watcher-informer had simply taken
advantage of knowledge that the
merchant was a gay dog to raise a
few pounds, the affair would have
ended very differently.
This week is published the portrait of Miss
Edith Courtenay, of the “ Morocco Bound ” Co.,
who so cleverly burlesques the “ society skirt
dance ” in the musico-farcical production still
going strong at Sydney Lyceum. However, Miss
Courtenay herself considers her metier to be
that of a vocalist and actress rather than of a
dancer. She was the principal contralto (Lady
Sophy) of the 00. lately sent to America by
Mr. D’Oyley Carte for the first production
of Gilbert and Sullivan’s “ Utopia Limited ”
—and returned to London only to be en-
gaged for “Morocco Bound,” in Australia,
whither she sailed at very short notice. Miss
Courtenay was born in London in 1871, and after
various appearances throughout England she
attracted the attention of the well-known
manager, Mr. Marcus Meyer, who engaged her to
support Agnes Huntingdon in the big American
tour of “ Paul Jones.” Her career has, so far,
been very fortunate.
Adelaide has, for 18 months, lacked a R.C-
Archbishop, and now Dr. Kennion has left the
Anglicans. Worldly folk suggest that retrench-
ment being a cardinal doctrine in most religions
the two billets should be rolled into one.
* * «
The Christian World sagely opines that
the reason why Anglican bishops transfer their
services so frequently to England, whereas R.O.
bishops stick to their sees in Australia, is that the
former have wives and the latter haven’t. Always
throw the blame on Eve.

After the Adelaide Mayor’s ball. Exit
from the vestibule a high official with a lady on
his arm and two others behind. Watchful con-
stable, in charge of traffic, yells : “Mr. ’s
carriage !” There is a whispered correction and
the annoyed functionary amends the original
shout with “Mr. ’s keb I” Still another
expostulation, and he shrieks with undisguised
disgust and disdain : “Fust keb on the renk I”
amid the laughter of the crowd.
» * »
Judge Harding, in Q. Supreme Court,
recently drew attention to that good old all-Austra-
lian scandal —the lack of provision for the efficient
defence of indigent prisoners. In Queensland a
total sum of £lO is provided for the defence of
aborigines in serious cases—for a white man prac-
tically nothing. In the words of Judge Harding:
“ He is supposed in the most distressing situation
to have all his wits about him and to be able to
meet the most skilled lawyers the country can
provide.” In this matter Australia contrasts
most unfavorably with America, where counsel for
the defence in capital cases is most amply re-
munerated.

Mrs. Besanfc’s orations drew the Melb.
curiosities out of their holes, like moles or bats.
Queer, long-haired people, rat-eyed or ferret-eyed,
with comforters on, roofed in Noaohian style, with
long faded coats of the time of Diaforius, and
boots turned up at the toes. They carried their
Bibles, whatever such might be, under their arms,
and when the meeting was over, there was an
indescribable effusion as of star-dust, and the
people came out with their eyes turned up, like
ducks at thunder.
# * *
Mrs. Besanfc has been lecturing on, among
other subjects, Charles Bradlaugh. Charles, with
the mystic lady, is like the other Charles with Mr.
Dick —she can’t keep him out of the memorial.
She quotes him all the time, and explained their
friendship by saying that it was but a renewal of
friendships in other and previous existences. At
their first meeting they both felt a bond of intense
sympathy, which led them to believe each had
found something which had been a long-felt want.
Here Edwin, who had taken his best girl to the
Bijou to improve her mind, gave a deep sigh, as if
he now understood why Angelina’s society was
beginning to bore him a trifle, since he might have
been “walking out” with her in dozens of pre-
vious lives.
• * •
Mrs. Besant’s idea of reincarnation plays
the deuce with official opinions on criminal punish-
ment. If one inherits a criminal’s soul and that
criminal soul is passed on after death, a judicial
hanging is simoly wasted, since it kills the body
Photo. by Rockwood] [Broadway, N.T.
Miss EDITH COURTENAY (of the “Morocco Bound” Co. at
Sydney Lyceum).
merely and liberates a bloodthirsty spirit that at
once sets to work to hunt up another suitable
body to aid in the perfection of its diabolical
schemes. If there is anything in this, it is mad-
ness to kill murderers and such like; the only
wise plan is to lock them up, and take excellent
care of them, so that they may live as long as
possible. Will some Mahatma tell us what body
own’s Deeming’s soul now ?
* * *
John Douglas is of opinion that the
fatality among Northern divers is largely due to
the speed at which they are lowered and raised
again. It is imperatively necessary that men
should be raised and lowered slowly. Douglas
asserts, however, that men are plunged almost
instantaneously to the bottom of 25-fathom water
and hauled up again just as rapidly. The remedies
applied to those who succumb are truly heroic.
They are often scalded, like a slaughtered pig,
until large blisters rise on the skin, and make
them regret, on recovery, that they didn’t die
outright.
* • «
One of the Speight v. Syme jurymen died
and was buried, the other day, and thereby hangs
a tale. The funeral - procession to Melbourne
cemetery included a cab containing George Moir
and Gordon Stewart, legal managers representing
Syme v. Speight, respectively. It was touching
to observe the manner in which these friendly
rivals stuck to each other upon the melancholy
occasion, each of them being instructed, no
doubt, nob to let the other fellow’s grief loom
larger than his own woe in the eyes of the eleven
surviving jurymen, who were also, of course,
assisting at the interment. No sooner did Moir
(on behalf of the reconstructed firm of Giilott and
Bates) shed a silent Symey tear than G. Stewart
(for Malleson, England, and Stewart) made a
point of wiping his Speighty eye. If Stewart
sighed, Moir sighed, and if Moir’s sigh was
unfairly profound Stewart promptly got level by
passing a hand across his brow. In the matter of
wreaths it is supposed that the contending firms
came to an amicable agreement to give duplicate
orders at the same florists’ shops.

The ex-Age reporter, Herbert Low, who
came at the last minute into the Speight-Sjme
case and bore witness against his former boss, has

served one very useful purpose. He is the key-

Williams on Low : “Why was he not called at
the last trial ? He says because he was then in
the employment of the Age.” The explanation
astounds justice Williams. To his clear, legal
mind it seems incredible that Speight should
shrink from asking one of Syme’s employes to
trip gaily into the witness-box and make a state-
ment against the Age’s honesty. Why wasn’t he
called at the first trial! Why wasn’t Syme
called as a witness for Speight, or Speight called
on behalf of Syme ? And why wasn’t the aunt of
Judge Williams his beloved uncle?
• • •
The latest rumour from Rue Australia is
that sundry members of Lane’s own colony are
suffering from elephantiasis, a weird and horrible
complaint in which the leg swells up to enormous
dimensions, and the skin becomes thick and rugged
like the hide of a patriarchal alligator. Elephan-
tiasis is vaguely supposed to be a sort of deceased
wife’s sister to leprosy; it is a disease of slow
development, and the patient may drag his enor-
mous members about for years before anything
further of a definite nature takes place.
# * *
A prospective Rue Australian, who paid
.£BO into the funds and then decided that the
Paraguayan paradise wasn’t up to the mark,
has sued the Sydney trustee, for the return
of his money, and got a verdict for £7O
and costs. This may possibly encourage some
more of the pioneers to take similar proceedings,
and may lead to awkward consequences. Mean-
while, Mrs. Lane, wife of the Dictator, is under-
stood to be running a boarding-house in Sydney,
and has no present intention of going back. From
which it would appear that she, at all events, has
had enough of Paraguay for the time being. The
lady’s discontent is based largely on the fact that
she wants a house of her own, “with a fence
round it, too,” so that the rude, uncultured, harsh-
voiced she-immigrant from the West can’t come
in and talk when she isn’t wanted. One of
the first results of true mateship was that this
freckled, hard-faced person insisted, in a spirit of
equality, upon coming in and conversing for hours
in her limited bush vocabulary, and telling
strident, pointless stories about fencing, and tank-
sinking, and swagmen, till life became a misery
and a nightmare. Yet the spirit of mateship
made it impossible to turn her out, and the
Dictator’s poor little wife was well-nigh talked to
death, and the fencing and tank-sinking yarns
will ring in her ears for the rest of her days.
« * *
Emotional meetings for religious (and
other) purposes still boom in Melb. Every few
nights the Town Hall is besieged by a pack of
people with hymn-books. Last Friday the
Christian Endeavourers commenced their endea-
vouring at 6 p.m.—9o min. before commencing-
time. On the steps one crowd had banners, and
warbled to Swanaton-st. amid marvel ment. At
the close of the meeting the lads and lasses hied
them home, some in furniture vans, singing the
songs of the sensuous Sankey all in the moonlight
fair. Yum, yum!

In the case of Richard Salomon and wife
against N.S.W. Railway Commissioners, in which
£5OOO damages were claimed for the loss of one
leg and the foot of another by the female plaintiff
in a railway-accident, the intelligent jury returned
with a verdict of £l2OO for the wife and £lOO for
her husband. It makes you think hard.

The authorities of Sydney Benevolent
Society require a lady collector, but stipulate that
she must be of “ good presence. ” Here again
crops up the worldly wisdom which in the pro-
prietors of gin-palaces and beer-barracks is de-
nounced as burnable blackguardism.

At present there are five men in the
Debtors’ Ward at Darlinghurst prison under com-
mittal for contempt in disobeying Divorce Court
decrees. Strangely enough, four of these prisoners’
wives were born in Windsor, N.S.W. Matri-
monially-inclined young men should paste this in
their hats.
The Society of Woman Journalists, Lon-
don, says that it will admit men to its club when
men’s clubs are thrown open to women. That
will be never, for a man’s club is an institution
deliberately established for the purpose of getting
away from women. However, a very short experi-
ence of women’s clubs shows that they are by no
means designed to afford facilities for keeping
away from men. Rather are they man traps.
Look, for instance, at the Austral Salon, which,
from an outside view, always recalls a lot of fox-
terriers crowding round a tenanted rat-trap. The
rat-trap, of course, is the club, the time is the
Salon reception-day, and the imprisoned man is
the literary or other lion of the hour, to whom
homage is being paid.

The Argus cablegram, announcing the
execution, a few days ago, of a homicidal French
priest for the murder of a clerical superior, wound
up with the remark : “ The conviction of a priest
on a capital charge is a very rare occurrence in
France.” This is one of these blessed items of
knowledge which make us feel the value of the
dear old cable,

At Rockhampton (Q.) one of the Salvation officers
is a lady, well-favored and somewhat eloquent.

is eloquent in Chinese. And John rolls up from

gazes on the fair missionary, rolls his eyes from
side to side, and gently murmurs to himself
“Welly ni’.”

• • •
Amongst recent arrivals at Cue is our
friend of yore Carr-Boyd, who has been exploring
between there and Coolgardie. Somewhere “ out
back ” he found the body of a well-known local
prospector, William Rutter. Which recalls that
the first time the writer met “ Potjostler ” was on
the Diamantina, about 14 years ago. He had
been sent out by the Q. Govt, to find the bodies of
the brothers Prout, who were supposed to have
perished further West. Of course he found them
—anyhow, he had a bag of bones with him and
wore a half-sovereign on his watch-chain, with
some scratches on it, which he was sure were the
marks of dingoes’ teeth.
