A very notable Sydney man, who indulges
in spasmodic “ bursts,” when on one jamboree
flung away his false-teeth overnight and had to
waste four hours next morning with a shovel and
a rake to find them. On another occasion his
doctor found him standing on his head, with his
head in a hole in the ground, which he had himself
just dug. “What were you doing?” asked the
doctor, when he had up-ended him. “ Trying to
bury ray brains ; they’re no use to me now.”
# * *
A Victorian youth named Oockroft is in
custody charged with murdering his step-sister.
He wanted to marry her, and when she refused
ho is alleged to have stunned her with a stick and
then sawn her head partly off. The front name
of the accused is Elijah. Any father who christens
his son Elijah or Jedediah sends him forth to run
in life’s race under a 481 b. handicap.

Cape Colony has now, as the price of
“cheap and reliable ” nigger-labor, 600 lepers on
Booben Island leper-station, and is daily adding
to this gruesome collection of rotting humanity.
Two medical attendants are in charge of the
station. Two doctors to this mass of corruption,
600 strong! When the lepers numbered but 60
there were two medicos j and even then com-
plaints were heard of the insufficiency.

Apropos of cheap vegetables. A man
sold Is. fid. worth at a Sydney suburban house.
Later, another offered a similar amount of stuff for
9d. No. 1 was asked next time why he sold so
much higher than others? “Oh ! they steals ’em,
mum,” was the reply—“ steals ’em from our gar-
dings.” This is like the broom-sellers’ yarn in one
of Lytton’s novels. A man who stole the handles
and stole the fibre and stole the twine to make up
his brooms still found he was undersold by a
rival. So he went despairingly and asked him
how it was done. “Oh,” said the rival, “I steals
mv brooms ready -made !”

The other day, at Circular Quay, Sydney,
a stout, pleasant-looking, middle-aged lady with a
straw hat on her head and pinned to her back
hair, an umbrella in one hand and a purse in the
other, made a jump for a departing North Shore
boat, and fell overboard. It was fully 40seo.
before she came up again, and meanwhile the
steamer put back, and two dozen men lay down
flat on the edge of the wharf and grabbed ineffeo-
tically at the green waves, and there was a rush
with life-buoys, ropes, and boat-hooks, and two
ladies went into hysterics. Then the straw hat
appeared again, and the stout lady was seized by
every part of her frock at once, and landed safely
on^the 1 wharf. She came up smiling, just aB she
had gone down, with her umbrella in one hand
and her purse in the other, and after she had
shaken the water out of her dress and thankedJier
rescuers she got on board and went her way. Thk
Bulletin reckons that woman should have a vote,
whoever else goes without.

Dear Bulletin, —In thinking over the
criticism of the late lamented Talmage on the
theory of the evolutionists, I was reminded that
most of the unco’ guid who flout the glonfied-
monkey theory are men who shave their upper
lips, and let a (monkey) beard grow all round the
outskirts of their features. Even a tolerably
good-looking man will look like a monkey under
the circumstances; and to hear one of these gentle-
men raving about the descent of man from angels
is most consoling. Why do certain sects attach a
pious significance to the wearing of the shaven
upper lip and an “ atrocious coal-heavers collar
throat-beard? Every Brigham Young you meet
in life wears either a monkey beard or a shaven
upper lip.—S.S.

LOST LIGHT.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
In starless night I heeded not
The utter blank above ;
I dree’d a lonely, changeless lot,
Untouched nor cheered by Love,
A meteor shot across the sky—
Brief blaze of dazzling light—
And evermore aware was I
How dreary was the night.
So, life was blank before you crossed
Its aimless, cheerless ways ;
Then Love flashed—star-like—and was lost,
Save mem’ry of its rays.
Had heart ne’er thrilled with love for you,
Nor sight the meteor seen.
Night’s dreariness I never knew,
Nor—what life might have been.
Warrego.
THE BREAKER.
THE RULING PASSION.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
** Is it a bargain—yes, or no ?”
Madge tossed her head and did not speak—
The blush that ought to come and go
She could not summon to her cheek.
His wooing was so rough and plain,
Unsoftened by one fond caress ;
A hand to give, a wife to gain—-
How like a man of business I
When “ No ” was almost on her tongue
Fair cousin Amy met her eye :
A forward creature, plump and young,
Who would not make the same reply.
And since, by nature’s simple law,
Madge hated Amy more or less,
She took her suitor’s honest paw
And closed the bargain with a “ Yes."
Melb.

W. J. Hegarty : Letter for you at Richmond
P.O Renunciation : Verse quite unsuitable for 8.,
which counts “self-development higher than self-sacri-
fice,” and tries to teach its readers to base their morality
on utility tested by consolidated race-experience rather
than on unproven myth interpreted hy dubious dogma.
You strike not “ the human,” but the anti-human “ key-
note.” The natural man doesn’t want to renounce any-
thing. His appetites are all good and desirable, and need
only self-control in the light of self-knowledge

The Bulletin does not wish to be “on file at the local
Athenasum.” The Bulletin wants to sell as many copies
and to have as few free readers as possible, and it will be
greatly obliged to any parson who succeeds in having it
tabooed at any public reading-rooms. In fact, any shep-
herd who can prove that he has been the means of
securing its exclusion from a reading-room will be sup-
plied free with a copy mailed in envelope at MS. rateso as
to be “free from public observation.”

lAMBS
The latest suggestion in re the shearing
difficulty;—“Let the squatters cross their sheep
with carpet-snakes and get them to shed their
wool in season.”
Wool and Produce Reports.
M'Burney, Wallis & Co. report: No marked improve-
ment in the Sydney Wool market may be looked for until
result of opening of the London September series of
sales is knbwn, This week has been one of inactivity,
but next will see busy operations at our local auction
sales. A good forecast of the value of this season’s
clip may be taken from the result of the London opening,
ami we think it must be in the producers’ favour. We
have now almost daily to hand news of trade improve-
ment in England and on the Continent, and this, coupled
with the fact that American buyers are already on their
way to our city, must give an impetus to the Sydney wool
sales of the season upon the verge of which we now find
ourselves. Very large catalogues cannot yet be placed
before buyers owing to most clips being incomplete.
Rain in some parts, and shearing troubles in orhers, are
the chief causes of delay. We will hold our opening sales
early in October, when the full buying strength of the
world will be with us, and our constituents must, there-
fore, receive the best possible competition for the wool.
Sydney, 14th Sept., 1894.
The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Com-
pany, Limited, report : —Wool : The market remains,
more or h ss, in a state of expectancy, and the volume of
business transacted during the week has not been large.
Sellers are indisposid to part freely with their stocks
until something definite is known as to the actual condi-
tion of affairs in the manufacturing centres of Europe,
and are waiting to receive news of the opening of the
London wool-sales this week. Buyers, on the other hand,
have indicated a willingness to make commitments, and a
strong private inquiry has been witnessed. Prices show
no important all station and may be quoted firm. The
new dip is arriving more freely, and the condition of the
wools is, for the most part, satisfactory, the bulk of the
clip being apparently sound, well-grown and healthy.
The company will hold a sale of about 3000 bales of the
new season’s supply on October 8, and will, after that
date, continue their weekly auction sales on each Monday
throughout the season.
The N.S.W. solidarity M.’sP. put forward
two candidates for the Public Works Corrim it tee-
ship, the only paid committee of the House. It may
be remembered to the credit of the pioneer labor
Ms.P. that they voluntarily refused such pick-
ings when they had a much better chance of
getting men on the committee than those who
were such severe critics and ante-electoral
moralists.
Ed. Bulletin, — Sydney Mail’s Deniliquin
correspondent says, “A. Landale, of Wandook, is
expected to start shearing with free laborers.
Now, ever since Mr. Landale had Wandook he has
shorn under a verbal agreement, and we started
shearing here last Friday under that agreement—
.£l a hundred, in and out —without any trouble,
and the men engaged months previously.—Yours,
Constancy.
The New Order is dead. It was a Sydney
weekly started for the purpose of getting its
brother Bill into Parliament, and having got its
brother Bill in, or failed to get its other brother Bill
in, its mission ended with N.S.W. general election.
It is surprising that the several varieties of brother
Bill who got in made no effort to save its attenu-
ated existence. But, doubtless, they judged that
brother Bill out was a different being to Brother
Bill, M.P., and acted accordingly.
The most remarkable feature of the N.S.
Wales Parliamentary Public Works Committee
was the downfall of that energetic placeman,
O’Sulli van, the famous keystone of the Democratic
arch, who was badly left. The O’Sullivan now
explains that he didn’t want the billet, that he
wouldn’t have accepted it if elected, and that he
told his friends and supporters not to vote tor
him. In fact, if the O’Sullivan had been elected,
he would have been the most infuriated keystone
in all Australia.
Every Irish member of Q. Parliament
who has taken an active part in Home Rule
leagues voted like a machine for Coercion in
Queensland. Duffy, member for Bundaberg, was
especially prominent in collecting money for con-
gratulatory cablegrams to the Irish party in the
House of Commons ; and Willie O’Connell, mem-
ber for Musgrave, and another coercionist, is
nephew of the titled Maurice O’Connell, first
president of the Q. Legislative Council, and a
relative of the great Irish tribune.
Sleath, M.P., according to Sydney D.T.,
made, “for him,” a temperate speech on the
shearing difficulty. This implies a certain sense
of disappointment on the part of the D.T. Long
ago they had editorially settled it that Sleath was
a mixture of a pirate, a buccaneer and an insur-
rectionist. It is very annoying that he should
turn out to be able to make “on the whole, for
him, a temperate speech.” Probably this admis-
sion was wrung from the paper by the contrast
between the moderation of Sleath and the wild
insurrectionary ravings of the Upper House the
night before on the same subject.
Premier Patterson waves the last disinte-
grated remnant of his vaunted spire in the politi-
cal atmosphere and announces that there are
going to be no shearing-troubles in Victoria while
he is on top, Patterson isn’t going to allow any
shearing troubles, and he has ordered all dis-
satisfaction to be abolished, and disputes are
strictly prohibited. He has also instructed
that “everything shall be held in readiness’’
—the exact mature of the thing that is in
readiness is left to the imagination. The
Bulletin respectfully asks Patterson why he
makes an ass of himself in this manner. The
shearing-trouble hasn’t reached Victoria yet, and
the man who tries to advertise himself by shaking
his little fist at an absent danger is just the same
kind of hero as the one who stands in front of an
empty cannon and announces that he will die at
his post if necessary.
A N.S.W. strike chapter, price £3OO.
The P.U. engaged an English gentleman in Mel-
bourne to convey 54 free shearers from there to
Kilfera and other sheds beyond Booligal, N.S. W.
At Junee the E.G. chartered a special tram to
take them to Hay. Whilst changing trains three
free men became homesick and disappeared. Hay
was reached at midnight, where they were met by
400 wild unionists, all a-roaring and a-bellowing
like bulls. As they relieved the E.G. of his
revolver, they assured him of their undying love
for Lawnorder; they also mentioned that they
were loyal to the Queen. 20 free men succumbed
here to a severe attack of unionism and joined the
gang a-bellowing, &c.; the E.G. got the remainder
away in Cobb’s coaches. At 13-uiile Gate, whilst
changing horses, 12 more felt a feeling of aoTdarity
coming over them, and refused to go further. At
Booligal another vast horde of wild unionists
a-bellowing and a-roaring ; more Solidarity, with
the result that the. remaining 19 went over m a
body to the enemy. The E.G., to use his own
words, was left “all alone like a pelican in the
wilderness,” when five hairy persons, with their
pockets and shirts bulged out with unionist litera-
ture, insisted on his joining the union. The poor
English gentleman almost wept; and then the
hardened villains tried to give him in charge for
riotous behavior. Total cost of trip, £3OO.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The much-tipped and heavily-backed Nobleman
made a poor show in A. J.O. Derby. After fighting for his
head and pulling double from the start, he died away
when the straight was entered. At the distance the race
looked a one-horse show for Bonnie Scotland, hut Martin
Gallagher brought the unthought-of Chesterman with
such a rush that the pair were level a few strides from
home. Bonnie Scotland, however, battled it out bravely
and got his head in front on the post. Cobbitty was
third, some eight lengths away. Time, 2min. 44sec.
Albecore started favorite at 6 to 1 for Epsom
Handicap, at Randwick, on Saturday, but Volney, at 14
to 1, was as well backed as anything in the race. Volney
held it until passing the distance, when Uabba came with
a determined run, and passing Kelso’s gelding won by
half-a-length, Euroka running into a place. Mr. Dyke
must have had a nice win, as his colt was at 100’s to 3.
The manner in which Brockleigh won the
Spring Stakes showed him to be in great fettle. Once in
the home stretch, Paris and Brockleigh had the first set-
to, but Brockleigh soon dispersed of the pony’s preten-
tions, and then Ruenalf unavailingly put in his claim.
Paris ran third.
King Orry started favorite for the Trial Stakes,
but succumbed to the Caulfield Cup horse, Isaac, who
beat him without an effort, Blarneystone running third.
* « * •
Bonnie Scotland, winner of the A. J.O. Derby,
is the first Maorilander that has got home In that race
since Martini-Henry’s year.
• • • •
The starting-machine was used at Randwick in
the Derby and Spring Stakes—and proved a great success.

fines will soon be ancient history.

ahead. Ths other day at Epsom, Melb., as the nags were
drawn up before the light bamboo barrier, one jockey

Five horses down, one killed, a man’s jaw and
a collar-bone broken—the list of accidents in the Williams-
town (Vic.) Steeplechase on Saturday. Allowing the Hon.
Bill Long to be light enough to ride, the writer wonders
what his price would be to ride in a steeplechase.

Saracen, who will probably come over for the
Caulfield Oup, is older than his brothers, Loyalty and
Bonnie Scotland, but has not their wear-and-tear look.
Maorilanders can’t stand him, but the truth is they know
very little about him. His rumored Caulfield mission has
an element of significance about it.
• * * *
The Sydney correspondent of a Melb. sporting
paper, wiring a tip for the Epsom Handicap, said
“Acmena would be a certainty but for her sex.” No
prophet can hope to find a sounder excuse than this for
the defeat of a “ dead bird.”
• * * •
Throstle, a little-known filly, was good enough
to take the Doncaster St. Leger from colts that ran first
and second in the Derby. The autumn in England is so
clearly the season for mares that there is no excuse now
for the punter who lays odds upon a Leger colt as long as
one good-looking lady remains in the race. Probably
Throstle bad improved two stone between June and Sep-
tember, yet, knowing that these reversals of “form ” are
according to nature and precedent, the public accept
them as surprises when they come. Once more The
Bulletin wants to know why the female horse of Aus-
tralia is so consistently inferior to the male, and why she
so rarely shows a marked improvement upon her summer
form when the leaves begin to fall?

Mr. 0. H. Jones, of Maoriland, Australasian 10
miles cycling champion, whose portrait is printed in this
week’s Bulletin, was born in London in 1872, and arrived
in Maoriland in *77. He has only been two years racing,
has scored many good wins, and favors the 10-mile dis-
tance.

• * * *
George Giffen was, after all, not so much to
blame, if rumor be true, in the trouble which caused him
to leave Norwood (S.A.) cricket-club, for which he hag
played for 14 years. Giffen is much incensed because the
club elected Blinman captain, and did not even put the
champion on the match-committee. Giffen’s secession
will, however, do much for Adelaide club cricket, as he
and Lyons will be indifferent teams.
• • • •
The Essendon club having now won the Vic.
premiership four years in succession, it will be to their
own advantage to go off a bit next year, as their monoto-
nous success is killing all public interest in the game. A
couple of years back, when South Melbourne, Carlton,
Geelong, and a couple of other clubs all had a chance, a
crowd of 30,000 was nothing unusual, but now people
know the result is a foregone conclusion, and all but
Eisendon supporters stay away.
* ♦ » •
Essendon’s recent defeat by Geelong is evidently
due to Thurgood’s having failed to notch his usual
number of goals. “The demon” had one shot at about
39vds., bub failed to score. Added to this the Qeelongese
played it pretty low down on the visitors by kicking the
ball out of bounds into the crowd, who refused to return
it, thus delaving the play.

Next year Queensland has to make provision for
paying off £766,000 of the old 6 per cent, loan of 1870, and
£700,000 of 4 per cent. Treasury bills. Last time aQ.
loan of any magnitude fell due the province paid it by rush-
ing through, at the last moment, a new per cent, loan
at about 88—in ocher words, it made a dead loss of nearly
£300,000 on the transaction, with the soulful spectacle of
M'llwraith crawling on his bands and his vast
freckled knees to the British money-lender thrown
in. If the Nelson Government has any financial
capacity, it will start operations at least six months
in advance by offering 3£ per cent, interminable deben-
tures at par in exchange (or the old securities, thus
doing away witfi the big flotation expenses. A lot of
holders will doubtless accept, and, for the rest, Queens-
land may possibly manage to pay them off without.serious
loss, and if it can’t do that it may perhaps end by ex-
plaining to them that it is 3£ per cent or nothing. There
is £22,600 a year to be saved by converting into Si per
cents in this manner, and there is a big sum to be lost by
converting in the old “ heaven-born ” M'llwraithian style.

• • • •
The London Times on Ananias, the auditor :
“Next to the great director swindle, the most cruel trick
practised on the poor is the auditor swindle. We have
recently had the repeated spectacle of men who call them-
selves chartered accountants avowing that their pre-
tended audit of accounts is nothing more than a
mechanical checking of entries, which does not in ihe
remotest manner touch the question of a company’s sol-
vency or honesty. Chartered accountants are persons
who hold their heads high. They have an institute,
fellowship of which is supposed by the public to be a
guarantee for thoroughness in the work of verifying a
balance-sheet. But the public cannot too speedily under-
stand that the fellowship conveys no assurance of the
kind, and that an auditor’s certificate is absolutely
worthless for any purpose that an investor need
consider. There may be, and probably are, audits
which really afford an independent corroboration
of the statements in a balance-sheet. But the public
have no means of knowing whether an audit means this
or merely a clerical comparison of a set of figures with
the published copy as them. Indeed, so long as auditors
are appointed by the persons they are supposed to check,
and have no independent power to investigate or disallow
improper transactions, it does not seem reasonable to
expect their work to be of any real value."
Tliis is exactly what The Bulletin has been
saying over and over again for years past. The auditor
simply guarantees that the balance-sheet is a full and fair
statement of the accounts as shown by the books. If the
books are right, then the balance-sheet is right; if the
books are wrong, then the balance-sheet is wrong also.
And as this is exactly how things would be if there
were no auditors, these gentlemen are *of very
little visible use to anyone except themselves.
Now and then, however, the auditor is so exceed-
ingly careful to state that he only guarantees the
balance-sheet to be a true statement of the affairs “ as
shown by the company’s books,” and so unusually par-
ticular to avoid all responsibility for anything that lies
outside the books, that the public gets a glimpse of the
fact that the books are hopelessly cronk, and that the
auditor is aware of the fact, and is carefully safeguarding
himself from the gaol which is to come. But it is only
when he begins to stand from under in this fashion that
the public learns anything. And even then it doesn't
learn much.
• • * •
It is not always a good move to save your biz
from creditors by making it into a limited co. A certain
Northern printer hit on a literary “enterprise,” and after
a hard struggle made a fair success thereof. On a few of
Ids long-suffering creditors “tumbling” to the fact that
the typo was likely to strike oil at last they began to press
for *• a bit of the old." Whereupon the over-prudent one
promptly ran the concern into a limited liability co., in
which he gave a lot of literary men of straw fully-paid
shares, amounting in the aggregate to twice the number
held by himself. At the time he gave the shares away, a
lot of money was owed to the concern by agents, and
he himself purposely allowed the co. to run into his
debt some hundreds, so as to lend color to the genuine-
ness of the sale of the affair. The riff-raff who held
the majority of the shares had a high old time for a
few months or so, but the promoter-printer could not get
paid, and threatened to sue the co., who promptly called
a meeting and, by sheer force of votes, decided to raise
wages all round 100 per cent, so as to prevent a divi-
dend and dispense with the promoter’s services as a
printer. The devil of it is that the promoter is waiting
a certain certificate, and ,can, therefore, do nothing to
enforce his claims.

Overheard outside a Southern Stock Exchange :
A : “ I think of going in for a few Qordelpum gold-mining
shares. Do you know anything about ’em?" B (a
notorious shark): “Yes. They are as good as gold."
A (who knows all about B): “Then you think I’ll be
right?” B: Yes. On my word of honor.” A: “ That
fettles HI” B: “All right! How many do.you want ?”
A : “None, old chap.”
• • • •
The greater part of the money for working the
diamond drill boring for coal at the Red Bluff, St. Kilda
(Melb.), is being found by a Spiritualistic lady of ample
fortune. A medium said the spooks told her there was
coal at the Bluff, and the fair one has backed the opinion
to the extent of some thousands. It keeps men employed,
at any rate, though no coal has yet been found.
• * • •
A London financial paper is painfully surprised
at not hearing anything more about “the astonishing
diamond mines at Bingara.” The Bulletin has to explain
that, owing to the wool season, freights to London have
gone up, and the “ tons of diamonds " are crowded out
till the slack season.
• • • •
Months back the writer read in an English
paper of the wonders in mining performed by Lord Percy
Douglas and the Hon. Carnegie, and a shaft 80ft. sunk,
and splendidly timbered by them unaided, was mentioned.
Since then the writer has seen the shaft, which is only 30ft.
deep and is not timbered at all, and, above all, he learned
that the noble lord and bis crew never got any farther
than the windlass.

In U.S.A. the general common law rule is that
mines below the surface pass to the grantee of the sur-
face. As a curious exception, gold and silver mining is
reserved in the State of Pennsylvania to the Penn family.
This is a hint to the Forrest family, of Westralia.

The latest style of company-promoting in Mel-
bourne is to allot your shares to anybody who seems
solvent enough to pay for them, and then write to the
Photo. by Freeman] (George-st., Sydney.
CHARLES HENRY JONES Christchurch M.L. 10-Mile Bicycle-
Champlon of Australasia).
allottee demanding, in a piratical fashion, that the money
shall be sent along by return post. The man who is
running the enterprise does his business by circular, as
follows: —
“Dear Sir,—Several important leases adjoining the
mine have been applied for.
The existing railway to passes through one of
the blocks.
I am forming the Company (No Liability), and
have allotted you one promoter’s share of £lO.
To cover expenses, such as deposit to Warden and
survey fees, the first and only levy of £1 is made, which
please remit by return post. The share will then be
issued fully paid up to £lO.
The above-mentioned expenses will not exceed £4O,
and the whole of the balance subscribed will go to the
credit of the company.—Yours faithfully.
This business ought to pay. The ohanoe of giving a
complete stranger 20s. for a £lO promoters' share with
his own sole assurance that several important leases have
been taken up somewhere or other, and with no informa-
tion as to who takes charge of the money, or whether
there are 40 or a million shares in the co., or what the
prospects of the mine amount to, or anything else, is a
Joy which comes to most men only onoe in a lifetime.

Desperate attempts are being made to meet the
coming Coolgardie boom in London. One of the greediest
is that described in the prospectus of the W.A. Crushing
Syndicate, capital £lO,OOO in 1000 £lO shares, of which
the promoters magnanimously agree to allow the con-
tributing shares a preferential 10 per cent, div., but
£lO,OOO a year profit is anticipated from the working of a
20-head battery. It is estimated that the battery, bought
in England and erected at Coolgardie, will only cost
£3OOO. A complete 20-head battery will cost that in any
part of the world, and the cost would, at the very least,
be £6OOO at Coolgardie. £lO,OOO a year out of a 20-head
battery—more than £1 a ton profit by working day
and night 1 Not a word about absence of water I

It is said that there is in England no man
more conversant with British History than Lord
Rosebery, the present Prime Minister. This is as
it should be, for no man can hope to make future
history without the light of the past to guide
him. This applies equally to social economy and
to polities, for history ever repeats itself, and
there is one instance where 40 years of constant
and unrivalled success is sufficient guarantee for
another 40 years. It need hardly be said the allu-
sion is to Priddy, the high-class hatter, whose fame
is world-wide.
The Art Society of N.S. Wales, in place
of its customary art-union with guinea tickets,
this year holds a “ tombola ” —which is an art-
union under a more obscure and mysterious name
—with tickets at 1/- each. A large number of
works of art will be distributed, the first-prize
being a painting valued at .£lO5. Tickets can be
obtained from the manager at the society’s rooms,
70 Pitt-street, Sydney, and also from almost all
stationers, music-sellers and booksellers through-
out the city.
Dear Bulletin, — “P.H.’s”
distinction between “ muscle ”
and “sinew*’ is an imaginary
one, as far as function is con-
cerned. The sole source of
animal mechanical force is the
contractile power of the muscles
proper, the sheath portions of
which—modified into what is
called sinew or tendon—serve
merely to transmit the power
over joints and into extremities
the action of which would be
impeded by such fibrous masses
as are required to originate the
energy. To say that a sinewy
man (nervous conditions being
equal) is stronger than a mus-
cular one is to say that the rope
is stronger than the windlass !
Physios.
Dear Bulletin, —“ P.H.”
complains that I “imply that
great muscular development de-
notes strength. ” Certainly Ido;
but it is not a question of quan-
tity, but quality. A working
bullock in good condition is
stronger than a fat one of the
same weight. He is also smaller,
and his muscular tissue is harder
and tougher, as “P.H.’’ would
find if he tried to eat some of
him. A 12-stohe navvy looks
smaller and slighter than a man
of the same weight who lives a
sedentary life, because he has
much less fat mixed with his
muscular tissue, and, though his
muscles may not be so bulky,
they are harder. Sinews are
simply the attachments which
join the ends of the muscles to
the bones. Of course, large
bones, which are really the levers
of the animal machine, are
essential to strength; so are
large and strong tendons and
muscles, which represent the
ropes and bands of the machine.
By their contraction and extension they produce
motion.— Unt.

“ How is it spelt?” 1.5.: “Shpelt, indeed 1 Why,
now, it’s D-o-oherty—y. Sure any fool could
shpell that.”
A STAMP MANIAC.
A stamp-collector advertised in Melbourne last
week that he was the possessor of a collection of
12,544 stamps, and wished to marry a lady who
was an ardent collector and the possessor of the
blue penny stamp of Mauritius, issued in 1847.
He received only one answer from a lady who
owned the coveted stamp; but, as a conditian to
her union with him, she required a proof that he
smoked nothing stronger than Frossard’s Cavour
Cigars, as every other kind affected her nerves,
and she stated that a man indulging in these mild
and fragrant weeds was giving such a sign of
sound judgment and economical habits that a
woman could safely trust herself to him. It must
be remarked here that Frossard’s Cavour Cigars
cost only Is. for a packet of 8, and, considering
the quality, this lady was quite correct in alluding
to their cheapness.
THE NEW TEA.
The vigorous and novel advertisements that have
been, and are still appearing, in the Sydney and
provincial papers, of “My Favorite” tea, have
opened the eyes of some to the possibilities of
advertising, and the very satisfactory report from
Messrs. D. Mitohell and Co., the enterprising
proprietors of this famous blend of tea, goes to
prove that, given a good article, backed up by
intelligent and liberal advertising, success is
pretty certain. It appears that about two years
ago Messrs. D. Mitchell and Co. plaoed on this
market a jam of surpassing quality, known as
“My Favorite ” jam. There were no end to the
different brands of jam then existing, and friends
of the firm prognosticated failure. Mr. Mitchell,
however, was determined to try the experiment,
and see whether a jam of the best possible quality
would not succeed when the inferior brands then
ruling were making, to use a nautical phrase,
“bad weather of it.’’ The almost phenomenal
gucoess of “My Favorite” jam led to a similar
experiment with “My Favorite” tea, and the
result, so far, has been beyond expectations. The
difficulty in getting good tea is well-known, but
it is a fact that few will oare to dispute, that when
onoe good tea is found its flavor varies and thereby
causes disappointment. The reason of this is
apparent; it is not that the quality is necessarily
lower in grade, but the blending is done by
those who are not experts at the business. Messrs.
D. Mitohell and Co. have in their employ experts
who not only blend the tea in the same proportion
but are also careful to blend teas ot the exact
quality and flavor required to produce what is now
known as “My Favorite ” Tea. There is no
doubt of the permanent success of this tea, for it is
most delicious in flavor and of exceptional quality
and strength. “My Favorite Tea ” is put up in
elegant air-tight tin caddies, got up to represent
japannedware, in lib., 51b., 101 b., 201 b. caddies
and half-cheats. It is sold throughout this pro-
vince by grooers and storekeepers, and is a per-
fectly pure, delicious and fragrant tea, and cer-
tain to be true to its striking name whenever it is
introduced into the home circle.*
HOW A LOT OF MONEY LEAKS OUT.
What does a man do when he finds a hole in th«
pocket where he carries his money ? Anybody
can answer that question. He has it sewed up, of
course, directly—and good and strong, too. I
suppose it is with you just as it is with me. When
I spend money, even foolishly, I oan tell where it
went, and maybe I’ve had some sort of pleasure
out of it. But Ido mortally hate to lose money;
lose it out and out, you know, and have no satis-
faction from it, or know how or when it left my
possession.
Well, now, let me show you the worst and biggest
hole any man ever had in his pocket; a hole
that lets the cash leak away like water through a
sieve, a hole that is the hardest in the world to
sew up. A short story will show it best.
“Drummore South Cottage,
“ Musselburgh, near Edinburgh,
“September 16th, 1891.
“ Gentlemen,—Up to 1885 I was always strong
and healthy. About this time I began to feel bad.
I was tired, languid, dull, and listless, and every-
thing was a burden to me. I had no desire for
company, and what had come over me I could not
make out. My tongue and mouth were dry, and
I had a deal of phlegm on my stomach. The
whites of my eyes next became discolored and my
skin was yellow. I had no appetite, and after
eating I had great pain at my chest and sides, also
across my stomach. After a time the pain settled
in mjr left side, and my heart would beat and
jump in a manner that alarmed me. By-and-by I
got so weak that I was not able to go about the
house, and I felt that I ought to be in bed. The
pains at my side and stomach became so bad that
I had to remove my clothing (everything seemed
so tight), and I used to press my stomach and
hold my sides to try and ease the pain. Getting
worse, I saw a doctor at Musselburgh, and was
under him for three months, but his medicine
gave me no relief. After this I went to a clever
doctor at Preston Pans who said I was suffering
from indigestion and dyspepsia. He sent to
London for some celebrated medicine which was
packed in small phials. This medicine seemed to
dissolve my food, and I felt easier for a time, but
I gained no strength or real benefit, and after per-
severing with his treatment for six months, I
gave it up and fell into my old state. I next
went to a doctor at Musselburgh, but all his
medicines did me no good. After this I saw
another doctor (that is a fourth doctor), but with
the same result; none of them gave me anything
that reached my complaint. I now lost all faith
in physic, for I had spent a deal of money and
taken so much medicine ‘ that I lost all my teeth
through it ’ and was no better for it. In great
misery I lingered on month after month, always
ailing, when in August of last year (1890), my
husband called at Mr. Jack’s Drug Stores, High-
street, Fisherrow, and told him what my condition
was. Mr. Jack gave him an account of the
wonderful cures he heard of from many of his
customers that had taken a medicine called
Seigel’s Syrup, and strongly recommended him to
bring me a bottle. He did so, and I commenced
taking the Syrup, and I found some relief from
the first bottle, and by the time I had taken four
bottles I was as well and strong as ever I was in
my life, and have since kept in good health. I tell
every one what Seigel’s Syrup has done for me.
* I never thought to get better again, and I con-
sider it has saved my life. I wish others to know
this, and if by publishing this statement it will be
the means of helping others, as it helped me, you
can use this letter as you like. Yours truly
(Signed) Jemima Watson.”
Look back to about the middle of the above
letter and again read what the writer says : “ I
had spent a deal of money for medicine.” Yes,
and money she could poorly afford to spare. Ill-
ness and the expense of illness is the great hole in
the pocket that I alluded to. It costs so much,
and what does it give us in return ? Pain, weari-
ness, and misery. There is another consideration
besides. When we are ill we not only have to
bear the increased outgo, but manage to meet
larger demands out of a decreased income. Our
candle is burning at both ends. “Yes,” you say,
“but how can we keep from falling ill ?” You
oannot always, but in view of the fact that most
illnesses arises from indigestion and dyspepsia, a
timely use of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup will
prevent it. A few shillings thus invested will
save pounds in money and perhaps months of
wretchedness. Think over the striking points in
Mrs. Watson’s excellent letter and you will think
the same.

THE LAST OF SIX.”
Price: One Shilling; per Post, Is. 3d.
“No. 154.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
The fat Dutch sentry challenged. A mere
matter of form—for it was a number— and a
number cannot give a countersign. Nevertheless
“ 154 ” —huge red figures on a blue cotton blouse
—had the outward semblance of a big-boned man,
well-featured and not old, but wild-beast-eyed
and toil-oppressed. The soldier levelled at the
number’s head; the number nodded and stood
fast. “ I came for that,” it said, and the sentry,
gaping, dropped his rifle to the “ready.” His
orders were explicit. “You will fire instantly
upon any convict coming, unaccompanied by an
official, within 20 metres of your post.”
But here was a convict within bayonet-thrust.
Jan Ruycker had already broken the regulations;
but still, perplexed by want of precedent, he
hesitated to erase for ever this audacious number
at its own request. For compromise, he lowered
his bayonet to the “ charge.”
“Yes—that way, if you will! ” the convict said,
indifferently. “And here.” He laid a gaunt
forefinger on the upper angle of the “4” that
made the units of his only designation on Padak
Island. But the sentry had known him by
another; in Ruycker’s brain the precision of the
action condensed like magic a misty recollection.
He dropped, together, his jaw and his rifle-butt.
“My God !” he said —“ Dr. Van Kloon ! ”
On the sodden sand the tw o men stood, secluded
by the blinding rain that shut out all save here and
there the ghostly shadow of a cocoa-palm, and,
down at the end of the sandspit, the jetty fading
into the dreary mist that hung upon the sullen
sea.
Through this white wetness came a four-oared
boat, the rowers cooped—like monstrous blue-red
parrots—in an iron cage that stopped a few feet
short of stem and stern. In the bows a green-
coated warder nursed his rifle; a superior official
—his verdure relieved by silver-lace—sat aft and
steered.
The two men facing each other beside the sentry-
box saw nothing of the boat—heard nothing, till
the hollow thump of footsteps on the jetty made
them start —the soldier towards “attention” —the
convict like a bullock to the whip. Habit, this,
with both ; but the soldier’s plight, if thus caught
convict-parleying, was no whit better than the
convict’s own, and the sudden realisation of the
fact spurred Ruycker’s jog-trot wits into unwonted
gallop. He snatched at the convict’s coarse blue
sleeve. “ Behind the box !” he gasped.
The other shook his shoulders. “What use?”
he said. “The new sentry will put a bullet
through me, if you won’t.”
“ It’s not the relief, I tell you —it’s the civil-
controller.”
With a spring the convict was behind the box,
all but his head.
“ Zevenbergen ? ” he asked, with a savage glad-
ness in his face.
“ Ay! curse him! he’s always on the prowl.
Keep close I ” and Ruycker faced about and
challenged.
The new-comer answered, and came up till he
towered close above the sentry, for civil-controller
Zevenbergen was a very tall man, whose jolly red
face gave a most emphatic lie to his disposition.
“All well?” he asked, flinging back his long
green overcoat, and appearing to look everywhere
but at the sentry.
“ All well, sir 1 ”
“You lie, you Walloon scoundrel I 1 see it in
your mutton-face! ”
Now Private Jan Ruycker was no Walloon.
He was as good a Hollander as Zevenbergen, and
thus, when from behind the sentry-box a heavy
blue something hurled itself suddenly forth upon
the green official, the soldier’s anger held his hand
for a moment. But that moment was enough!
The convict’s iron-bound shoe had caught the
fallen controller tremendously twice beneath the
fat red jaw, and to all appearance it was hanging
for ever.
With a contented laugh the convict sat down
upon the wet sand. He had finished his tyrant!—
what mattered the rest ? But the soldier’s stimu-
lated mind embraced the situation in a flash. To
linger here was death —and, in any case, he was
weary of a lot but little easier than a convict’s.
He dragged the apathetic killer to his feet.
“ Rouse up !” he said. “ The boat’s below —let
us get away from hell!”
“From hell!” the other echoed, and was half-
way to the jetty before a sudden recollection
pulled him up. “ There are warders in her,” he
whispered. “Armed!”
The cold light of desperation came into the
soldier’s eyes as he changed the cartridge in his
wet rifle. “There’s only one,” he answered,
“ and ” —a slap on the breech said the rest.
Van Kloon thrust him backwards. “Not so.
No murder. That”—and be pointed—“was an
executionl Leave this to me.”
Beside the shell-encrusted piles the cage-boat
rose and fell, fended-off, through the bars, by the
four dejected wretches that watched with envious
eyes the glow of the warder’s cheroot. Malays
and murderers all —there wasn’t one of them who
wouldn’t cheerfully have slit the official’s wind-
pipe for the sake of that little roll of tobacco—the
knowledge of which fact but gave the weed a
keener zest to the hard-eyed man that peered
through the pallid mist for the coming of his
chief.
At last a form just shaped itself from out the
veil of vapour—stopped at that—waved above its
head a shadowy gun. This was curious! —and, as
the warder stood up to look, a long blue arm
swooped swiftly over the outer gunwale, caught
him by the ankle, and flung him heavily over
between boat and jetty. When he got his head
up again he found himself looking up the barrel
of his own rifle, and had no difficulty in under-
standing that for him the jetty was now much
healthier than the boat. Upon the steps the
soldier passed him with a curse—there was a jab-
bering of the caged convicts—a word or two from
Van Kloon —then straight out into the mist went
the boat, as fast as oars could drive her.
• • •
A certain five numbers, having levanted,
became for the nonce men again, with names and
histories —to be looked up in the prison-records,
and long did old Vander. Eyde twist his white
moustaches over an entry which set forth, in sub-
stance, that Julius Van Kloon, chemist and
native of Rotterdam, had on a certain date been
sentenced at Batavia to ten years’ imprisonment
for supplying, contrary to law, certain poisons to
an insurgent Javani chief.
The military-controller turned round to his
secretary. “ That was the Sidi business, wasn’t
it? Poisoned the water for our troops, the old
villain did ! But there’s something in the matter
that puts me in mind of the valuable colleague I
am about, alas I to lose.”
The ancient warrior’s face belied his tone, and
the secretary ventured on a decorous smile. “ The
connection is this, Excellency. Van Kloon was
convicted wholly on the evidence of his partner,
one Zevenbergen. ”
Colonel Van der Eyde banged his fist on the
desk. “ That’s it!—our esteemed controller’s son !
I remember there was a notion at the time that
Zevenbergen had a hand in it himself !”
The secretary looked discreetly to the doors and
windows. He knew —who better? —that prison-
officers are ex-officio eavesdroppers, and the civil-
controller might recover, after all. Then, with
bated breath, “ There are those who say still,
Excellency, that the job was wholly Zevenbergen’s;
and that his father knows it.”
The colonel, with a mighty military oath,
declared that he fully believed it, and that, if the
Government wanted to catch that poor devil of a
Van Kloon, the Government might do it, for he,
Van der Eyde, was essentially bedevilled if he’d
try.
* « •
The Javanese of Moodan were not yet
“ protected.” So little, indeed, were they in love
with the paternal Hollander that when, into one
of their coast villages there crawled a wretched
creature in a tattered uniform, the people would
have krissed him on the spot, but that close
behind him staggered other starvelings in another
livery they recognised. Prison-men had reached
the Moodan coast before—bitter enemies of the
Dutchman and his works —and this soldier, doubt-
less, was of their mind. So it was welcome,
instead of murder!
• « •
When at last, in the Batavian papers, it
was announced that the Moodanese had gone
under, it was mentioned with much flourishing of
trumpets that in the midst of this ferocious people
two hardy Hollanders had dwelt for years. “ Our
gallant troops,” quoth editorial rigmarole, “were
thunderstruck to receive in the heart of hostile
territory a welcome from these adventurous
pioneers !”
The “pioneers”—a run-away convict and a
deserter—kept their own counsel.
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.

Rev. E. H. Scott, of Kangaroo Flat, Vic.,
writes to Melb. papers, in a mental perspiration,
affirming that local M.’sP. must be all that the
Rev, Edgar painted them, inasmuch as they are
responsible for the existing divorce-law, “a law
that legalises adultery, and teaches people to sin
under the shelter of legal enactment.” Yes, and
the blessed clergy are a very bad lot, in that they
teach people that they can have no end of a flighty
time here below, so long as they preserve their
presence of mind sufficiently to square up at the
eleventh hour.
H. H. Champion declares that Socialistic
progress all over the world has been retarded by
the woeful maladministration of the State’s under-
takings in Victoria. All things considered, it
will be as well for Victoria not to start a State
bank until the present ruling forces have become
extinct. And after then—what ? Mrs. Besant
telling her first Melbourne audience that the
national character of the future would be evolved
from the thoughts of to-day was in effect saying
that Victoria would go from bad to worse, for
there doesn’t seem to be a noble thought left in
the country.
Percy Chirnside, deah boy, as a candi-
date for Grant, is another of those who would
put Victoria straight with a “ wealth ’’-tax,
instead of an income-tax. His unselfish spirit
yearns for a halfpenny, or even a penny, tax on
all ratable property, on the distinct understanding
that it would remain in force for one year only.
With a little persuasion Percy might even be got
to approve of a graduated-tax, starting at three-
pence on very small properties and working down
to a farthing on the very large ones.
Premier Braddon writes to Premier Reid
that “local matters, financial and political, have
very fully of late occupied Australian statesmen.”
Braddon, Patterson, Reid, Nelson; take them
from south to north, and then study the title
“statesman.” Take them all round Australasia,
and study it. Parkes once said publicly that he
hated the word, and afterwards, qualifying his
expression (and no doubt thinking of_ himself),
said that only men of pre-eminent public services
were entitled to be so described, and that the
only place it looked well in was a memoir.
At an English Court, recently, a publican
was fined £25 for adulterating beer by “unlaw-
fully mixing 151 b. weight of sugar with 567 gals. of
wort and beer to increase the gravity.” The plea
that the sugar was thrown in to make the stuff
more palatable didn’t count worth the talking.
Australia takes its guzzle more recklessly. It is a
public suspicion, whether founded or unfounded,
that a lot of “colonial beer” is a semi-fermented
decoction of sugar and hops, quite innocent of
malt, and having “grains of paradise ” and other
cheap but poisonous bitters thrown in as luxuries.
Candidates for S.A. Parliament have now
to file an account of election expenses immediately
after the poll. In that of one of the Labor-candi-
dates at the recent elections appeared the name of
a bank clerk as a contributor of .£3. A prominent
squatter and ex-M.L.C. called on the bank-
manager and asked that the clerk should be
warned or punished. The manager had the pluck
to say that he would not interfere, and the
squatter then threatened to report him to head
office. The organ of the S.A. Ass openly advo-
cates that this clerk and others similarly placed
be taught a lesson, and no doubt he will suffer
sooner or later for daring to back Ins political
opinion.
On the night of the division in the Vic-
torian Assembly which stranded the Patterson
Ministry, Shiels, that man of dramatic entrances
and exits, made the most dramatic entrance of his
life. M'Lellan, on behalf of the Government,
was pointing out all the horrors that would come
if the voluble ex-premier were brought back to
office. “ When he does come,” said M.L., whirl-
ing his long arms round seventeen times or more
—“ When he does enter,” and the excited member
flapped his limbs desperately in the air, “ he will
enter like a destroying angel—like a destroying
angel, Mr. Speaker.” Then there was a yell of
laughter, for Shiels was just entering, with a
gentle, heavenly smile on his countenance, and
that mild, upward expression that betokens the
saint whose thoughts are fixed on things above.

“Hamlet" at Bunyip Hill .
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
Fifteen years ago Bunyip Hill was a lively
mining township. It had sprung up unexpectedly
in the leafy wilderness, immediately after the dis-
covery of rich alluvial on the flat and a good reef
in the hill. For about four years the place
revelled in prosperity, and every pocket was
heavy. Then yields began to slow down and local
public sentiment took a more sober tone. The
alluvial “cut out,” and the reefing drifted into
the hands of tributors. Every departing Cobb’s
coach took away a band of miners to seek fresh
fields and diggings new, little richer for all the
gold that had passed through their hands ; and at
last the township sank into stagnation and decay.
In its heyday, Bunyip Hill possessed a dramatic
club named “The Thespian Amusers,” but
facetiously reohristened “The Tub Thumpers”
or “The Thirsty Brigade.” The moving spirit,
business-manager, stage-carpenter, scene-painter,
costumier, and mechanic, was Mr. William Harper
—for convenience, “Glib Bill.” William had a taste
for the stage. At onetime in his early career he
had appeared successfully as the leading feature
of some mythological animal in a London panto-
mime, and subsequently he won honorable mention
from the Bungbong Express for his inimitable
rendering of the Chinese character-song, “ Hi,
yah, yah ; sing, sing, sing.”
Bill, in his clayey “ moles,” yellow flannel, and
water-tights, was manager and sole proprietor of
a puddling-machine down by the creek, which
concern he glorified with the title of “The Bunyip
Puddling Company, Limited.” The remainder of
the working staff of the Bunyip Puddling Com-
pany consisted of a tarnished small boy, who rode
round on the harrows, and a long-drawn, melan-
choly horse, the color of all concerned in the
undertaking—a dead yellow. But Mr. William
Harper, radiant after a clean shave and magnifi-
cent in a high hat, a paget coat, polished boots,
check pants, and a scarf-pin as big as a whip-
handle, was the manager and bright particular
star of the Thespian Amusers, and nothing smaller.
His compatriots observed the distinction, and Mr.
Harper was happy.
The Bunyip Hill dramatic club had made its
reputation in nigger-minstrel performances, the
distressing solemnity of which, combined with the
aggravation of an exceptionally hot summer, was
known to have driven several reputable residents
to drink and one to suicide—Taffy Davis having
gone direct from the hall on one memorable occa-
sion and drowned himself in the Hand in Hand
Co.’s dam. The colored minstrel is not an
exhilarating exhibition as a rule. Fixed tradi-
tions, decayed jokes, and a stereotyped double-
shuffle, combine with his funereal hue to make
him pall readily on the educated taste. The
Thespian Amusers were sad beyond the average.
They were about the worst collection of hands and
feet and diseased intellect that was ever permitted
to disturb the peace of a district by concerted
screaming and individual reaching after notes
elevated beyond human hopes, or to violate the
human understanding with antique gags, solos on
the bones, and various instrumental annoyances.
Despite all this, the men of Bunyip Hill coun-
tenanced the Thirsty Brigade, and refrained from
throwing offensive articles at the enthusiastic
band of amateurs. The forbearance of the miners
did not arise out of any appreciation of the per-
formers as artists, or any consideration for their
relations. But so many were beholden to the
amusers for “benefits,” and so few knew how
soon a fall of dirt or a slip in a “ jump up,” in
their own cases, might make one of those “ bene-
fits” very acceptable to their surviving friends by
relieving the widow of the expenses of burial, that
Harpei’s song and dance were accepted with
chastened resignation, and the united efforts of
the galaxy with Christian fortitude, relieved only
by an occasional oath and a timely resort to the
consolations of rum.
The Bunyip Hill dramatic club would play a
benefit on the slightest provocation—a local
broken leg or a famine in China ; a death in the
township, or an eviction in Ireland ; an accident
in a mine, or a slice of bad luck in salting a porker.
No excuse was too thin for those rude, uncultivated
mummers to break out in a variety entertainment
of awful proportions and unmitigated wicked-
ness, and every man held it a duty to attend and
immolate himself on the altar of friendship.
Mrs. Kelly, wife of the pitman at the Hand in
Hand, had a baby, and straightway a benefit was
tendered to the family. Twenty-four hours later
Mr. Ben. Mann, boss of a shift, was part-pro-
prietor of twins. The Thespian Amusers were
equal to the occasion—they tendered him two
benefits. Three days later it became known that
an unprecedented calamity had happened to the
township. Mrs. Stacey, whose husband’s claim
had panned out a mean average of four penny-
weights to the load for two months past, had
given birth to triplets. A benefit per head was
awarded to the stricken parents, at the conclusion
of which season Sam Goon had a cow smothered
in the slurry, and was rewarded with a per-
formance. Seven doses of the Thespian Amusers
within a fortnight was enough to unhinge in-
tellects and subvert all the finer attributes of the
animal man. An endeavor was made to obviate
the last night’s evil by obliterating all traces of
the deceased cow, and taking up a private collec-
tion to recompense Mr. Goon, but Harper
heard of the catastrophe, and Sam got the benefit
all the same.
The loss of a horse or the death of a resident
were equally sad calamities on Bunyip Hill. Con-
siderable anxiety began to manifest itself that
people and domestic animals should not die about
the township, but, had that consummation been
attainable, it would never have tempered the zeal
of Harper’s gifted society. In a world full of woe
they would iiave found reason for more. They
once gave a performance for the alleviation of the
condition of the sufferers by the plagues of Egypt,
and only permitted the profits to be diverted to
the improvement of the local cemetery on being
convinced of the antiquity of the Egyptian
incident.
Nobody ever understood what species of mental
aberration induced Manager Harper to put on
Hamlet.” When the announcement went out it
created a profound sensation, and several gentle-
mi’n immediately left the township to spend a
we 1< or two with sick aunts in the metropolis.
Die cast for the legitimate drama contained all
the names of the gentlemen with a parochial repu-
tiou for presenting a pronounced appearance of
“* n( K feet, and diffidence in blackened characters.
Miss Rosabella Jones, the favorite singer of senti-
mental ditties, was billed as Ophelia, and Mrs.
Rowland Lewis consented to appear for the first
time on any stage as Gertrude, Queen of Den-
mark.
A large audience awaited the players on the
night appointed for the initial production of
Shakespeare’s great play at Lang’s Temperance
Hall. All the youth and beauty and fashion of
Bunyip Hill paid at the gate, and the toilers and
moilers crowded the remaining space in painful
anticipation of something great in the way of
chaste entertainment. There is a stage in tragical
incapacity that is infinitely more amusing than
deliberate extravaganza. The gentlemen of
Bunyip Hill did not expect too much of the local
talent—they were content to be edified by its
defects.
Proceedings begfan at the specified time; the
orchestra took his place at the borrowed har-
monium, and, having played a preliminary canter
in the shape of the “Old Hundredth” on that
cheerful instrument, subsided as the curtain
rolled up, disclosing the platform before the castle,
and Francisco and Bernardo armed with revolvers
and attired in costumes which recalled about
equally the dress of a bullock-puncher and that of
a Spanish brigand. Horatio came on eventually,
wearing a paper collar and trailing an old army
sabre; he had on, in addition, knickerbockers and
stockings, a tucked-in jumper, and a Scotch cap
ornamented with a turkey’s feather. The student
Horatio was impersonated by Matty Clark, dis-
agreeably known to the public as the singer of a
lamentable dirge about “Molly, ochone, th’
loight uv me loife.” Matty was a faceman and
had brought from Cork a brogue unexcelled for
richness. This brogue fitted Matty, but it seemed
out of place in Horatio. However, the Bunyip
Hill audience was nob hypercritical, and Matty
was an immense success. He convulsed the house
every time he stepped up to pass a remark, and
his encounter with the ghost of Hamlet’s father
was the occasion of the wildest demonstration of
hysterical enthusiasm ever provoked on Bunjdp
Hill. For this, however, the spirit was partly
accountable. Stacey, the butcher, theatrically
known as Boss Eye, was put up to play the regal
spectre; and when the “majesty of buried Denmark”
rolled on, robed in a tablecloth that had done its
week and was garnished with greasy stains and
frescoed with boiled egg and brands of jam, the
audience just gripped itself and sat still, hard
held, determined to do justice to the gravity of
the situation or rupture a blood-vessel in the
struggle. Boss Eye was the weightest man on the
camp, and the thick end of his adiposity had
settled in his vest; but he made a conscientious
endeavor to look sepulchral, and sympathy was
with him. Hamlet’s father lurched abruptly into
the centre of the stage, brought himself up short
with a tremendous effort, then stood swaying
gently in a limp attitude, and regarding the audi-
ence with a large, inflamed and enquiring eye.
After a vain effort to strengthen his position and
recall the occasion of his arrival, he turned a bland
smile on the crowd, and awaited events,
Fwat are yez thet usurp’st th’ toirae av night,
T’gither wid that fair an’ warr-loike forum?
said Horatio.
This seemed to remind the spectre that some-
thing was expected of him, and he backed up
against the castle, struck a half-tragic, half-
collapsed attitude, waved his gilded broomstick
and cried—
“ Hamlet! sha, ole feller ! (hie !) Hamlet, I
am ” (hie!) Then he paused, assumed a con-
templative face and scratched his ear—he had
forgotten what he has.
“ Hamlet, I am shi” (hie whup!)
Again he paused to ponder. Frantic whispers
were heard from the wings, calling him off; dire
threats, coupled with local profanity to lend them
strength, were wafted over the slush-lamp foot-
lights ; but the ghost didn’t care a hang, he broke
out again ;
“ Hamlesh, I am !”
“ Come off, you infernal ass !” gasped Harper,
from “ behind.” “ It ain’t time for that!”
“ Oh, hie ! ” said the spectre, easting a con-
temptous look into the wings,
“ Hamlesh, I am—l (hie!) am —washa blazes am
I, Hamlesh ?”
“Ye’re a bloinded idjot!” hissed Horatio,
closing with the ghost, with the intention of lead-
ing him off. But a 16-stone ghost is not easily
portable. The resurrected Dane rolled heavily,
grasped at the atmosphere in an agony of appre-
hension, and came down with a crash like a col-
lapsed house, fetching away the castle of Elsinore
and numerous other fixtures in his overthrow, while
the curtain fell on the first scene amidst sqrearasof
delirious merriment.
In the next scene Hamlet appeared. Harper
impersonated the melancholy Dane, in a pair of
runner’s tights, with screens of purple and yellow.
He also wore a pair of patent-leather dancing-
pumps, a blue cape, and a Tam o’ Shanter hat,
with a scarlet plume ; and the long, tangled locks
of a rich, brick-dust wig fell in regal profusion
about his shoulders. Old Jones, the manager of
the North Star, appeared as Polonius, in a Druid’s
regalia; Jack Brown, the braceman, officiated as the
King ; and Laertes, Osric, Rosencrantz, Guilden-
stern, and the rest, were ambitious truckers from
the Hand-in-Hand, all caparisoned according to
the dictates of their own consciences and the
limited resources of the Thespian Amusers’ ward-
robe.
No show half so humorous ever lit up the dreary
realms of farce or pantomime. The audience was
convulsed from post to finish; shrieks of dis-
tracted laughter filled the house and flowed over
the town. People who had failed to secure seats,
attracted by the unwonted sounds of revelry,
besieged the windows, and “ Hamlet, Prince of
Denmark,” was voted a great success. It did not
take long to detect the fact that, barring the
ladies, the full strength of the company was, like
the regal apparition, considerably influenced by
divine afflatus imported in bottles. Hamlet’s
lugubrious gloom intensified with every scene, but
his princely bearing was modified in a like pro-
portion ; and, as the night wore on, his centre of
gravity became extremely erratic. The Ghost
would persist in discarding Shakespeare and tell-
ing his story in his own way. He had to be
shoved on, with his shroud turned twice round
his neck and hanging down his back, and he was
propped up from behind with a stick. The burden
of his lay was : “ I am she (hie!) father’s shpirit!
Come outen have a drink.”. Boss Eye made the
most convivial kind of a spirit, and the fact that
he had not slavishly followed stage traditions
endeared him to the audience. He was called
before the curtain at the close of the act with
encouraging enthusiasm. The cheerful apparition
scrambled out in response to the call, and balanced
himself before the audience, nursing a black bottle
with kingly dignity. When the applause had
subsided, he waved his hand in grateful recogni-
tion, and fell into the orchestra. Throughout the
remainder of the performance the ghost of Ham-
let’s father persisted in wandering on during the
progress of effective scenes, and proffering spiritual
solace from his black bottle to the melancholy
Dane. He spoiled several intense situations by
falling on them, and maiming the performers with
his solid adiposity. Hamlet had no sooner worked
himself up to a pitch of righteous indignation
about things in general than the weighty shade of
his deceased parent would drift on, collide against
him like a staggered camel, explode with a double-
barrelled, back-action hiccup, and then endeavor
to mend matters by inviting the Prince to take a
drink. Then Hamlet had to delay the perform-
ance whilst he hauled the Ghost away, and the
audience could hear him beseeching somebody,
with earnest eloquence and heartfelt profanity, to
sit on the spectre whilst he got on with the play.
After that the goblin would be anchored under
something for a time, but only to float on again
and disturb the gravity of a touching tableau.
The fact was the Thespian Amusers were the
victims of a callous and vulgar joke. Gravity
Brown, the billiard-marker at the Royal Mail
Hotel, and several other conspirators, had intro-
duced themselves behind the scenes, under the
plea of giving their assistance. They had primed
the Ghost before the commencement of the play,
and they kept liquor invitingly handy, and
seduced the cast of “Hamlet” to give way to
alcoholic beverages on every occasion. The Bun-
yip Hill dramatic club had not been nick-
named the “Thirsty Brigade” for nothing;
they required very little pressing, and the
conspirators were easily triumphant. It was
manifestly this plot that led to Harper’s
unprecedented reading of the scene with Ophelia
in the third act. After advising the heroine to
“ get to a nunnery,” Hamlet introduced an
original sensation by rapturously embracing the
lady and kissing her lips in an energetic and appre-
ciative manner. This prompted the affianced of
Miss Rosabella Jones, who was in the audience,
to rise up in open revolt. He elevated himself in
his place with every demonstration of excitement,
and brandishing a fist like a knot on a log, yelled,
“ Look here, that’s enough, Harper ! I’ll swell
your head !”
Hamlet did not heed the admonition ; he
sampled the damsel again.
“ Come homo, Bella Jones !” screamed the lover;
“ I’ll smash you for this, Harper !”
“If thou wilt needs marry,” roared Hamlet,
“ marry a fool.”
This was too much for the lover; he thought the
Dane was getting personal, and scrambled for that
melancholy individual, and presently Hamlet and
the young man were fighting to a finish in the
regal halls of Denmark; They raged round for
seven minutes, and then retired fighting.
Ophelia had to be dispensed with for the re-
mainder of the play, and when Hamlet came on
again he wore a black eye that ill befitted his
exalted position.
Shortly after this the machinations of the con-
spirators “ behind ” attained unrestricted play,
and the players strayed across the scenery bearing
debasing labels which read ; “This is Hamlet” —
or Horatio, or Laertes, or Polonius, as the case
might be, Claudius, King of Denmark, appeared
with a blackened face, wearing a ’possum-rug and
a brass crescent setting forth that the bearer was
King Billy, of the Burrumbeet tribe. Horatio
would persist in singing his favorite dirge,
“Molly, ochone, th’ loight uv me loife,” during
the progress of the “ chamber” scene, and Laertes
returned prematurely from France to dance a jig
on an inverted churn.
The interval between acts three and four was so
protracted that the audience began to despair of
further education in the subtleties of the immortal
bard. At length, however, when curiosity had
been strained to a dangerous pitch, the curtain
beeran to ascend. Very slowly it rolled, and the
excited spectators arose with it. Presently the
joke struck home, and the audience gave way to
its feelings, and did justice to the occasion with a
roar that brought down rain. The rising curtain
had disclosed the grave-yard scene, and the full
strength of the company (always excepting the
ladies) was discovered spread over the grave-yard
in artless abandon, all drunk, and all sunk in
slumber. Gravity Brown walked on to the stage,
and regretted to inform the audience that a slight
indisposition which had attacked the company
compelled the management to postpone the re-
mainder of the play till the Thespian Amusers
could be persuaded to take the pledge.
“Hamlet” was never concluded on Bunyip
Hill. Harper could not be persuaded again to
lead his company along the higher walks of the
profession, and nigger-minstrel business preyed on
the intelligence of the neighborhood till the bitter
end.

Wasted Money.
Ifc is simply wonderful in an enlightened age like
the present, how people will waste their money on
useless medicines. There are thousands of suf-
ferers in this country from liver torpidity, indiges-
tion, kidney complaint, and biliousness, who pay
half-crowns for so-called “cures,” when they
could get far better and safer results from half an
ounce of Epsom salts, costing one half-penny. Of
course, we know this chemical will not give per-
manent results—neither will many of the adver-
tised nostrums —but it will give temporary relief
and not injure the system, whereas the disguised
drastic purging mixtures sold by many quacks
will cause many serious diseases. Clements Tonic
does not cure or relieve disease by purging. It is
a natural tonic restorative, and acts by stimulat-
ing the digestive and secretive apparata to perform
their proper functions. It rebuilds wasted nerve,
bone and muscular tissue, restores strength to the
debilitated system, excites and permanently
strengthens the power of concupescence, and en-
sures a long, happy and enjoyable life, as proved
by the experience of Mr. Ernest Edward Feah,
Berry, N.S.W., who writes on April Bth, 1893
I am only too happy to give you a testimony re
Clements Tonic. I had a severe attack of influenza
nearly two years ago. I had the attendance of a
medical man from whom I derived no benefit. The
symptoms from which I suffered were severe
headaches, sore throat; I was unable to retain
anything I had taken on my stomach. My atten-
tion was drawn to one of your books, and after I
had taken three bottles of Clements Tonic 1
found great relief from that time until now.
When I feel unwell, I always take Clements Tonic
and no other. I wish anyone suffering from the
above symptoms would do the same ; I believe it is
the best medicine in the world. As far as I am
concerned, it cured me when all others failed.—
Yours truly, Ernest Edward Feah, Berry, N.S.W.
Camels at Coolgurdie.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
Fez Mahomet is the camel-man of Coolgardie;
he is also the camel-man of the Murchison ; he is
said to still be the camel-man of Bourke and of
South Australia. There seems to be no limit to
his camel carrying-operations. He is said to have
taken 20,000 sovereigns to Westralia; he has cer-
tainly taken thither upwards of 2000 camels.
More than half of these are employed on the
Coolgardie goldfield. His camel-staff is believed
to consist chiefly of brothers-in-law ; many wives,
many brothers-in-law. It is not probable that
Fez has as many wives as camels ; if he is a much
over-married man his looks belie him. One of his
brothers-in-law claims to be champion wrestler of
Westralia. He is a stalwart Afghan who dresses
in spotless white, even at Coolgardie. No one has
yet thrown him, though many have tried; it is
believed he could throw a camel. On my way
back I met a champion wrestler from Victoria
who was going to Coolgardie to tackle Fez’s cele-
brated wrestling brother-in-law; but as no news
of the Victorian champion’s death has arrived in
the East the match cannot have come off. The
Afghan is bigger than Donald Dinnie, and has a
back like John L. Sullivan’s.
• # •
Afghans at Coolgardie are an exclusive section of
the community. They mix not with whites, and
are encamped outside the town. They never
trouble man or beast, but leave their' camels for
that business. Law prevents their dry-blowing
or working quartz-reefs, but even if the statute
were repealed to-morrow there would be no mad
Afghan rush. Fez and his minions allow the
homogeneous white man to find the gold, and then
they gather it in by other means.
• • •
The Afghans’ detestation of pork exceeds even
that of the children of Israel, They will not carry
pig-meat for any consideration. They will eat
no flesh which they have not killed themselves;
therefore they eschew tinned meats and live
principally on rice. The Afghan only asks for
meat when he has a white-man boss. To carry
live sheep is an expensive encumbrance ; therefore, t
Fez Mahomet has a monopoly of Afghan cheap
labour.
• # #
Heretofore, throughout Australia, Afghans
have been regarded as inseparable from the
camel as is the hump itself. This is a mistake,
and the hump is a nuisance. To protect the
hump the Oriental people have for centuries used
a saddle weighing from 70 to lOOlbs. The hump
is the softest part of a camel’s anatomy, but you
can’t load him anywhere else. That’s where all
the fat is. Camels, when without food or water,
live on their hump, and when you see a camel
with his hump considerably reduced you may
infer that he has had hard experience. It is time
the camel enigma were solved. Why not load his
neck, where lots of room and strength seem to
waste? His six to eight feet of able-bodied neck
might be used for carrying rolls of canvas, or
other softgoods,. which might be so placed as to be
run off by means of a windlass. This ship of the
desert might surely be better trimmed and bal-
lasted. However, this is a purely amateur idea.
• • * •
The Australian destiny of the camel is in the
shafts. Explorer Lindsay has several camel-teams
drawing goods, and they work “ like bullocks.”
They require a small collar with rope traces and
very little other harness. It takes time and
patience to break them in. They or their forbears
have not been brought up to the business, but
they will, I am pretty sure, be taught it in Wes-
tralia. I saw two in a buggy, where they looked
more grotesque than picturesque. Returning
from the 90-mile one bright moonlight night I met
two of Lindsay’s loaded camel-teams, eight and
six. I had never previously seen a camel-team,
and as they came on me suddenly in a bend of the
road I got my life’s biggest scare. But my horses
turned not a hair; they had seen them before.
Within the next 12 months there will be hundreds
of camel-teams in Westralia. To use back-laden
camels means unloading every night and re-loading
every morning, and neither is done in an instant.
The camel only carries an average of sowt., while
he can pull about half-a-ton. I saw six drawing
four tons over a sand-plain xvith the greatest ease.
I saw hundreds with sore backs, and sore-backed
camels have to be turned out for a spell. I never
saw one in a team with sore shoulders. The Wes-
tralian roads are bad in places, but they are in-
variably level, and, for unformed tracks, are excep-
tionally good.
« * •
The most highly-respected camel of Coolgardie is
Misery. Misery’s early career is lost in the mists
of antiquity. Some 40 years back he was im-
ported into South Australia, and having served his
purpose in laying the foundations qf Australian
camel-industry—his offspring outnumber Maho-
met’s brother S-in-law—he went into the carrying
business. Two years ago he was shipped Out to
Coolgardie, where he became the favourite riding-
camel of Warden Finnerty. He is at least as old
as Parkes, and while he lacks the cunning and
shrewdness of that statesman, he carries much
more weight in the community in which he lives.
If anything, Parkes has a keener eye to the main
chance, but is not so phlegmatic. Parkes is moved
by the force of public opinion ; Misery is absolute
adamant.
• * •
All camels are imperturbable. To attempt to
frighten a camel is to labor in vain. He has no
nervous system. A 100-ton gun-report would not
turn a camel’s hair. They are, it would seem,
absolutely destitute of sympathy or affection.
You could, possibly, by trying hard, make a pet
of a camel, but he never would reciprocate. He
is a smileless beast. A cow-camel will, however,
fondle its calf. I have seen cows lie down and

to them, with particulars of what they want.
