The Afghan, however, does not allow the cow to
fool away time with her youngster in business
hours. Within a few hours of the birth of the
calf I have seen Afghans load the mother with
several cwt., place the baby on top, and move off as
if nothing of moment had happened. A train of 50
camels cannot be delayed by any business of
that kind. After the third day the mother is
relieved of the calf, which runs alongside the train
and gambols with other small-fry, if there are any.
My ambition is to bring a sixty-days-old camel-
calf to Sydney, and offer a prize to anyone who
will guess what it is.
• » «
All camels are “ roarers.” Their groan is worse
than their bite. They groan most when loading,
and the groaning of 100 camels would almost be
tuo much for the inhabitants of a cemetery,
Camel-trains are made up of various lengths,
Some are nearly a-quarter-of-a-mile long, com-
prising 70 to 80 camels, with a clothes-line running
through their noses, in which studs like an over-
coat-button are fastened. A quick-walking camel
is placed at the head of the string to make the
pace. Sometimes the drivers ride; generally they
walk. The regulation pace is three miles an hour,
and it seldom varies. Time is kept as regularly as
with locomotives. When loading or discharging
cargo the camels drop down together, at the
ejaculation “ Hushla 1” and, when required, rise,
not together, but one after the other in regulation
turn. They drop into line with the precision of
soldiers. When a week without water, a camel is
limp and baggy. After drinking, he is like a
balloon.
* * *
The Australian-bred camel is said to be far
far better in this country than others-Indian or
Arabian-as a weight-carrier or for endurance. He
is bred chiefly at Hergot Springs, S. A., and most
of him is produced on the estates of squatter
Klder, who has sent 1200 to Westralia during the
’ast two years. Camel-farming has now been
tarted at Coolgardie. If a camel is brought
up where there is an abundance of water it will
drink every day, and in after-years would probably
die of thirst in a week. Hence the custom is to
breed them in paddocks without water, and from
their youth up they are allowed to drink but once
a week. This expands the stomach and enables
a big lasting supply to be taken in—a wrinkle in
camel-breeding worth knowing by amateurs. I
have seen a camel drink at the Government well
after being 17 days without water ; he quenched
his magnificent thirst with 30 gallons, containing
altogether a stone or so of salt and other ingre-
dients, such as gypsum, magnesia, and soon, which
no other stomach would stand. A friend of mine
lost his camel in a waterless bush for 23 days, and
the time that beast occupied in drinking at the
well satisfied me that no one had “ shouted ” for
him during that period. The Coolgardie salt
water is a little too much tor even camels. They
don’t fatten on it. They take it in the absence of
fresh, and because it is Hobson’s choice. But they
don’t like it; rather it doesn’t like them. There
is a palpable and painful distinction between
Afghans’ camels, which get nothing else but bore-
water, and those whose owners give them fresh
water. There is a limit to the drinking-capacity
of a camel. Like the Afghan, he won’t drink
whisky, and he would want to be very thirsty to
drink a barrel of “colonial” beer. He enjoys
a “ shandygaff,” which, being interpreted at Cool-
gardie, is a mixture of saltwater and condensed
water* But I would like to know what a camel
wouldn’t eat. He would eat a bush-house in a
meal. He is omnivorous. He eats “blind.”
As a camel-train moves along the track the
beasts stretch out their necks and take in
everything without discrimination— pine-scrub, if
it happens to be there—if not, anything that is;
saltbush, salmon, blue, white, gimlet, or any
other gum-leaves, currajong, sandal-wood—any-
thing, everything—nothing escapes his grinders,
If you like to give him chaff and oats that
is your business ; he will eat them, but he will
fatten just as well on brushwood, which does not
look to contain an ounce of nutriment to the ton.
Nothing grows that he will not eat—even poison-
plants don’t trouble him.
• • *
Camels are the personification of docility. Men
who have never seen them before have loaded
them at Esperanoe Bay, Albany, and Perth, and
marched away with them without the slightest
trouble. But it must not be considered that the
camel is incapable of mischief; he, as a rule,
simply doesn’t consider it worth while to exercise
his illimitable powers in that direction. He could
kick like a mule, with a five-horse-power kick, if
he liked, and he is much longer in the reach than
either horse or ass. He has a mouth large enough
to take in a man’s head, and his neck is long
enough to reach for you even if riding over his
stumpy tail. That he is capable of infinite buck-
jumping I had an opportunity of witnessing,
David Lindsay bought, or borrowed, a riding-
camel from Fez, and mounted him in Bayley-st.,
Coolgardie. To the amazement of the rider, and
the amusement of the crowd, a camel buck-
jumping show ensued. Never shall I forget it—
nor will Lindsay. For the first lOsec. the camel
skirmished sideways, then he ran his neck along
the ground and walked on his fore-legs, carrying
his hind-legs above him, and sculled the air with
them making what a Sydney News reporter
would call “ a long, clean, sweeping stroke. No
discredit to Lindsay that he was spilt. It was a
wonder to me that the camel did not buck his
hump off as well. 1 he danger of a camel-fall is
the heighfrof the brute. You are nine feet above
the ground to start with, and if you come off in
pne of his sudden back-arch movements you are
hftsd up another nine, and have a total drop of 18
eeb > which is too much for a heavy-weight. A
camel-race occurred last Queen s Birthday at Oool-
gafdl^’ l a . ® oor V, aft , er tb P * a . looked
» dead bird for Fez’s unbacked brute. The Eng-
s camels carried all the money, and true
*> *e instincts of British Fair Play, a number of
headed the leading camel and shouted
Hushla. Down he dropped in an instant,
gba ° s ros - e fco V l ®, occasion Wlt d saplings,
Off he went again and down once more at a re-
peaked Hushla, and down also came the saplings
°“ back. At last he won by a long neck, and
the Britishers went down for their money Good-
conditioned camels are worth at Coolgardie,
or -880 each a broken-kneed, sore-ba,cked beast
can be bought for £3O, while a first-class riding-
camel fetches <£l2o, and is well-worth it, for he
will carry you a hundred miles a day for a week
without a drink.
# * *
A camel, in his road-gait, rises in four distinct
motions, two aft and two for’ard ; and sometimes
he starts at one end and occasionally at the other,
so if you don’t watch him closely in the saddle
when rising you run the risk of breaking your
neck by a backward movement or dislocating your
back with a forward motion. The first ride on a
camel will fix up any bad liver, but completely dis-
organ iso all other internal arrangements. Easy
stages are recommended at first. A good
riding-camel will travel eight miles an hour
all day—a long swinging, ambling gait, with-
out variation: he will either walk at three
miles an hour or amble at eight, he has no
middle pace. Never tether a camel at night,
as most amateurs do. Close-hobble him and
let him loose —that is the right thing to do.
Sometimes they will travel a mile or two during
the night, especially if single, but when in com-
pany they are usually found together within a
short distance of camp. All wear bells, but some
are so cunning that they will carry their bells
when mustering-time arrives without ringing
them. Camels love each other’s society, and I often
found them grazing on salt-bush with horses for
the sake of company. Cow-camels never come
into season—that depends upon the nature of the
bull alone —a fact, as well as many others stated
above, probably unknown to nineteen-twentieths
of Australians.
# » *
If I wanted to lay Sydney in ruins I would use
camels in preference to dynamite. I would land
thirty six camels at Circular Quay, and start them
start them out in single file, carrying pianos and
boiler-plates; turn into George-street at twelve to
one o’clock on a fine Saturday and march to the
railway-station. I would get there; but long
before I reached Redfern the big city shops would
be full of ’buses and cabs and horses with broken
legs. Also, the coroner would have about 200
inquests fixed for next day.
OLD BENDIGO.
A very large number of Darlinghursfc
prisoners learn shorthand. The march of crime is
towards the press.
Melbourne Argus, which is running Free-
trade for all it is worth, lately demonstrated that,
on the machinery required for the equipment of a
single butter-factory, no less than .£447 had to be
paid in Customs duties. It not only asserted this,
but proved it by bard, unimpeachable figures.
It now transpires that the whole machinery for a
butter-factory only costs about £360 delivered,
duty and all, so the unimpeachable figures must
be lying again. The Freetrader can always prove,
by the most irrefutable logic, that Protection
makes things dearer —that it must make things
dearer, and can’t possibly do otherwise. But
somehow —no one seems to know how —things
don’t get dearer, all the same.
Melbourne bosh :
The sentiment of Freetrade seemed to have its personi-
fication in the noblest type of man—the unselfish man.
If so, importers, shipowners, squatters, stock-
brokers, agents and middlemen, and barnacles
generally—who form the bulk of Freetraders,
along with the slave who ‘‘thanks God and his
employer ” for being allowed to starve himself in
return for hard work—are “ unselfish men.” The
truly unselfish man does not exist. As “ self-
preservation is the first law of nature,” so self-
interest is the second. Love and ambition are
ruled by it. Nations rise and decline according
to its success or failure. The whole realm of
nature, zoological and inanimate, writhes with
selfishness. The almost-unselfish man ends always
as a wreck, a scouted failure, trodden down by his
enemies and contemned by his friends. But the
Freetrader? He is a freebooter —a wolf for
selfishness.
When You Tighten Up Your Belt.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
When you take to smoking gum-leaves as a substi-
tute for weed,
When you’d swap your hopes of glory for a good
old-fashioned feed,
When you feel like John the Baptist on his locusts
must have felt,
Then your sentiments get sultry as you tighten up
your belt.
When your boots are worn with walking till your
toes are on the ground,
When you ask for “ five-and-twenty ” and the
squatter says “a pound,”
You consign him to a region where he’d very
quickly melt—
You re-hoist your tattered bluey and you tighten
up your belt.
When the ration-bags are empty and the fish
refuse to bite,
And you seek your dismal shelter to endure a
hungry night;
When the wind begins to bluster and the rain
begins to pelt,
Then you curse the land that bred you as you
tighten up your belt.
Next you scramble up a sapling with a deluge all
around,
And you wonder when its over if your carcase
will be found.
Oh, the words to paint your feelings are with
difficulty spelt—
The recording angel’s busy as you tighten up your
belt.
And so the play goes onward, and you act your
lonely part
With a gnawing in your stomach and with murder
in your heart —
You’re a danger to the country be you Saxon,
Gaul or Celt,
And I think you’re well worth watching as you
tighten up your belt.
Queensland.
CURLEW.
When our old-time friend, Mrs. Gordon
Baillie, made her last appearance in the dock, she
addressed herself strongly to the poetic aspect of
the case. She was still very lovely, and she sat
tearfully in a beautiful attitude of resignation,
wiping her large, pathetic eyes with a lace hand-
kerchief, and looking appealingly at the jury.
She had a charming and soulful young lawyer
with the finest hair-parting in the profession, and
he talked in broken accents about the world’s
inhumanity to woman when she has made one
false step. If a jury of poets, artists, and hair-
dressers’ assistants could have been arranged for,
the lady would have got off, but the twelve men in
the box were mostly shopkeepers who had all been
taken in, at some time or other, by the same soul-
ful kind of defendant, and the result was seven
years.

—BY—
ERNEST FAVENC,
With prefatory note by
Half Boldrewood,
PRICE : : Is.
Posted to any Australian
Address, Is. .id.
“ The volume takes its name from one of the sketches, ‘ The Last of Six,’ though in point of merit they all stand pretty well on a. «• ar. The
author, who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the Whites and the natives in these fa away
parts. A strong vein of mysticism runs through his narratives, and there are ghosts in almost every chapter, and for those who arc fond of
realistic work the volume will prove attractive reading.”— S.M. Herald, July 8, 1893.
“ No writer has given the world a more vivid description of Australia and its wilderness than Ernest Favenc, and the secret of tips is that
he has, by many years’ experience of travel and bush life, acquired a knowledge of this continent which is possessed by scarcely any other writer.
He, too, possesses great descriptive powers. Whatever he puts pen to is certain to be good. In the stories under notice he has given us some of
his best work. That they will be widely read is certain.”— Sydney Mail, July 8, 1893.
“ They present with force and vividness, as well as with the accuracy arising from long contact and keen observation, striking pictures of
parts of Australia unfamiliar to most of its inhabitants ; they tell, sometimes with remarkable power, of incidents such as happen, in wild places
when human hearts are stirred by strong passions, and of weird imaginings that move the minds of lonely men. ‘ The Last of Six ’is a terrible
tale of cannibalism, treachery, and revenge upon the dead, which in other hands might be gross and hideous, but which Mr. I' avenc makes of
thrilling interest. ‘ The Haunt of the Jinkarras’ suggests Rider Haggard and his imitators to some extent, but is itself relieved from the
suspicion of imitation by faithful local colour and a grotesquerie of conception, in which Mr. Favenc here and there approaches Poe. This
characteristic is especially noticeable in ‘ Spirit Led,’ which the great American might have written. ‘ A Cup of Cold Water is, perhaps, the
best talc in a collection that should be widely read, and which forms a worthy addition to purely Australian literature.”— Daily Telegraph.
July 15, 1893.
“ ‘ The Last of Sixand other stories. By Ernest Favenc. (Sydney: The Bulletin Newspaper Co ) This is a collection of short but
graphic sketches by Mr. Favenc, the well-known explorer, who has a rare collection of Australian legendary lore. All the stories here are good,
and we can heartily endorse the recommendatory notice by Rolf Boldrewood prefixed to this collection. The present issue forms the third of The.
Bulletin series of Australian reminiscences.”— Australian Star, July 15, 1893.
